In a narrow lane which communicate with the shady street I discern the rich old merchant put himself to the top of his speed lest the rain should convert his hair-powder to a paste .
Unhappy gentleman !
By the slow vehemence and painful moderation wherewith he journey , it be but too evident that Podagra have leave it thrill tenderness in his great toe .
But yonder , at a far more rapid pace , come three other of my acquaintance , the two pretty girl and the young man unseasonably interrupt in their walk .
Their footstep be support by the risen dust , the wind lend them it velocity , they fly like three sea-birds drive landward by the tempestuous breeze .
The lady would not thus rival Atalanta if they but know that any one be at leisure to observe them .
Ah !
a they hasten onward , laugh in the angry face of nature , a sudden catastrophe have chance .
At the corner where the narrow lane enters into the street they come plump against the old merchant , whose tortoise-motion have just bring him to that point .
He like not the sweet encounter ; the darkness of the whole air gather speedily upon his visage , and there be a pause on both side .
Finally he thrust aside the youth with little courtesy , seize an arm of each of the two girl , and plod onward like a magician with a prize of captive fairy .
All this be easy to be understood .
How disconsolate the poor lover stand , regardless of the rain that threaten an exceed damage to his well-fashioned habiliment , till he catch a backward glance of mirth from a bright eye , and turn away with whatever comfort it convey !
The old man and his daughter be safely house , and now the storm let loose it fury .
In every dwelling I perceive the face of the chambermaid a they shut down the window , exclude the impetuous shower and shrink away from the quick fiery glare .
The large drop descend with force upon the slat roof and rise again in smoke .
There be a rush and roar a of a river through the air , and muddy stream bubble majestically along the pavement , whirl their dusky foam into the kennel , and disappear beneath iron grate .
Thus do Arethusa sink .
I love not my station here aloft in the midst of the tumult which I be powerless to direct or quell , with the blue lightning wrinkle on my brow and the thunder mutter it first awful syllable in my ear .
I will descend .
Yet let me give another glance to the sea , where the foam break out in long white line upon a broad expanse of blackness or boil up in far-distant point like snowy mountain-tops in the eddy of a flood ; and let me look once more at the green plain and little hill of the country , over which the giant of the storm be stride in robe of mist , and at the town whose obscured and desolate street might beseem a city of the dead ; and , turn a single moment to the sky , now gloomy a an author 's prospect , I prepare to resume my station on low earth .
But stay !
A little speck of azure have widen in the western heaven ; the sunbeam find a passage and go rejoice through the tempest , and on yonder darkest cloud , bear like hallowed hope of the glory of another world and the trouble and tear of this , brighten forth the rainbow .
THE HOLLOW OF THE THREE HILLS .
In those strange old time when fantastic dream and madman 's revery be realize among the actual circumstance of life , two person meet together at an appoint hour and place .
One be a lady graceful in form and fair of feature , though pale and troubled and smitten with an untimely blight in what should have be the full bloom of her year ; the other be an ancient and meanly-dressed woman of ill-favored aspect , and so withered , shrunken and decrepit that even the space since she begin to decay must have exceed the ordinary term of human existence .
In the spot where they encounter no mortal could observe them .
Three little hill stand near each other , and down in the midst of them sink a hollow basin almost mathematically circular , two or three hundred foot in breadth and of such depth that a stately cedar might but just be visible above the side .
Dwarf pine be numerous upon the hill and partly fringe the outer verge of the intermediate hollow , within which there be nothing but the brown grass of October and here and there a tree-trunk that have fall long ago and lay moulder with no green successor from it root .
One of these mass of decay wood , formerly a majestic oak , rest close beside a pool of green and sluggish water at the bottom of the basin .
Such scene a this -LRB- so gray tradition tell -RRB- be once the resort of a power of evil and his plighted subject , and here at midnight or on the dim verge of even they be say to stand round the mantling pool disturb it putrid water in the performance of an impious baptismal rite .
The chill beauty of an autumnal sunset be now gild the three hill-tops , whence a paler tint steal down their side into the hollow .
`` Here be our pleasant meeting come to pass , '' say the age crone , `` accord a thou hast desire .
Say quickly what thou wouldst have of me , for there be but a short hour that we may tarry here . ''
As the old withered woman speak a smile glimmer on her countenance like lamplight on the wall of a sepulchre .
The lady tremble and cast her eye upward to the verge of the basin , a if meditating to return with her purpose unaccomplished .
But it be not so ordain .
`` I be stranger in this land , a you know , '' say she , at length .
`` Whence I come it matter not , but I have leave those behind me with whom my fate be intimately bind , and from whom I be cut off for ever .
There be a weight in my bosom that I can not away with , and I have come hither to inquire of their welfare . ''
`` And who be there by this green pool that can bring thee news from the end of the earth ? ''
cry the old woman , peer into the lady 's face .
`` Not from my lip mayst thou hear these tidings ; yet be thou bold , and the daylight shall not pass away from yonder hilltop before thy wish be grant . ''
`` I will do your bidding though I die , '' reply the lady , desperately .
The old woman seat herself on the trunk of the fall tree , throw aside the hood that shroud her gray lock and beckon her companion to draw near .
`` Kneel down , '' she say , `` and lay your forehead on my knee . ''
She hesitate a moment , but the anxiety that have long be kindle burn fiercely up within her .
As she kneel down the border of her garment be dip into the pool ; she lay her forehead on the old woman 's knee , and the latter draw a cloak about the lady 's face , so that she be in darkness .
Then she hear the muttered word of prayer , in the midst of which she start and would have arise .
`` Let me flee !
Let me flee and hide myself , that they may not look upon me ! ''
she cry .
But , with return recollection , she hush herself and be still a death , for it seem a if other voice , familiar in infancy and unforgotten through many wandering and in all the vicissitude of her heart and fortune , be mingle with the accent of the prayer .
At first the word be faint and indistinct -- not render so by distance , but rather resemble the dim page of a book which we strive to read by an imperfect and gradually brighten light .
In such a manner , a the prayer proceed , do those voice strengthen upon the ear , till at length the petition end , and the conversation of an age man and of a woman broken and decay like himself become distinctly audible to the lady a she kneel .
But those stranger appear not to stand in the hollow depth between the three hill .
Their voice be encompass and re-echoed by the wall of a chamber the window of which be rattle in the breeze ; the regular vibration of a clock , the crackling of a fire and the tinkling of the ember a they fell among the ash render the scene almost as vivid a if paint to the eye .
By a melancholy hearth sit these two old people , the man calmly despondent , the woman querulous and tearful , and their word be all of sorrow .
They speak of a daughter , a wanderer they know not where , bear dishonor along with her and leave shame and affliction to bring their gray head to the grave .
They allude also to other and more recent woe , but in the midst of their talk their voice seem to melt into the sound of the wind sweeping mournfully among the autumn leave ; and when the lady lift her eye , there be she kneel in the hollow between three hill .
`` A weary and lonesome time yonder old couple have of it , '' remark the old woman , smile in the lady 's face .
`` And do you also hear them ? ''
exclaim she , a sense of intolerable humiliation triumphing over her agony and fear .
`` Yea , and we have yet more to hear , '' reply the old woman , `` wherefore cover thy face quickly . ''
Again the withered hag pour forth the monotonous word of a prayer that be not mean to be acceptable in heaven , and soon in the pause of her breath strange murmuring begin to thicken , gradually increase , so a to drown and overpower the charm by which they grow .
Shrieks pierce through the obscurity of sound and be succeed by the singing of sweet female voice , which in their turn give way to a wild roar of laughter break suddenly by groanings and sob , form altogether a ghastly confusion of terror and mourning and mirth .
Chains be rattle , fierce and stern voice uttered threat and the scourge resound at their command .
All these noise deepen and become substantial to the listener 's ear , till she could distinguish every soft and dreamy accent of the love-song that die causelessly into funeral-hymns .
She shudder at the unprovoked wrath which blaze up like the spontaneous kindling of flume , and she grow faint at the fearful merriment rag miserably around her .
In the midst of this wild scene , where unbound passion jostle each other in a drunken career , there be one solemn voice of a man , and a manly and melodious voice it might once have be .
He go to and fro continually , and his foot sound upon the floor .
In each member of that frenzied company whose own burning thought have become their exclusive world he seek an auditor for the story of his individual wrong , and interpret their laughter and tear a his reward of scorn or pity .
He speak of woman 's perfidy , of a wife who have break her holiest vow , of a home and heart make desolate .
Even a he go on , the shout , the laugh , the shriek , the sob , rise up in unison , till they change into the hollow , fitful and uneven sound of the wind a it fight among the pine tree on those three lonely hill .
The lady look up , and there be the withered woman smile in her face .
`` Couldst thou have think there be such merry time in a mad-house ? ''
inquire the latter .
`` True , true ! ''
say the lady to herself ; `` there be mirth within it wall , but misery , misery without . ''
`` Wouldst thou hear more ? ''
demand the old woman .
`` There be one other voice I would fain listen to again , '' reply the lady , faintly .
`` Then lay down thy head speedily upon my knee , that thou mayst get thee hence before the hour be past . ''
The golden skirt of day be yet linger upon the hill , but deep shade obscure the hollow and the pool , a if sombre night wear rise thence to overspread the world .
Again that evil woman begin to weave her spell .
Long do it proceed unanswered , till the knolling of a bell stole in among the interval of her word like a clang that have travel far over valley and rise ground and be just ready to die in the air .
The lady shook upon her companion 's knee a she hear that bode sound .
Stronger it grow , and sadder , and deepen into the tone of a death-bell , knolling dolefully from some ivy-mantled tower and bearing tidings of mortality and woe to the cottage , to the hall and to the solitary wayfarer , that all might weep for the doom appoint in turn to them .
Then come a measure tread , pass slowly , slowly on , a of mourner with a coffin , their garment trail on the ground , so that the ear could measure the length of their melancholy array .
Before them go the priest , read the burial-service , while the leaf of his book be rustle in the breeze .
And though no voice but his be hear to speak aloud , still there be revilings and anathema , whisper but distinct , from woman and from men , breathe against the daughter who have wrung the aged heart of her parent , the wife who have betray the trust fondness of her husband , the mother who have sin against natural affection and leave her child to die .
The sweeping sound of the funeral train fade away like a thin vapor , and the wind , that just before have seem to shake the coffin-pall , moan sadly round the verge of the hollow between three hill .
But when the old woman stir the kneeling lady , she lift not her head .
`` Here have be a sweet hour 's sport ! ''
say the withered crone , chuckle to herself .
THE TOLL-GATHERER 'S DAY .
A SKETCH OF TRANSITORY LIFE .
Methinks , for a person whose instinct bid him rather to pore over the current of life than to plunge into it tumultuous wave , no undesirable retreat be a toll-house beside some throng thoroughfare of the land .
In youth , perhaps , it be good for the observer to run about the earth , to leave the track of his footstep far and wide , to mingle himself with the action of numberless vicissitude , and , finally , in some calm solitude to fee a musing spirit on all that he have see and felt .
But there be nature too indolent or too sensitive to endure the dust , the sunshine or the rain , the turmoil of moral and physical element , to which all the wayfarer of the world expose themselves .
For such a man how pleasant a miracle could life be make to roll it variegated length by the threshold of his own hermitage , and the great globe , a it be , perform it revolution and shift it thousand scene before his eye without whirl him onward in it course !
If any mortal be favor with a lot analogous to this , it be the toll-gatherer .
So , at least , have I often fancy while lounge on a bench at the door of a small square edifice which stand between shore and shore in the midst of a long bridge .
Beneath the timber ebb and flow an arm of the sea , while above , like the life-blood through a great artery , the travel of the north and east be continually throb .
Sitting on the aforesaid bench , I amuse myself with a conception , illustrate by numerous pencil-sketches in the air , of the toll-gatherer 's day .
In the morning -- dim , gray , dewy summer 's morn -- the distant roll of ponderous wheel begin to mingle with my old friend 's slumber , creak more and more harshly through the midst of his dream and gradually replace it with reality .
Hardly conscious of the change from sleep to wakefulness , he find himself partly clothe and throw wide the toll-gates for the passage of a fragrant load of hay .
The timber groan beneath the slow-revolving wheel ; one sturdy yeoman stalk beside the ox , and , peer from the summit of the hay , by the glimmer of the half-extinguished lantern over the toll-house be see the drowsy visage of his comrade , who have enjoy a nap some ten mile long .
The toll be pay ; creak , creak , again go the wheel , and the huge hay-mow vanishes into the morning mist .
As yet nature be but half awake , and familiar object appear visionary .
But yonder , dash from the shore with a rattle thunder of the wheel and a confused clatter of hoof , come the never-tiring mail , which have hurry onward at the same headlong , restless rate all through the quiet night .
The bridge resound in one continued peal a the coach roll on without a pause , merely afford the toll-gatherer a glimpse at the sleepy passenger , who now bestir their torpid limb and snuff a cordial in the briny air .
The morn breathes upon them and blush , and they forget how wearily the darkness toil away .
And behold now the fervid day in his bright chariot , glitter aslant over the wave , nor scorn to throw a tribute of his golden beam on the toll-gatherer 's little hermitage .
The old man look eastward , and -LRB- for he be a moralizer -RRB- frame a simile of the stage-coach and the sun .
While the world be rouse itself we may glance slightly at the scene of our sketch .
It sit above the bosom of the broad flood -- a spot not of earth , but in the midst of water which rush with a murmur sound among the massive beam beneath .
Over the door be a weatherbeaten board inscribed with the rate of toll in letter so nearly efface that the gilding of the sunshine can hardly make them legible .
Beneath the window be a wooden bench on which a long succession of weary wayfarer have repose themselves .
Peeping within-doors , we perceive the whitewashed wall bedeck with sundry lithographic print and advertisement of various import and the immense show-bill of a wandering caravan .
And there sit our good old toll-gatherer , glorify by the early sunbeam .
He be a man , a his aspect may announce , of quiet soul and thoughtful , shrewd , yet simple mind , who of the wisdom which the passing world scatter along the wayside have gather a reasonable store .
Now the sun smile upon the landscape and earth smile back again upon the sky .
Frequent now be the traveller .
The toll-gatherer 's practised ear can distinguish the weight of every vehicle , the number of it wheel and how many horse beat the resounding timber with their iron tramp .
Here , in a substantial family chaise , set forth betimes to take advantage of the dewy road , come a gentleman and his wife with their rosy-cheeked little girl sit gladsomely between them .
The bottom of the chaise be heap with multifarious bandbox and carpet-bags , and beneath the axle swing a leathern trunk dusty with yesterday 's journey .
Next appear a four-wheeled carryall people with a round half dozen of pretty girl , all draw by a single horse and drive by a single gentleman .
Luckless wight doom through a whole summer day to be the butt of mirth and mischief among the frolicsome maidens !
Bolt upright in a sulky rid a thin , sour-visaged man who a he pay his toll hand the toll-gatherer a printed card to stick upon the wall .
The vinegar-faced traveller prove to be a manufacturer of pickle .
Now pace slowly from timber to timber a horseman clad in black , with a meditative brow , a of one who , whithersoever his steed might bear him , would still journey through a mist of brood thought .
He be a country preacher go to labor at a protracted meeting .
The next object pass townward be a butcher 's cart canopy with it arch of snow-white cotton .
Behind come a `` sauceman '' drive a wagon full of new potato , green ear of corn , beet , carrot , turnip and summer squash , and next two wrinkle , wither witch-looking old gossip in an antediluvian chaise draw by a horse of former generation and go to peddle out a lot of huckleberry .
See , there , a man trundle a wheelbarrow-load of lobster .
And now a milk-cart rattle briskly onward , cover with green canvas and convey the contribution of a whole herd of cow , in large tin canister .
But let all these pay their toll and pas .
Here come a spectacle that cause the old toll-gatherer to smile benignantly , a if the traveller bring sunshine with them and lavish it gladsome influence all along the road .
It be a barouche of the new style , the varnished panel of which reflect the whole move panorama of the landscape , and show a picture , likewise , of our friend with his visage broaden , so that his meditative smile be transform to grotesque merriment .
Within sits a youth fresh a the summer morn , and beside him a young lady in white with white glove upon her slender hand and a white veil flow down over her face .
But methinks her blushing cheek burn through the snowy veil .
Another white-robed virgin sits in front .
And who be these on whom , and on all that appertain to them , the dust of earth seem never to have settle ?
Two lover whom the priest have bless this bless morn and send them forth , with one of the bride-maids , on the matrimonial tour .
-- Take my bless too , ye happy one !
May the sky not frown upon you nor cloud bedew you with their chill and sullen rain !
May the hot sun kindle no fever in your heart !
May your whole life 's pilgrimage be as blissful a this first day 's journey , and it close be gladden with even bright anticipation than those which hallow your bridal-night !
They pass , and ere the reflection of their joy have fade from his face another spectacle throw a melancholy shadow over the spirit of the observe man .
In a close carriage sit a fragile figure muffle carefully and shrink even from the mild breath of summer .
She lean against a manly form , and his arm enfold her a if to guard his treasure from some enemy .
Let but a few week pas , and when he shall strive to embrace that love one , he will press only desolation to his heart .
And now have Morning gather up her dewy pearl and flee away .
The sun roll blaze through the sky , and can not find a cloud to cool his face with .
The horse toil sluggishly along the bridge , and heave their glistening side in short quick panting when the rein be tighten at the toll-house .
Glisten , too , the face of the traveller .
Their garment be thickly bestrewn with dust ; their whisker and hair look hoary ; their throat be choke with the dusty atmosphere which they have leave behind them .
No air be stir on the road .
Nature dare draw no breath l she should inhale a stifling cloud of dust .
`` A hot and dusty day ! ''
cry the poor pilgrim a they wipe their begrime forehead and woo the doubtful breeze which the river bear along with it .
-- `` Awful hot !
Dreadful dusty ! ''
answer the sympathetic toll-gatherer .
They start again to pass through the fiery furnace , while he re-enter his cool hermitage and besprinkle it with a pail of briny water from the stream beneath .
He think within himself that the sun be not so fierce here a elsewhere , and that the gentle air doth not forget him in these sultry day .
Yes , old friend , and a quiet heart will make a dog-day temperate .
He hear a weary footstep , and perceive a traveller with pack and staff , who sit down upon the hospitable bench and remove the hat from his wet brow .
The toll-gatherer administers a cup of cold water , and , discover his guest to be a man of homely sense , he engage him in profitable talk , utter the maxim of a philosophy which he have find in his own soul , but know not how it come there .
And a the wayfarer make ready to resume his journey he tell him a sovereign remedy for blistered foot .
Now come the noontide hour -- of all the hour , near akin to midnight , for each have it own calmness and repose .
Soon , however , the world begin to turn again upon it axis , and it seem the busy epoch of the day , when an accident impede the march of sublunary thing .
The draw be lift to permit the passage of a schooner laden with wood from the Eastern forest , she stick immovably right athwart the bridge .
Meanwhile , on both side of the chasm a throng of impatient traveller fret and fume .
Here be two sailor in a gig with the top thrown back , both puff cigar and swear all sort of forecastle oath ; there , in a smart chaise , a dashingly-dressed gentleman and lady , he from a tailor 's shop-board and she from a milliner 's back room -- the aristocrat of a summer afternoon .
And what be the haughtiest of u but the ephemeral aristocrat of a summer 's day ?
Here be a tin-pedler whose glitter ware bedazzle all beholder like a travel meteor or opposition sun , and on the other side a seller of spruce beer , which brisk liquor be confine in several dozen of stone bottle .
Here conic a party of lady on horseback , in green riding habit , and gentleman attendant , and there a flock of sheep for the market , patter over the bridge with a multitude nous clatter of their little hoof ; here a Frenchman with a hand-organ on his shoulder , and there an itinerant Swiss jeweller .
On this side , herald by a blast of clarion and bugle , appear a train of wagon convey all the wild beast of a caravan ; and on that a company of summer soldier march from village to village on a festival campaign , attend by the `` brass band . ''
Now look at the scene , and it present an emblem of the mysterious confusion , the apparently insolvable riddle , in which individual , or the great world itself , seem often to be involve .
What miracle shall set all thing right again ?
But see !
the schooner have thrust her bulky carcase through the chasm ; the draw descend ; horse and foot pas onward and leave the bridge vacant from end to end .
`` And thus , '' muse the toll-gatherer , `` have I find it with all stoppage , even though the universe seem to be at a stand . ''
The sage old man !
Far westward now the reddening sun throw a broad sheet of splendor across the flood , and to the eye of distant boatman gleam brightly among the timber of the bridge .
Strollers come from the town to quaff the freshening breeze .
One or two let down long line and haul up flap flounder or cunner or small cod , or perhaps an eel .
Others , and fair girl among them , with the flush of the hot day still on their cheek , bend over the railing and watch the heap of seaweed float upward with the flow tide .
The horse now tramp heavily along the bridge and wistfully bethink them of their stable .
-- Rest , rest , thou weary world !
for to-morrow 's round of toil and pleasure will be as wearisome a to-day 's have be , yet both shall bear thee onward a day 's march of eternity .
-- Now the old toll-gatherer look seaward and discern the lighthouse kindle on a far island , and the star , too , kindle in the sky , a if but a little way beyond ; and , mingle revery of heaven with remembrance of earth , the whole procession of mortal traveller , all the dusty pilgrimage which he have witness , seem like a flit show of phantom for his thoughtful soul to muse upon .
THE VISION OF THE FOUNTAIN .
At fifteen I become a resident in a country village more than a hundred mile from home .
The morning after my arrival -- a September morning , but warm and bright a any in July -- I ramble into a wood of oak with a few walnut tree intermix , form the close shade above my head .
The ground be rocky , uneven , overgrown with bush and clump of young sapling and traverse only by cattle-paths .
The track which I chance to follow lead me to a crystal spring with a border of grass a freshly green a on May morning , and overshadow by the limb of a great oak .
One solitary sunbeam find it way down and play like a goldfish in the water .
From my childhood I have love to gaze into a spring .
The water fill a circular basin , small but deep and set round with stone , some of which be cover with slimy moss , the others naked and of variegated hue -- reddish , white and brown .
The bottom be cover with coarse sand , which sparkle in the lonely sunbeam and seem to illuminate the spring with an unborrowed light .
In one spot the gush of the water violently agitate the sand , but without obscure the fountain or break the glassiness of it surface .
It appear a if some living creature be about to emerge -- the naiad of the spring , perhaps , in the shape of a beautiful young woman with a gown of filmy water-moss , a belt of rainbow-drops and a cold , pure , passionless countenance .
How would the beholder shiver , pleasantly yet fearfully , to see her sit on one of the stone , paddle her white foot in the ripple and throw up water to sparkle in the sun !
Wherever she lay her hand on grass and flower , they would immediately be moist , a with morning dew .
Then would she set about her labor , like a careful housewife , to clear the fountain of withered leaf , and bit of slimy wood , and old acorn from the oak above , and grain of corn leave by cattle in drinking , till the bright sand in the bright water be like a treasury of diamond .
But , should the intruder approach too near , he would find only the drop of a summer shower glisten about the spot where he have see her .
Reclining on the border of grass where the dewy goddess should have be , I bend forward , and a pair of eye meet mine within the watery mirror .
They be the reflection of my own .
I look again , and , lo !
another face , deep in the fountain than my own image , more distinct in all the feature , yet faint a thought .
The vision have the aspect of a fair young girl with lock of paly gold .
A mirthful expression laugh in the eye and dimple over the whole shadowy countenance , till it seem just what a fountain would be if , while dance merrily into the sunshine , it should assume the shape of woman .
Through the dim rosiness of the cheek I could see the brown leaf , the slimy twig , the acorn and the sparkling sand .
The solitary sunbeam be diffuse among the golden hair , which melt into it faint brightness and become a glory round that head so beautiful .
My description can give no idea how suddenly the fountain be thus tenant and how soon it be leave desolate .
I breathe , and there be the face ; I hold my breath , and it be go .
Had it pass away or fade into nothing ?
I doubt whether it have ever be .
My sweet reader , what a dreamy and delicious hour do I spend where that vision find and leave me !
For a long time I sit perfectly still , wait till it should reappear , and fearful that the slight motion , or even the flutter of my breath , might frighten it away .
Thus have I often start from a pleasant dream , and then keep quiet in hope to wile it back .
Deep be my musing a to the race and attribute of that ethereal be .
Had I create her ?
Was she the daughter of my fancy , akin to those strange shape which peep under the lid of child 's eye ?
And do her beauty gladden me for that one moment and then die ?
Or be she a water-nymph within the fountain , or fairy or woodland goddess peeping over my shoulder , or the ghost of some forsaken maid who have drown herself for love ?
Or , in good truth , have a lovely girl with a warm heart and lip that would bear pressure steal softly behind me and throw her image into the spring ?
I watch and wait , but no vision come again .
I depart , but with a spell upon me which draw me back that same afternoon to the haunt spring .
There be the water gushing , the sand sparkling and the sunbeam glimmering .
There the vision be not , but only a great frog , the hermit of that solitude , who immediately withdraw his speckle snout and make himself invisible -- all except a pair of long leg -- beneath a stone .
Methought he have a devilish look .
I could have slay him a an enchanter who keep the mysterious beauty imprison in the fountain .
Sad and heavy , I be return to the village .
Between me and the church-spire rise a little hill , and on it summit a group of tree insulate from all the rest of the wood , with their own share of radiance hover on them from the west and their own solitary shadow fall to the east .
The afternoon be far decline , the sunshine be almost pensive and the shade almost cheerful ; glory and gloom be mingle in the placid light , a if the spirit of the Day and Evening have meet in friendship under those tree and find themselves akin .
I be admire the picture when the shape of a young girl emerge from behind the clump of oak .
My heart know her : it be the vision , but so distant and ethereal do she seem , so unmixed with earth , so imbue with the pensive glory of the spot where she be stand , that my spirit sunk within me , sad than before .
How could I ever reach her ?
While I gaze a sudden shower come patter down upon the leaf .
In a moment the air be full of brightness , each raindrop catch a portion of sunlight a it fell , and the whole gentle shower appear like a mist , just substantial enough to bear the burden of radiance .
A rainbow vivid a Niagara 's be paint in the air .
Its southern limb come down before the group of tree and envelop the fair vision a if the hue of heaven be the only garment for her beauty .
When the rainbow vanish , she who have seem a part of it be no long there .
Was her existence absorb in nature 's lovely phenomenon , and do her pure frame dissolve away in the varied light ?
Yet I would not despair of her return , for , rob in the rainbow , she be the emblem of Hope .
Thus do the vision leave me , and many a doleful day succeed to the parting moment .
By the spring and in the wood and on the hill and through the village , at dewy sunrise , burn noon , and at that magic hour of sunset , when she have vanish from my sight , I seek her , but in vain .
Weeks come and go , month roll away , and she appear not in them .
I impart my mystery to none , but wander to and fro or sit in solitude like one that have catch a glimpse of heaven and could take no more joy on earth .
I withdraw into an inner world where my thought live and breathe , and the vision in the midst of them .
Without intend it , I become at once the author and hero of a romance , conjure up rival , imagine event , the action of others and my own , and experience every change of passion , till jealousy and despair have their end in bliss .
Oh , have I the burn fancy of my early youth with manhood 's colder gift , the power of expression , your heart , sweet lady , should flutter at my tale .
In the middle of January I be summon home .
The day before my departure , visit the spot which have be hallow by the vision , I find that the spring have a frozen bosom , and nothing but the snow and a glare of winter sunshine on the hill of the rainbow .
`` Let me hope , '' think I , `` or my heart will be as icy a the fountain and the whole world as desolate a this snowy hill . ''
Most of the day be spend in prepare for the journey , which be to commence at four o'clock the next morning .
About an hour after supper , when all be in readiness , I descend from my chamber to the sitting-room to take leave of the old clergyman and his family with whom I have be an inmate .
A gust of wind blew out my lamp a I pass through the entry .
According to their invariable custom -- so pleasant a one when the fire blaze cheerfully -- the family be sit in the parlor with no other light than what come from the hearth .
As the good clergyman 's scanty stipend compel him to use all sort of economy , the foundation of his fire be always a large heap of tan , or ground bark , which would smoulder away from morning till night with a dull warmth and no flame .
This even the heap of tan be newly put on and surmount with three stick of red oak full of moisture , and a few piece of dry pine that have not yet kindle .
There be no light except the little that come sullenly from two half-burnt brand , without even glimmer on the andiron .
But I know the position of the old minister 's arm-chair , and also where his wife sit with her knitting-work , and how to avoid his two daughter -- one a stout country lass , and the other a consumptive girl .
Groping through the gloom , I find my own place next to that of the son , a learned collegian who have come home to keep school in the village during the winter vacation .
I notice that there be less room than usual to-night between the collegian 's chair and mine .
As people be always taciturn in the dark , not a word be say for some time after my entrance .
Nothing break the stillness but the regular click of the matron 's knitting-needles .
At time the fire throw out a brief and dusky gleam which twinkle on the old man 's glass and hover doubtfully round our circle , but be far too faint to portray the individual who compose it .
Were we not like ghost ?
Dreamy a the scene be , might it not be a type of the mode in which departed people who have know and love each other here would hold communion in eternity ?
We be aware of each other 's presence , not by sight nor sound nor touch , but by an inward consciousness .
Would it not be so among the dead ?
The silence be interrupt by the consumptive daughter address a remark to some one in the circle whom she call Rachel .
Her tremulous and decayed accent be answer by a single word , but in a voice that make me start and bend toward the spot whence it have proceed .
Had I ever hear that sweet , low tone ?
If not , why do it rouse up so many old recollection , or mockery of such , the shadow of thing familiar yet unknown , and fill my mind with confused image of her feature who have speak , though bury in the gloom of the parlor ?
Whom have my heart recognize , that it throb so ?
I listen to catch her gentle breathing , and strive by the intensity of my gaze to picture forth a shape where none be visible .
Suddenly the dry pine caught ; the fire blaze up with a ruddy glow , and where the darkness have be , there be she -- the vision of the fountain .
A spirit of radiance only , she have vanish with the rainbow and appear again in the firelight , perhaps to flicker with the blaze and be go .
Vet her cheek be rosy and lifelike , and her feature , in the bright warmth of the room , be even sweet and tender than my recollection of them .
She know me .
The mirthful expression that have laugh in her eye and dimple over her countenance when I behold her faint beauty in the fountain be laugh and dimple there now .
One moment our glance mingle ; the next , down roll the heap of tan upon the kindle wood , and darkness snatch away that daughter of the light , and give her back to me no more !
Fair lady , there be nothing more to tell .
Must the simple mystery be reveal , then , that Rachel be the daughter of the village squire and have leave home for a boarding-school the morning after I arrive and return the day before my departure ?
If I transform her to an angel , it be what every youthful lover do for his mistress .
Therein consist the essence of my story .
But slight the change , sweet maid , to make angel of yourselves .
FANCY 'S SHOW-BOX .
A MORALITY .
What be guilt ?
A stain upon the soul .
And it be a point of vast interest whether the soul may contract such stain in all their depth and flagrancy from deed which may have be plot and resolve upon , but which physically have never have existence .
Must the fleshly hand and visible frame of man set it seal to the evil design of the soul , in order to give them their entire validity against the sinner ?
Or , while none but crime perpetrate be cognizable before an earthly tribunal , will guilty thought -- of which guilty deed be no more than shadow , -- will these draw down the full weight of a condemn sentence in the supreme court of eternity ?
In the solitude of a midnight chamber or in a desert afar from men or in a church while the body be kneel the soul may pollute itself even with those crime which we be accustom to deem altogether carnal .
If this be true , it be a fearful truth .
Let u illustrate the subject by an imaginary example .
A venerable gentleman -- one Mr. Smith -- who have long be regard a a pattern of moral excellence be warm his age blood with a glass or two of generous wine .
His child be go forth about their worldly business and his grandchild at school , he sit alone in a deep luxurious arm-chair with his foot beneath a richly-carved mahogany table .
Some old people have a dread of solitude , and when good company may not be have rejoice even to hear the quiet breathing of a babe asleep upon the carpet .
But Mr. Smith , whose silver hair be the bright symbol of a life unstained except by such spot a be inseparable from human nature -- he have no need of a babe to protect him by it purity , nor of a grown person to stand between him and his own soul .
Nevertheless , either manhood must converse with age , or womanhood must soothe him with gentle care , or infancy must sport around his chair , or his thought will stray into the misty region of the past and the old man be chill and sad .
Wine will not always cheer him .
Such might have be the case with Mr. Smith , when , through the brilliant medium of his glass of old Madeira , he behold three figure enter the room .
These be Fancy , who have assume the garb and aspect of an itinerant showman , with a box of picture on her back ; and Memory , in the likeness of a clerk , with a pen behind her ear , an inkhorn at her buttonhole and a huge manuscript volume beneath her arm ; and lastly , behind the other two , a person shroud in a dusky mantle which conceal both face and form .
But Mr. Smith have a shrewd idea that it be Conscience .
How kind of Fancy , Memory and Conscience to visit the old gentleman just a he be begin to imagine that the wine have neither so bright a sparkle nor so excellent a flavor a when himself and the liquor be less age !
Through the dim length of the apartment , where crimson curtain muffle the glare of sunshine and create a rich obscurity , the three guest draw near the silver-haired old man .
Memory , with a finger between the leaf of her huge volume , place herself at his right hand ; Conscience , with her face still hide in the dusky mantle , take her station on the left , so a to be next his heart ; while Fancy set down her picture-box upon the table with the magnifying-glass convenient to his eye .
We can sketch merely the outline of two or three out of the many picture which at the pulling of a string successively people the box with the semblance of living scene .
One be a moonlight picture , in the background a lowly dwelling , and in front , partly shadow by a tree , yet besprinkle with flake of radiance , two youthful figure , male and female .
The young man stand with folded arm , a haughty smile upon his lip and a gleam of triumph in his eye a he glance downward at the kneel girl .
She be almost prostrate at his foot , evidently sink under a weight of shame and anguish which hardly allow her to lift her clasped hand in supplication .
Her eye she could not lift .
But neither her agony , nor the lovely feature on which it be depict , nor the slender grace of the form which it convulse , appear to soften the obduracy of the young man .
He be the personification of triumphant scorn .
Now , strange to say , a old Mr. Smith peep through the magnifying-glass , which make the object start out from the canvas with magical deception , he begin to recognize the farmhouse , the tree and both the figure of the picture .
The young man in time long past have often meet his gaze within the looking-glass ; the girl be the very image of his first love -- his cottage-love , his Martha Burroughs .
Mr. Smith be scandalize .
`` Oh , vile and slanderous picture ! ''
he exclaim .
`` When have I triumph over ruin innocence ?
Was not Martha wed in her teen to David Tomkins , who win her girlish love and long enjoy her affection a a wife ?
And ever since his death she have live a reputable widow ! ''
Meantime , Memory be turn over the leaf of her volume , rustle them to and fro with uncertain finger , until among the early page she find one which have reference to this picture .
She read it close to the old gentleman 's ear : it be a record merely of sinful thought which never be embody in an act , but , while Memory be read , Conscience unveil her face and strike a dagger to the heart of Mr. Smith .
Though not a death-blow , the torture be extreme .
The exhibition proceed .
One after another Fancy display her picture , all of which appear to have be paint by some malicious artist on purpose to vex Mr. Smith .
Not a shadow of proof could have be adduce in any earthly court that he be guilty of the slight of those sin which be thus make to stare him in the face .
In one scene there be a table set out , with several bottle and glass half fill with wine , which throw back the dull ray of an expiring lamp .
There have be mirth and revelry until the hand of the clock stand just at midnight , when Murder step between the boon-companions .
A young man have fall on the floor , and lay stone dead with a ghastly wound crush into his temple , while over him , with a delirium of mingled rage and horror in his countenance , stand the youthful likeness of Mr. Smith .
The murder youth wear the feature of Edward Spencer .
`` What do this rascal of a painter mean ? ''
cry Mr. Smith , provoke beyond all patience .
`` Edward Spencer be my early and dear friend , true to me a I to him through more than half a century .
Neither I nor any other ever murder him .
Was he not alive within five year , and do he not , in token of our long friendship , bequeath me his gold-headed cane and a mourning-ring ? ''
Again have Memory be turn over her volume , and fix at length upon so confused a page that she surely must have scribble it when she be tipsy .
The purport be , however , that while Mr. Smith and Edward Spencer be heat their young blood with wine a quarrel have flash up between them , and Mr. Smith , in deadly wrath , have fling a bottle at Spencer 's head .
True , it miss it aim and merely smash a looking-glass ; and the next morning , when the incident be imperfectly remember , they have shake hand with a hearty laugh .
Yet , again , while Memory be read , Conscience unveil her face , strike a dagger to the heart of Mr. Smith and quell his remonstrance with her iron frown .
The pain be quite excruciate .
Some of the picture have be paint with so doubtful a touch , and in color so faint and pale , that the subject could barely be conjecture .
A dull , semi-transparent mist have be throw over the surface of the canvas , into which the figure seem to vanish while the eye seek most earnestly to fix them .
But in every scene , however dubiously portray , Mr. Smith be invariably haunt by his own lineament at various age a in a dusty mirror .
After pore several minute over one of these blur and almost indistinguishable picture , he begin to see that the painter have intend to represent him , now in the decline of life , a strip the clothes from the back of three half-starved child .
`` Really , this puzzle me ! ''
quoth Mr. Smith , with the irony of conscious rectitude .
`` Asking pardon of the painter , I pronounce him a fool as well a a scandalous knave .
A man of my standing in the world to be rob little child of their clothes !
Ridiculous ! ''
But while he speak Memory have search her fatal volume and find a page which with her sad calm voice she pour into his ear .
It be not altogether inapplicable to the misty scene .
It tell how Mr. Smith have be grievously tempt by many devilish sophistry , on the ground of a legal quibble , to commence a lawsuit against three orphan-children , joint-heirs to a considerable estate .
Fortunately , before he be quite decided , his claim have turn out nearly a devoid of law a justice .
As Memory cease to read Conscience again thrust aside her mantle , and would have strike her victim with the envenomed dagger only that he struggle and clasp his hand before his heart .
Even then , however , he sustain an ugly gash .
Why should we follow Fancy through the whole series of those awful picture ?
Painted by an artist of wondrous power and terrible acquaintance with the secret soul , they embody the ghost of all the never-perpetrated sin that have glide through the lifetime of Mr. Smith .
And could such being of cloudy fantasy , so near akin to nothingness , give valid evidence against him at the day of judgment ?
Be that the case or not , there be reason to believe that one truly penitential tear would have wash away each hateful picture and leave the canvas white a snow .
But Mr. Smith , at a prick of Conscience too keen to be endure , bellow aloud with impatient agony , and suddenly discover that his three guest be go .
There he sit alone , a silver-haired and highly-venerated old man , in the rich gloom of the crimsoned-curtained room , with no box of picture on the table , but only a decanter of most excellent Madeira .
Yet his heart still seem to fester with the venom of the dagger .
Nevertheless , the unfortunate old gentleman might have argue the matter with Conscience and allege many reason wherefore she should not smite him so pitilessly .
Were we to take up his cause , it should be somewhat in the following fashion .
A scheme of guilt , till it be put in execution , greatly resemble a train of incident in a projected tale .
The latter , in order to produce a sense of reality in the reader 's mind , must be conceive with such proportionate strength by the author a to seem in the glow of fancy more like truth , past , present or to come , than purely fiction .
The prospective sinner , on the other hand , weave his plot of crime , but seldom or never feel a perfect certainty that it will be execute .
There be a dreaminess diffuse about his thought ; in a dream , a it be , he strike the death-blow into his victim 's heart and start to find an indelible blood-stain on his hand .
Thus a novel-writer or a dramatist , in create a villain of romance and fit him with evil deed , and the villain of actual life in project crime that will be perpetrate , may almost meet each other halfway between reality and fancy .
It be not until the crime be accomplish that Guilt clench it gripe upon the guilty heart and claim it for his own .
Then , and not before , sin be actually felt and acknowledge , and , if unaccompanied by repentance , grow a thousandfold more virulent by it self-consciousness .
Be it consider , also , that men often overestimate their capacity for evil .
At a distance , while it attendant circumstance do not press upon their notice and it result be dimly see , they can bear to contemplate it .
They may take the step which lead to crime , impel by the same sort of mental action a in work out a mathematical problem , yet be powerless with compunction at the final moment .
They know not what deed it be that they deem themselves resolve to do .
In truth , there be no such thing in man 's nature a a settled and full resolve , either for good or evil , except at the very moment of execution .
Let u hope , therefore , that all the dreadful consequence of sin will not be incur unless the act have set it seal upon the thought .
Yet , with the slight fancy-work which we have frame , some sad and awful truth be interwoven .
Man must not disclaim his brotherhood even with the guilty , since , though his hand be clean , his heart have surely be pollute by the flitting phantom of iniquity .
He must feel that when he shall knock at the gate of heaven no semblance of an unspotted life can entitle him to entrance there .
Penitence must kneel and Mercy come from the footstool of the throne , or that golden gate will never open .
DR. HEIDEGGER 'S EXPERIMENT .
That very singular man old Dr. Heidegger once invite four venerable friend to meet him in his study .
There be three white-bearded gentleman -- Mr. Medbourne , Colonel Killigrew and Mr. Gascoigne -- and a withered gentlewoman whose name be the widow Wycherly .
They be all melancholy old creature who have be unfortunate in life , and whose great misfortune it be that they be not long ago in their graf .
Mr. Medbourne , in the vigor of his age , have be a prosperous merchant , but have lose his all by a frantic speculation , and be now little good than a mendicant .
Colonel Killigrew have waste his best year and his health and substance in the pursuit of sinful pleasure which have give birth to a brood of pain , such a the gout and diver other torment of soul and body .
Mr. Gascoigne be a ruined politician , a man of evil fame -- or , at least , have be so till time have bury him from the knowledge of the present generation and make him obscure instead of infamous .
As for the widow Wycherly , tradition tell u that she be a great beauty in her day , but for a long while past she have live in deep seclusion on account of certain scandalous story which have prejudice the gentry of the town against her .
It be a circumstance worth mention that each of these three old gentleman -- Mr. Medbourne , Colonel Killigrew and Mr. Gascoigne -- be early lover of the widow Wycherly , and have once be on the point of cut each other 's throat for her sake .
And before proceed farther I will merely hint that Dr. Heidegger and all his four guest be sometimes think to be a little beside themselves , as be not infrequently the case with old people when worry either by present trouble or woeful recollection .
`` My dear old friend , '' say Dr. Heidegger , motion them to be seat , `` I be desirous of your assistance in one of those little experiment with which I amuse myself here in my study . ''
If all story be true , Dr. Heidegger 's study must have be a very curious place .
It be a dim , old-fashioned chamber festoon with cobweb and besprinkle with antique dust .
Around the wall stand several oaken bookcase , the low shelf of which be fill with row of gigantic folio and black-letter quarto , and the upper with little parchment-covered duodecimos .

In the obscure corner of the room stand a tall and narrow oaken closet with it door ajar , within which doubtfully appear a skeleton .
Between two of the bookcase hang a looking-glass , present it high and dusty plate within a tarnished gilt frame .
Among many wonderful story relate of this mirror , it be fabled that the spirit of all the doctor 's deceased patient dwell within it verge and would stare him in the face whenever he look thitherward .
The opposite side of the chamber be ornament with the full-length portrait of a young lady array in the faded magnificence of silk , satin and brocade , and with a visage as fade a her dress .
Above half a century ago Dr. Heidegger have be on the point of marriage with this young lady , but , be affect with some slight disorder , she have swallow one of her lover 's prescription and die on the bridal-evening .
The great curiosity of the study remain to be mention : it be a ponderous folio volume bound in black leather , with massive silver clasp .
There be no letter on the back , and nobody could tell the title of the book .
But it be well know to be a book of magic , and once , when a chambermaid have lift it merely to brush away the dust , the skeleton have rattle in it closet , the picture of the young lady have step one foot upon the floor and several ghastly face have peep forth from the mirror , while the brazen head of Hippocrates frown and say , `` Forbear ! ''
Such be Dr. Heidegger 's study .
On the summer afternoon of our tale a small round table as black a ebony stood in the centre of the room , sustain a cut-glass vase of beautiful form and elaborate workmanship .
The sunshine come through the window between the heavy festoon of two fade damask curtain and fell directly across this vase , so that a mild splendor be reflect from it on the ashen visage of the five old people who sit around .
Four champagne-glasses be also on the table .
`` My dear old friend , '' repeat Dr. Heidegger , `` may I reckon on your aid in perform an exceedingly curious experiment ? ''
Now , Dr. Heidegger be a very strange old gentleman whose eccentricity have become the nucleus for a thousand fantastic story .
Some of these fable -- to my shame be it spoken -- might possibly be trace back to mine own veracious self ; and if any passage of the present tale should startle the reader 's faith , I must be content to bear the stigma of a fiction-monger .
When the doctor 's four guest hear him talk of his propose experiment , they anticipate nothing more wonderful than the murder of a mouse in an air-pump or the examination of a cobweb by the microscope , or some similar nonsense with which he be constantly in the habit of pester his intimate .
But without wait for a reply Dr. Heidegger hobble across the chamber and return with the same ponderous folio bound in black leather which common report affirm to be a book of magic .
Undoing the silver clasp , he open the volume and take from among it black-letter page a rise , or what be once a rise , though now the green leaf and crimson petal have assume one brownish hue and the ancient flower seem ready to crumble to dust in the doctor 's hand .
`` This rise , '' say Dr. Heidegger , with a sigh -- `` this same wither and crumble flower -- blossom five and fifty year ago .
It be give me by Sylvia Ward , whose portrait hang yonder , and I mean to wear it in my bosom at our wedding .
Five and fifty year it have be treasure between the leaf of this old volume .
Now , would you deem it possible that this rise of half a century could ever bloom again ? ''
`` Nonsense ! ''
say the widow Wycherly , with a peevish toss of her head .
`` You might as well ask whether an old woman 's wrinkled face could ever bloom again . ''
`` See ! ''
answer Dr. Heidegger .
He uncover the vase and throw the fade rise into the water which it contain .
At first it lay lightly on the surface of the fluid , appear to imbibe none of it moisture .
Soon , however , a singular change begin to be visible .
The crushed and dried petal stir and assume a deepen tinge of crimson , a if the flower be revive from a deathlike slumber , the slender stalk and twig of foliage become green , and there be the rise of half a century , look as fresh a when Sylvia Ward have first give it to her lover .
It be scarcely full-blown , for some of it delicate red leave curl modestly around it moist bosom , within which two or three dewdrop be sparkle .

`` Pray , how be it effect ? ''
`` Did you never hear of the Fountain of Youth ? ''
ask Dr. Heidegger , `` which Ponce de Leon , the Spanish adventurer , go in search of two or three century ago ? ''
`` But do Ponce de Leon ever find it ? ''

`` No , '' answer Dr. Heidegger , `` for he never seek it in the right place .

But Dr. Heidegger beseech them to stay a moment .
`` Before you drink , my respectable old friend , '' say he , `` it would be well that , with the experience of a lifetime to direct you , you should draw up a few general rule for your guidance in pass a second time through the peril of youth .
Think what a sin and shame it would be if , with your peculiar advantage , you should not become pattern of virtue and wisdom to all the young people of the age ! ''
The doctor 's four venerable friend make him no answer except by a feeble and tremulous laugh , so very ridiculous be the idea that , know how closely Repentance tread behind the step of Error , they should ever go astray again .

There be a healthful suffusion on their cheek instead of the ashen hue that have make them look so corpse-like .

`` We be young , but we be still too old .
Quick !
give u more ! ''
`` Patience , patience ! ''
quoth Dr. Heidegger , who sit , watch the experiment with philosophic coolness .
`` You have be a long time grow old ; surely you might be content to grow young in half an hour .
But the water be at your service . ''
Again he fill their glass with the liquor of youth , enough of which still remain in the vase to turn half the old people in the city to the age of their own grandchild .
While the bubble be yet sparkle on the brim the doctor 's four guest snatch their glass from the table and swallow the content at a single gulp .
Was it delusion ?
Even while the draught be pass down their throat it seem to have work a change on their whole system .
Their eye grow clear and bright ; a dark shade deepen among their silvery lock : they sit around the table three gentleman of middle age and a woman hardly beyond her buxom prime .

Now he rattle forth full-throated sentence about patriotism , national glory and the people 's right ; now he mutter some perilous stuff or other in a sly and doubtful whisper , so cautiously that even his own conscience could scarcely catch the secret ; and now , again , he speak in measured accent and a deeply-deferential tone , a if a royal ear be listen to his well-turned period .

`` Certainly , my dear madam -- certainly , '' reply the complaisant doctor .
`` See !
I have already fill the glass . ''
There , in fact , stand the four glass brimful of this wonderful water , the delicate spray of which , a it effervesce from the surface , resemble the tremulous glitter of diamond .

Even while quaff the third draught of the Fountain of Youth , they be almost awe by the expression of his mysterious visage .
But the next moment the exhilarate gush of young life shot through their vein .
They be now in the happy prime of youth .
Age , with it miserable train of care and sorrow and disease , be remember only a the trouble of a dream from which they have joyously awake .
The fresh gloss of the soul , so early lost and without which the world 's successive scene have be but a gallery of faded picture , again throw it enchantment over all their prospect .
They felt like new-created being in a new-created universe .
`` We be young !
We be young ! ''
they cry , exultingly .
Youth , like the extremity of age , have efface the strongly-marked characteristic of middle life and mutually assimilate them all .
They be a group of merry youngster almost madden with the exuberant frolicsomeness of their year .

They laugh loudly at their old-fashioned attire -- the wide-skirted coat and flap waistcoat of the young men and the ancient cap and gown of the blooming girl .

`` Pray excuse me , '' answer the doctor , quietly .
`` I be old and rheumatic , and my dancing-days be over long ago .
But either of these gay young gentleman will be glad of so pretty a partner . ''
`` Dance with me , Clara , '' cry Colonel Killigrew .
`` No , no !
I will be her partner , '' shout Mr. Gascoigne .
`` She promise me her hand fifty year ago , '' exclaim Mr. Medbourne .

`` Come , come , gentleman !
Come , Madam Wycherly ! ''

Was it an illusion ?
Had the change of a lifetime be crowd into so brief a space , and be they now four age people sit with their old friend Dr. Heidegger ?
`` Are we grown old again so soon ? ''
cry they , dolefully .
In truth , they have .
The Water of Youth possess merely a virtue more transient than that of wine ; the delirium which it create have effervesce away .
Yes , they be old again .
With a shudder impulse that show her a woman still , the widow clasp her skinny hand before her face and wish that the coffin-lid be over it , since it could be no longer beautiful .
`` Yes , friend , ye be old again , '' say Dr. Heidegger , `` and , lo !
the Water of Youth be all lavish on the ground .
Well , I bemoan it not ; for if the fountain gush at my very doorstep , I would not stoop to bathe my lip in it -- no , though it delirium be for year instead of moment .
Such be the lesson ye have teach me . ''
But the doctor 's four friend have teach no such lesson to themselves .
They resolve forthwith to make a pilgrimage to Florida and quaff at morning , noon and night from the Fountain of Youth .
LEGENDS OF THE PROVINCE-HOUSE .

I. HOWE 'S MASQUERADE .
One afternoon last summer , while walk along Washington street , my eye be attract by a sign-board protruding over a narrow archway nearly opposite the Old South Church .
The sign represent the front of a stately edifice which be designate a the `` OLD PROVINCE HOUSE , keep by Thomas Waite . ''
I be glad to be thus remind of a purpose , long entertain , of visit and ramble over the mansion of the old royal governor of Massachusetts , and , enter the arch passage which penetrate through the middle of a brick row of shop , a few step transport me from the busy heart of modern Boston into a small and secluded court-yard .
One side of this space be occupy by the square front of the Province House , three story high and surmount by a cupola , on the top of which a gild Indian be discernible , with his bow bent and his arrow on the string , a if aiming at the weathercock on the spire of the Old South .
The figure have keep this attitude for seventy year or more , ever since good Deacon Drowne , a cunning carver of wood , first station him on his long sentinel 's watch over the city .
The Province House be construct of brick , which seem recently to have be overlay with a coat of light-colored paint .
A flight of red freestone step fence in by a balustrade of curiously wrought iron ascends from the court-yard to the spacious porch , over which be a balcony with an iron balustrade of similar pattern and workmanship to that beneath .
These letter and figure -- `` 16 P.S . 79 '' -- be work into the ironwork of the balcony , and probably express the date of the edifice , with the initial of it founder 's name .
A wide door with double leaf admit me into the hall or entry , on the right of which be the entrance to the bar-room .
It be in this apartment , I presume , that the ancient governor hold their levee with vice-regal pomp , surround by the military men , the counsellor , the judge , and other officer of the Crown , while all the loyalty of the province throng to do them honor .
But the room in it present condition can not boast even of faded magnificence .
The panelled wainscot be cover with dingy paint and acquire a duskier hue from the deep shadow into which the Province House be throw by the brick block that shut it in from Washington street .
A ray of sunshine never visit this apartment any more than the glare of the festal torch which have be extinguish from the era of the Revolution .
The most venerable and ornamental object be a chimney-piece set round with Dutch tile of blue-figured china , represent scene from Scripture , and , for aught I know , the lady of Pownall or Bernard may have sit beside this fireplace and tell her child the story of each blue tile .
A bar in modern style , well replenish with decanter , bottle , cigar-boxes and network bag of lemon , and provide with a beer-pump and a soda-fount , extend along one side of the room .
At my entrance an elderly person be smack his lip with a zest which satisfy me that the cellar of the Province House still hold good liquor , though doubtless of other vintage than be quaff by the old governor .
After sip a glass of port-sangaree prepare by the skilful hand of Mr. Thomas Waite , I beseech that worthy successor and representative of so many historic personage to conduct me over their time-honored mansion .
He readily comply , but , to confess the truth , I be force to draw strenuously upon my imagination in order to find aught that be interest in a house which , without it historic association , would have seem merely such a tavern a be usually favor by the custom of decent city boarder and old-fashioned country gentleman .
The chamber , which be probably spacious in former time , be now cut up by partition and subdivide into little nooks , each afford scanty room for the narrow bed and chair and dressing-table of a single lodger : The great staircase , however , may be term , without much hyperbole , a feature of grandeur and magnificence .
It wind through the midst of the house by flight of broad step , each flight terminate in a square landing-place , whence the ascent be continue toward the cupola .
A carved balustrade , freshly paint in the low story , but grow dingier a we ascend , border the staircase with it quaintly twist and intertwine pillar , from top to bottom .
Up these stair the military boot , or perchance the gouty shoe , of many a governor have tread a the wearer mount to the cupola which afford them so wide a view over their metropolis and the surrounding country .
The cupola be an octagon with several window , and a door open upon the roof .
From this station , a I please myself with imagining , Gage may have behold his disastrous victory on Bunker Hill -LRB- unless one of the tri-mountains intervene -RRB- , and Howe have mark the approach of Washington 's besieging army , although the building since erect in the vicinity have shut out almost every object save the steeple of the Old South , which seem almost within arm 's length .
Descending from the cupola , I pause in the garret to observe the ponderous white-oak framework , so much more massive than the frame of modern house , and thereby resemble an antique skeleton .
The brick wall , the material of which be import from Holland , and the timber of the mansion , be still as sound a ever , but , the floor and other interior part be greatly decay , it be contemplate to gut the whole and build a new house within the ancient frame-and brickwork .
Among other inconvenience of the present edifice , mine host mention that any jar or motion be apt to shake down the dust of age out of the ceiling of one chamber upon the floor of that beneath it .
We step forth from the great front window into the balcony where in old time it be doubtless the custom of the king 's representative to show himself to a loyal populace , requite their huzzas and tossed-up hat with stately bending of his dignified person .
In those day the front of the Province House look upon the street , and the whole site now occupy by the brick range of store , as well a the present court-yard , be lay out in grass-plats overshadow by tree and border by a wrought-iron fence .
Now the old aristocratic edifice hide it time-worn visage behind an upstart modern building ; at one of the back window I observe some pretty tailoresses sew and chat and laugh , with now and then a careless glance toward the balcony .
Descending thence , we again enter the bar-room , where the elderly gentleman above mention -- the smack of whose lip have speak so favorably for Mr. Waite 's good liquor -- be still lounge in his chair .
He seem to be , if not a lodger , at least a familiar visitor of the house who might be suppose to have his regular score at the bar , his summer seat at the open window and his prescriptive corner at the winter 's fireside .
Being of a sociable aspect , I venture to address him with a remark calculate to draw forth his historical reminiscence , if any such be in his mind , and it gratify me to discover that , between memory and tradition , the old gentleman be really possess of some very pleasant gossip about the Province House .
The portion of his talk which chiefly interest me be the outline of the following legend .
He profess to have receive it at one or two remove from an eye-witness , but this derivation , together with the lapse of time , must have afford opportunity for many variation of the narrative ; so that , despair of literal and absolute truth , I have not scruple to make such further change a seemed conducive to the reader 's profit and delight .
* * * * * At one of the entertainment give at the province-house during the latter part of the siege of Boston there pass a scene which have never yet be satisfactorily explain .
The officer of the British army and the loyal gentry of the province , most of whom be collect within the beleaguered town , have be invite to a masqued ball , for it be the policy for Sir William Howe to hide the distress and danger of the period and the desperate aspect of the siege under an ostentation of festivity .
The spectacle of this evening , if the old member of the provincial court circle might be believe , be the most gay and gorgeous affair that have occur in the annals of the government .
The brilliantly-lighted apartment be throng with figure that seem to have step from the dark canvas of historic portrait or to have flit forth from the magic page of romance , or at least to have fly hither from one of the London theatre without a change of garment .
Steeled knight of the Conquest , beard statesman of Queen Elizabeth and high-ruffed lady of her court be mingle with character of comedy , such a a parti-colored Merry Andrew jingle his cap and bell , a Falstaff almost a provocative of laughter a his prototype , and a Don Quixote with a bean-pole for a lance and a pot-lid for a shield .
But the broad merriment be excite by a group of figure ridiculously dress in old regimentals which seem to have be purchase at a military rag-fair or pilfer from some receptacle of the cast-off clothes of both the French and British army .
Portions of their attire have probably be wear at the siege of Louisburg , and the coat of most recent cut might have be rent and tattered by sword , ball or bayonet a long ago a Wolfe 's victory .
One of these worthy -- a tall , lank figure brandish a rusty sword of immense longitude -- purport to be no less a personage than General George Washington , and the other principal officer of the American army , such a Gates , Lee , Putnam , Schuyler , Ward and Heath , be represent by similar scarecrow .
An interview in the mock-heroic style between the rebel warrior and the British commander-in-chief be receive with immense applause , which come loud of all from the loyalist of the colony .
There be one of the guest , however , who stand apart , eye these antic sternly and scornfully at once with a frown and a bitter smile .
It be an old man formerly of high station and great repute in the province , and who have be a very famous soldier in his day .
Some surprise have be express that a person of Colonel Joliffe 's know Whig principle , though now too old to take an active part in the contest , should have remain in Boston during the siege , and especially that he should consent to show himself in the mansion of Sir William Howe .
But thither he have come with a fair granddaughter under his arm , and there , amid all the mirth and buffoonery , stand this stern old figure , the best-sustained character in the masquerade , because so well represent the antique spirit of his native land .
The other guest affirm that Colonel Joliffe 's black puritanical scowl throw a shadow round about him , although , in spite of his sombre influence , their gayety continue to blaze high , like -- an ominous comparison -- the flickering brilliancy of a lamp which have but a little while to burn .
Eleven stroke full half an hour ago have peal from the clock of the Old South , when a rumor be circulate among the company that some new spectacle or pageant be about to be exhibit which should put a fitting close to the splendid festivity of the night .
`` What new jest have Your Excellency in hand ? ''
ask the Reverend Mather Byles , whose Presbyterian scruple have not keep him from the entertainment .
`` Trust me , sir , I have already laugh more than beseem my cloth at your Homeric confabulation with yonder ragamuffin general of the rebel .
One other such fit of merriment , and I must throw off my clerical wig and band . ''
`` Not so , good Dr. Byles , '' answer Sir William Howe ; `` if mirth be a crime , you have never gain your doctorate in divinity .
As to this new foolery , I know no more about it than yourself -- perhaps not so much .
Honestly , now , doctor , have you not stir up the sober brain of some of your countryman to enact a scene in our masquerade ? ''
`` Perhaps , '' slyly remark the granddaughter of Colonel Joliffe , whose high spirit have be sting by many taunt against New England -- `` perhaps we be to have a masque of allegorical figure -- Victory with trophy from Lexington and Bunker Hill , Plenty with her overflow horn to typify the present abundance in this good town , and Glory with a wreath for His Excellency 's brow . ''
Sir William Howe smile at word which he would have answer with one of his dark frown have they be utter by lip that wear a beard .
He be spar the necessity of a retort by a singular interruption .
A sound of music be hear without the house , a if proceeding from a full band of military instrument station in the street , play , not such a festal strain a be suit to the occasion , but a slow funeral-march .
The drum appear to be muffle , and the trumpet pour forth a wailing breath which at once hush the merriment of the auditor , fill all with wonder and some with apprehension .
The idea occur to many that either the funeral procession of some great personage have halt in front of the province-house , or that a corpse in a velvet-covered and gorgeously-decorated coffin be about to be bear from the portal .
After listen a moment , Sir William Howe call in a stern voice to the leader of the musician , who have hitherto enliven the entertainment with gay and lightsome melody .
The man be drum-major to one of the British regiment .
`` Dighton , '' demand the general , `` what mean this foolery ?
Bid your band silence that dead march , or , by my word , they shall have sufficient cause for their lugubrious strain .
Silence it , sirrah ! ''
`` Please , Your Honor , '' answer the drum-major , whose rubicund visage have lose all it color , `` the fault be none of mine .
I and my band be all here together , and I question whether there be a man of u that could play that march without book .
I never hear it but once before , and that be at the funeral of his late Majesty , King George II . ''
`` Well , well ! ''
say Sir William Howe , recover his composure ; `` it be the prelude to some masquerade antic .
Let it pass . ''
A figure now present itself , but among the many fantastic mask that be disperse through the apartment none could tell precisely from whence it come .
It be a man in an old-fashioned dress of black serge and have the aspect of a steward or principal domestic in the household of a nobleman or great English landholder .
This figure advance to the out door of the mansion , and , throw both it leaf wide open , withdraw a little to one side and look back toward the grand staircase , a if expect some person to descend .
At the same time , the music in the street sound a loud and doleful summons .
The eye of Sir William Howe and his guest be direct to the staircase , there appear on the uppermost landing-place , that be discernible from the bottom , several personage descend toward the door .
The foremost be a man of stern visage , wear a steeple-crowned hat and a skull-cap beneath it , a dark cloak and huge wrinkle boot that come halfway up his leg .
Under his arm be a rolled-up banner which seem to be the banner of England , but strangely rent and torn ; he have a sword in his right hand and grasp a Bible in his left .
The next figure be of milder aspect , yet full of dignity , wear a broad ruff , over which descend a beard , a gown of wrought velvet and a doublet and hose of black satin ; he carry a roll of manuscript in his hand .
Close behind these two come a young man of very striking countenance and demeanor with deep thought and contemplation on his brow , and perhaps a flash of enthusiasm in his eye ; his garb , like that of his predecessor , be of an antique fashion , and there be a stain of blood upon his ruff .
In the same group with these be three or four others , all men of dignity and evident command , and bear themselves like personage who be accustom to the gaze of the multitude .
It be the idea of the beholder that these figure go to join the mysterious funeral that have halt in front of the province-house , yet that supposition seem to be contradict by the air of triumph with which they wave their hand a they cross the threshold and vanish through the portal .
`` In the devil 's name , what be this ? ''
mutter Sir William Howe to a gentleman beside him .
`` A procession of the regicide judge of King Charles the martyr ? ''
