''
`` Why have that young man a stain of blood upon his ruff ? ''
ask Miss Joliffe .
`` Because in after-years , '' answer her grandfather , `` he lay down the wise head in England upon the block for the principle of liberty . ''
`` Will not Your Excellency order out the guard ? ''
whisper Lord Percy , who , with other British officer , have now assemble round the general .
`` There may be a plot under this mummery . ''
`` Tush !
we have nothing to fear , '' carelessly reply Sir William Howe .
`` There can be no bad treason in the matter than a jest , and that somewhat of the dullest .
Even be it a sharp and bitter one , our best policy would be to laugh it off .
See !
here come more of these gentry . ''
Another group of character have now partly descend the staircase .
The first be a venerable and white-bearded patriarch who cautiously felt his way downward with a staff .
Treading hastily behind him , and stretch forth his gauntleted hand a if to grasp the old man 's shoulder , come a tall soldier-like figure equip with a plumed cap of steel , a bright breastplate and a long sword , which rattle against the stair .
Next be see a stout man dress in rich and courtly attire , but not of courtly demeanor ; his gait have the swing motion of a seaman 's walk , and , chance to stumble on the staircase , he suddenly grow wrathful and be hear to mutter an oath .
He be follow by a noble-looking personage in a curled wig such a be represent in the portrait of Queen Anne 's time and earlier , and the breast of his coat be decorate with an embroidered star .

`` What worthy be these ? ''
`` If it please Your Excellency , they live somewhat before my day , '' answer the doctor ; `` but doubtless our friend the colonel have be hand and glove with them . ''
`` Their living face I never look upon , '' say Colonel Joliffe , gravely ; `` although I have speak face to face with many ruler of this land , and shall greet yet another with an old man 's blessing ere I die .
But we talk of these figure .
I take the venerable patriarch to be Bradstreet , the last of the Puritans , who be governor at ninety or thereabouts .
The next be Sir Edmund Andros , a tyrant , a any New England schoolboy will tell you , and therefore the people cast him down from his high seat into a dungeon .
Then come Sir William Phipps , shepherd , cooper , sea-captain and governor .
May many of his countryman rise as high from a low an origin !
Lastly , you saw the gracious earl of Bellamont , who rule u under King William . ''

`` Governor Belcher -- my old patron -- in his very shape and dress ! ''

`` This be an awful mockery . ''

`` Methinks they be miserable men -- these royal governor of Massachusetts , '' observe Miss Joliffe .
`` Heavens !
how dim the light grows ! ''
It be certainly a fact that the large lamp which illuminate the staircase now burn dim and duskily ; so that several figure which pass hastily down the stair and go forth from the porch appear rather like shadow than person of fleshly substance .
Sir William Howe and his guest stand at the door of the contiguous apartment watch the progress of this singular pageant with various emotion of anger , contempt or half-acknowledged fear , but still with an anxious curiosity .
The shape which now seem hasten to join the mysterious procession be recognize rather by strike peculiarity of dress or broad characteristic of manner than by any perceptible resemblance of feature to their prototype .
Their face , indeed , be invariably keep in deep shadow , but Dr. Byles and other gentleman who have long be familiar with the successive ruler of the province be hear to whisper the name of Shirley , of Pownall , of Sir Francis Bernard and of the well-remembered Hutchinson , thereby confess that the actor , whoever they might be , in this spectral march of governor have succeed in put on some distant portraiture of the real personage .
As they vanish from the door , still do these shadow toss their arm into the gloom of night with a dread expression of woe .
Following the mimic representative of Hutchinson come a military figure hold before his face the cock hat which he have take from his powdered head , but his epaulette and other insignia of rank be those of a general officer , and something in his mien remind the beholder of one who have recently be master of the province-house and chief of all the land .

`` Villain , unmuffle yourself ! ''
cry he .
`` You pass no farther . ''
The figure , without blench a hair 's - breadth from the sword which be point at his breast , make a solemn pause and lower the cape of the cloak from about his face , yet not sufficiently for the spectator to catch a glimpse of it .
But Sir William Howe have evidently see enough .

`` Hark !
The procession move , '' say Miss Joliffe .

`` Would Your Excellency inquire far into the mystery of the pageant ? ''

The empire of Britain in this ancient province be at it last gasp to-night ; almost while I speak it be a dead corpse , and methinks the shadow of the old governor be fit mourner at it funeral . ''
With these word Colonel Joliffe throw on his cloak , and , draw his granddaughter 's arm within his own , retire from the last festival that a British ruler ever hold in the old province of Massachusetts Bay .
It be suppose that the colonel and the young lady possess some secret intelligence in regard to the mysterious pageant of that night .
However this might be , such knowledge have never become general .
The actor in the scene have vanish into deep obscurity than even that wild Indian hand who scatter the cargo of the tea-ships on the wave and gain a place in history , yet leave no name .
But superstition , among other legend of this mansion , repeat the wondrous tale that on the anniversary night of Britain 's discomfiture the ghost of the ancient governor of Massachusetts still glide through the portal of the Province House .
And last of all come a figure shroud in a military cloak , toss his clenched hand into the air and stamp his iron-shod boot upon the broad freestone step with a semblance of feverish despair , but without the sound of a foot-tramp .
* * * * * When the truth-telling accent of the elderly gentleman be hush , I draw a long breath and look round the room , strive with the best energy of my imagination to throw a tinge of romance and historic grandeur over the reality of the scene .
But my nostril snuff up a scent of cigar-smoke , cloud of which the narrator have emit by way of visible emblem , I suppose , of the nebulous obscurity of his tale .
Moreover , my gorgeous fantasy be woefully disturb by the rattling of the spoon in a tumbler of whiskey-punch which Mr. Thomas Waite be mingle for a customer .
Nor do it add to the picturesque appearance of the panelled wall that the slate of the Brookline stage be suspend against them , instead of the armorial escutcheon of some far-descended governor .
A stage-driver sat at one of the window read a penny paper of the day -- the Boston Times -- and present a figure which could nowise be bring into any picture of `` Times in Boston '' seventy or a hundred year ago .
On the window-seat lay a bundle neatly do up in brown paper , the direction of which I have the idle curiosity to read : `` MISS SUSAN HUGGINS , at the PROVINCE HOUSE . ''
A pretty chambermaid , no doubt .
In truth , it be desperately hard work when we attempt to throw the spell of hoar antiquity over locality with which the living world and the day that be pass over u have aught to do .
Yet , a I glance at the stately staircase down which the procession of the old governor have descend , and a I emerge through the venerable portal whence their figure have precede me , it gladden me to be conscious of a thrill of awe .
Then , dive through the narrow archway , a few stride transport me into the dense throng of Washington street .
II .
EDWARD RANDOLPH 'S PORTRAIT .
The old legendary guest of the Province House abode in my remembrance from midsummer till January .
One idle even last winter , confident that he would be find in the snug corner of the bar-room , I resolve to pay him another visit , hop to deserve well of my country by snatch from oblivion some else unheard-of fact of history .
The night be chill and raw , and render boisterous by almost a gale of wind which whistle along Washington street , cause the gaslight to flare and flicker within the lamp .
As I hurry onward my fancy be busy with a comparison between the present aspect of the street and that which it probably wear when the British governor inhabit the mansion whither I be now go .
Brick edifice in those time be few till a succession of destructive fire have sweep , and swept again , the wooden dwelling and warehouse from the most populous quarter of the town .
The building stand insulated and independent , not , a now , merge their separate existence into connect range with a front of tiresome identity , but each possessing feature of it own , a if the owner 's individual taste have shape it , and the whole present a picturesque irregularity the absence of which be hardly compensate by any beauty of our modern architecture .
Such a scene , dimly vanish from the eye by the ray of here and there a tallow candle glimmer through the small pane of scattered window , would form a sombre contrast to the street a I behold it with the gaslight blaze from corner to corner , flame within the shop and throw a noonday brightness through the huge plate of glass .
But the black , lower sky , a I turn my eye upward , wear , doubtless , the same visage a when it frown upon the ante-Revolutionary New Englanders .
The wintry blast have the same shriek that be familiar to their ear .
The Old South Church , too , still point it antique spire into the darkness and be lose between earth and heaven , and , a I pass , it clock , which have warn so many generation how transitory be their lifetime , speak heavily and slow the same unregarded moral to myself .
`` Only seven o'clock ! ''
thought I . `` My old friend 's legend will scarcely kill the hour ` twixt this and bedtime . ''
Passing through the narrow arch , I cross the courtyard , the confined precinct of which be make visible by a lantern over the portal of the Province House .
On enter the bar-room , I find , a I expect , the old tradition-monger seat by a special good fire of anthracite , compel cloud of smoke from a corpulent cigar .
He recognize me with evident pleasure , for my rare property a a patient listener invariably make me a favorite with elderly gentleman and lady of narrative propensites .
Drawing a chair to the fire , I desire mine host to favor u with a glass apiece of whiskey-punch , which be speedily prepared , steam hot , with a slice of lemon at the bottom , a dark-red stratum of port wine upon the surface and a sprinkling of nutmeg strewn over all .
As we touch our glass together , my legendary friend make himself know to me a Mr. Bela Tiffany , and I rejoice at the oddity of the name , because it give his image and character a sort of individuality in my conception .
The old gentleman 's draught act a a solvent upon his memory , so that it overflow with tale , tradition , anecdote of famous dead people and trait of ancient manner , some of which be childish a a nurse 's lullaby , while others might have be worth the notice of the grave historian .
Nothing impress me more than a story of a black mysterious picture which use to hang in one of the chamber of the Province House , directly above the room where we be now sit .
The following be a correct a version of the fact a the reader would be likely to obtain from any other source , although , assuredly , it have a tinge of romance approach to the marvellous .
* * * * * In one of the apartment of the province-house there be long preserve an ancient picture the frame of which be as black a ebony , and the canvas itself so dark with age , damp and smoke that not a touch of the painter 's art could be discern .
Time have throw an impenetrable veil over it and leave to tradition and fable and conjecture to say what have once be there portray .
During the rule of many successive governor it have hang , by prescriptive and undisputed right , over the mantel piece of the same chamber , and it still keep it place when Lieutenant-governor Hutchinson assume the administration of the province on the departure of Sir Francis Bernard .
The lieutenant-governor sat one afternoon rest his head against the carve back of his stately arm-chair and gaze up thoughtfully at the void blackness of the picture .
It be scarcely a time for such inactive musing , when affair of the deep moment require the ruler 's decision ; for within that very hour Hutchinson have receive intelligence of the arrival of a British fleet bring three regiment from Halifax to overawe the insubordination of the people .
These troop await his permission to occupy the fortress of Castle William and the town itself , yet , instead of affix his signature to an official order , there sit the lieutenant-governor so carefully scrutinize the black waste of canvas that his demeanor attract the notice of two young person who attend him .
One , wear a military dress of buff , be his kinsman , Francis Lincoln , the provincial captain of Castle William ; the other , who sit on a low stool beside his chair , be Alice Vane , his favorite niece .
She be clad entirely in white -- a pale , ethereal creature who , though a native of New England , have be educate abroad and seem not merely a stranger from another clime , but almost a be from another world .
For several year , until leave an orphan , she have dwelt with her father in sunny Italy , and there have acquire a taste and enthusiasm for sculpture and painting which she find few opportunity of gratify in the undecorated dwelling of the colonial gentry .
It be say that the early production of her own pencil exhibit no inferior genius , though perhaps the rude atmosphere of New England have cramp her hand and dim the glowing color of her fancy .
But , observe her uncle 's steadfast gaze , which appear to search through the mist of year to discover the subject of the picture , her curiosity be excite .
`` Is it known , my dear uncle , '' inquire she , `` what this old picture once represent ?
Possibly , could it be make visible , it might prove a masterpiece of some great artist ; else why have it so long hold such a conspicuous place ? ''
As her uncle , contrary to his usual custom -- for he be as attentive to all the humor and caprice of Alice a if she have be his own best-beloved child -- do not immediately reply , the young captain of Castle William take that office upon himself .
`` This dark old square of canvas , my fair cousin , '' say he , `` have be an heirloom in the province-house from time immemorial .
As to the painter , I can tell you nothing ; but if half the story tell of it be true , not one of the great Italian master have ever produce so marvellous a piece of work a that before you . ''
Captain Lincoln proceed to relate some of the strange fable and fantasy which , a it be impossible to refute them by ocular demonstration , have grow to be article of popular belief in reference to this old picture .
One of the wild , and at the same time the best-accredited , account state it to be an original and authentic portrait of the evil one , take at a witch-meeting near Salem , and that it strong and terrible resemblance have be confirm by several of the confessing wizard and witch at their trial in open court .
It be likewise affirm that a familiar spirit or demon abode behind the blackness of the picture , and have show himself at season of public calamity to more than one of the royal governor .
Shirley , for instance , have behold this ominous apparition on the eve of General Abercrombie 's shameful and bloody defeat under the wall of Ticonderoga .
Many of the servant of the province-house have catch glimpse of a visage frown down upon them at morning or evening twilight , or in the depth of night while rake up the fire that glimmer on the hearth beneath , although , if any be , bold enough to hold a torch before the picture , it would appear a black and undistinguishable a ever .
The old inhabitant of Boston recollect that his father -- in whose day the portrait have not wholly fade out of sight -- have once look upon it , but would never suffer himself to be question a to the face which be there represent .
In connection with such story , it be remarkable that over the top of the frame there be some ragged remnant of black silk , indicate that a veil have formerly hang down before the picture until the duskiness of time have so effectually conceal it .
But , after all , it be the most singular part of the affair that so many of the pompous governor of Massachusetts have allow the obliterated picture to remain in the state-chamber of the province-house .
`` Some of these fable be really awful , '' observe Alice Vane , who have occasionally shudder as well a smile while her cousin spoke .
`` It would be almost worth while to wipe away the black surface of the canvas , since the original picture can hardly be so formidable a those which fancy paint instead of it . ''
`` But would it be possible , '' inquire her cousin , '' to restore this dark picture to it pristine hue ? ''
`` Such art be know in Italy , '' say Alice .
The lieutenant-governor have rouse himself from his abstracted mood , and listen with a smile to the conversation of his young relative .
Yet his voice have something peculiar in it tone when he undertake the explanation of the mystery .
`` I be sorry , Alice , to destroy your faith in the legend of which you be so fond , '' remark he , `` but my antiquarian research have long since make me acquainted with the subject of this picture -- if picture it can be call -- which be no more visible , nor ever will be , than the face of the long-buried man whom it once represent .
It be the portrait of Edward Randolph , the founder of this house , a person famous in the history of New England . ''
`` Of that Edward Randolph , '' exclaim Captain Lincoln , `` who obtain the repeal of the first provincial charter , under which our forefather have enjoy almost democratic privilege -- he that be style the arch-enemy of New England , and whose memory be still hold in detestation a the destroyer of our liberty ? ''
`` It be the same Randolph , '' answer Hutchinson , move uneasily in his chair .
`` It be his lot to taste the bitterness of popular odium . ''
`` Our annals tell u , '' continue the captain of Castle William , `` that the curse of the people follow this Randolph where he go and work evil in all the subsequent event of his life , and that it effect be see , likewise , in the manner of his death .
They say , too , that the inward misery of that curse work itself outward and be visible on the wretched man 's countenance , make it too horrible to be look upon .
If so , and if this picture truly represent his aspect , it be in mercy that the cloud of blackness have gather over it . ''
`` These tradition be folly to one who have prove , a I have , how little of historic truth lie at the bottom , '' say the lieutenant-governor .
`` As regard the life and character of Edward Randolph , too implicit credence have be give to Dr. Cotton Mather , who -- I must say it , though some of his blood run in my vein -- have fill our early history with old woman 's tale a fanciful and extravagant a those of Greece or Rome . ''
`` And yet , '' whisper Alice Vane , `` may not such fable have a moral ?
And methinks , if the visage of this portrait be so dreadful , it be not without a cause that it have hang so long in a chamber of the province-house .
When the ruler feel themselves irresponsible , it be well that they should be remind of the awful weight of a people 's curse . ''
The lieutenant-governor started and gaze for a moment at his niece , a if her girlish fantasy have strike upon some feeling in his own breast which all his policy or principle could not entirely subdue .
He know , indeed , that Alice , in spite of her foreign education , retain the native sympathy of a New England girl .
`` Peace , silly child ! ''
cry he , at last , more harshly than he have ever before address the gentle Alice .
`` The rebuke of a king ; be more to be dread than the clamor of a wild , misguided multitude .
-- Captain Lincoln , it be decide : the fortress of Castle William must be occupy by the royal troop .
The two remain regiment shall be billet in the town or encamp upon the Common .
It be time , after year of tumult , and almost rebellion , that His Majesty 's government should have a wall of strength about it . ''
`` Trust , sir -- trust yet a while to the loyalty of the people , '' say Captain Lincoln , `` nor teach them that they can ever be on other term with British soldier than those of brotherhood , a when they fight side by side through the French war .
Do not convert the street of your native town into a camp .
Think twice before you give up old Castle William , the key of the province , into other keep than that of true-born New Englanders . ''
`` Young man , it be decide , '' repeat Hutchinson , rise from his chair .
`` A British officer will be in attendance this evening to receive the necessary instruction for the disposal of the troop .
Your presence also will be require .
Till then , farewell . ''
With these word the lieutenant-governor hastily leave the room , while Alice and her cousin more slowly follow , whisper together , and once pause to glance back at the mysterious picture .
The captain of Castle William fancy that the girl 's air and mien be such a might have belong to one of those spirit of fable -- fairy or creature of a more antique mythology -- who sometimes mingle their agency with mortal affair , half in caprice , yet with a sensibility to human weal or woe .
As he hold the door for her to pass Alice beckon to the picture and smile .
`` Come forth , dark and evil shape ! ''
cry she .
`` It be thine hour . ''
In the evening Lieutenant-governor Hutchinson sit in the same chamber where the foregoing scene have occur , surround by several person whose various interest have summon them together .
There be the selectman of Boston -- plain patriarchal father of the people , excellent representative of the old puritanical founder whose sombre strength have stamp so deep an impress upon the New England character .
Contrasting with these be one or two member of council , richly dress in the white wig , the embroidered waistcoat and other magnificence of the time , and make a somewhat ostentatious display of courtier-like ceremonial .
In attendance , likewise , be a major of the British army , await the lieutenant-governor 's order for the landing of the troop , which still remain on board the transport .
The captain of Castle William stand beside Hutchinson 's chair , with folded arm , glance rather haughtily at the British officer by whom he be soon to be supersede in his command .
On a table in the centre of the chamber stand a branch silver candlestick , throw down the glow of half a dozen waxlights upon a paper apparently ready for the lieutenant-governor 's signature .
Partly shroud in the voluminous fold of one of the window-curtains , which fell from the ceiling to the floor , be see the white drapery of a lady 's robe .
It may appear strange that Alice Vane should have be there at such a time , but there be something so childlike , so wayward , in her singular character , so apart from ordinary rule , that her presence do not surprise the few who notice it .
Meantime , the chairman of the selectman be address to the lieutenant-governor a long and solemn protest against the reception of the British troop into the town .
`` And if Your Honor , '' conclude this excellent but somewhat prosy old gentleman , `` shall see fit to persist in bring these mercenary sworders and musketeer into our quiet street , not on our head be the responsibility .
Think , sir , while there be yet time , that if one drop of blood be shed , that blood shall be an eternal stain upon Your Honor 's memory .
You , sir , have write with an able pen the deed of our forefather ; the more to be desire be it , therefore , that yourself should deserve honorable mention a a true patriot and upright ruler when your own doings shall be write down in history . ''
`` I be not insensible , my good sir , to the natural desire to stand well in the annals of my country , '' reply Hutchinson , control his impatience into courtesy , `` nor know I any good method of attain that end than by withstand the merely temporary spirit of mischief which , with your pardon , seem to have infect old men than myself .
Would you have me wait till the mob shall sack the province-house a they do my private mansion ?
Trust me , sir , the time may come when you will be glad to flee for protection to the king 's banner , the raising of which be now so distasteful to you . ''
`` Yes , '' say the British major , who be impatiently expect the lieutenant-governor 's order .
`` The demagogue of this province have raise the devil , and can not lay him again .
We will exorcise him in God 's name and the king 's . ''
`` If you meddle with the devil , take care of his claw , '' answer the captain of Castle William , stir by the taunt against his countryman .
`` Craving your pardon , young sir , '' say the venerable selectman , `` let not an evil spirit enter into your word .
We will strive against the oppressor with prayer and fasting , a our forefather would have do .
Like them , moreover , we will submit to whatever lot a wise Providence may send u -- always after our own best exertion to amend it . ''
`` And there peep forth the devil 's claw ! ''
mutter Hutchinson , who well understood the nature of Puritan submission .
`` This matter shall be expedite forthwith .
When there shall be a sentinel at every corner and a court of guard before the town-house , a loyal gentleman may venture to walk abroad .
What to me be the outcry of a mob in this remote province of the realm ?
The king be my master , and England be my country ; uphold by their armed strength , I set my foot upon the rabble and defy them . ''
He snatch a pen and be about to affix his signature to the paper that lay on the table , when the captain of Castle William place his hand upon his shoulder .
The freedom of the action , so contrary to the ceremonious respect which be then consider due to rank and dignity , awaken general surprise , and in none more than in the lieutenant-governor himself .
Looking angrily up , he perceive that his young relative be point his finger to the opposite wall .
Hutchinson 's eye follow the signal , and he saw what have hitherto be unobserved -- that a black silk curtain be suspend before the mysterious picture , so as completely to conceal it .
His thought immediately recur to the scene of the precede afternoon , and in his surprise , confuse by indistinct emotion , yet sensible that his niece must have have an agency in this phenomenon , he call loudly upon her : `` Alice !
Come hither , Alice ! ''
No sooner have he spoken than Alice Vane glide from her station , and , press one hand across her eye , with the other snatch away the sable curtain that conceal the portrait .
An exclamation of surprise burst from every beholder , but the lieutenant-governor 's voice have a tone of horror .
`` By Heaven ! ''
say he , in a low inward murmur , speak rather to himself than to those around him ; `` if the spirit of Edward Randolph be to appear among u from the place of torment , he could not wear more of the terror of hell upon his face . ''
`` For some wise end , '' say the age selectman , solemnly , `` hath Providence scatter away the mist of year that have so long hid this dreadful effigy .
Until this hour no living man hath see what we behold . ''
Within the antique frame which so recently have enclose a sable waste of canvas now appear a visible picture-still dark , indeed , in it hue and shading , but thrown forward in strong relief .
It be a half-length figure of a gentleman in a rich but very old-fashioned dress of embroidered velvet , with a broad ruff and a beard , and wear a hat the brim of which overshadow his forehead .
Beneath this cloud the eye have a peculiar glare which be almost lifelike .
The whole portrait start so distinctly out of the background that it have the effect of a person look down from the wall at the astonished and awe-stricken spectator .
The expression of the face , if any word can convey an idea of it , be that of a wretch detect in some hideous guilt and expose to the bitter hatred and laughter and wither scorn of a vast surrounding multitude .
There be the struggle of defiance , beat down and overwhelm by the crush weight of ignominy .
The torture of the soul have come forth upon the countenance .
It seem a if the picture , while hide behind the cloud of immemorial year , have be all the time acquire an intenser depth and darkness of expression , till now it gloomed forth again and throw it evil omen over the present hour .
Such , if the wild legend may be credit , be the portrait of Edward Randolph a he appear when a people 's curse have work it influence upon his nature . ''
` Twould drive me mad , that awful face , '' say Hutchinson , who seem fascinate by the contemplation of it .
`` Be warn , then , '' whisper Alice .
`` He trample on a people 's right .
Behold his punishment , and avoid a crime like his . ''
The lieutenant-governor actually tremble for an instant , but , exert his energy -- which be not , however , his most characteristic feature -- he strive to shake off the spell of Randolph 's countenance .
`` Girl , '' cry he , laugh bitterly , a he turn to Alice , `` have you bring hither your painter 's art , your Italian spirit of intrigue , your trick of stage-effect , and think to influence the council of ruler and the affair of nation by such shallow contrivance ?
See here ! ''
`` Stay yet a while , '' say the selectman a Hutchinson again snatch the pen ; `` for if ever mortal man receive a warning from a tormented soul , Your Honor be that man . ''
`` Away ! ''
answered Hutchinson , fiercely .
`` Though yonder senseless picture cry ` Forbear ! '
it should not move me ! ''
Casting a scowl of defiance at the pictured face -- which seem at that moment to intensify the horror of it miserable and wicked look -- he scrawl on the paper , in character that betoken it a deed of desperation , the name of Thomas Hutchinson .
Then , it be say , he shudder , a if that signature have grant away his salvation .
`` It be do , '' say he , and place his hand upon his brow .
`` May Heaven forgive the deed ! ''
say the soft , sad accent of Alice Vane , like the voice of a good spirit flit away .
When morning come , there be a stifled whisper through the household , and spread thence about the town , that the dark mysterious picture have start from the wall and spoken face to face with Lieutenant-governor Hutchinson .
If such a miracle have be work , however , no trace of it remain behind ; for within the antique frame nothing could be discern save the impenetrable cloud which have cover the canvas since the memory of man .
If the figure have , indeed , step forth , it have flee back , spirit-like , at the day-dawn , and hide itself behind a century 's obscurity .
The truth probably be that Alice Vane 's secret for restore the hue of the picture have merely effect a temporary renovation .
But those who in that brief interval have behold the awful visage of Edward Randolph desire no second glance , and ever afterward tremble at the recollection of the scene , a if an evil spirit have appear visibly among them .
And , a for Hutchinson , when , far over the ocean , his dying-hour drew on , he gasp for breath and complain that he be choke with the blood of the Boston Massacre , and Francis Lincoln , the former captain of Castle William , who be stand at his bedside , perceive a likeness in his frenzied look to that of Edward Randolph .
Did his broken spirit feel at that dread hour the tremendous burden of a people 's curse ?
* * * * * At the conclusion of this miraculous legend I inquire of mine host whether the picture still remain in the chamber over our head , but Mr. Tiffany inform me that it have long since be remove , and be suppose to be hide in some out-of-the-way corner of the New England Museum .
Perchance some curious antiquary may light upon it there , and , with the assistance of Mr. Howorth , the picture-cleaner , may supply a not unnecessary proof of the authenticity of the fact here set down .
During the progress of the story a storm have be gather abroad and rag and rattle so loudly in the upper region of the Province House that it seem a if all the old governor and great men be run riot above stair while Mr. Bela Tiffany babble of them below .
In the course of generation , when many people have live and die in an ancient house , the whistling of the wind through it cranny and the creaking of it beam and rafter become strangely like the tone of the human voice , or thunder laughter , or heavy footstep tread the deserted chamber .
It be a if the echo of half a century be revive .
Such be the ghostly sound that roar and murmur in our ear when I take leave of the circle round the fireside of the Province House and , plunge down the doorstep , fight my way homeward against a drifting snow-storm .
III .
LADY ELEANORE 'S MANTLE .
Mine excellent friend the landlord of the Province House be please the other evening to invite Mr. Tiffany and myself to an oyster-supper .
This slight mark of respect and gratitude , a he handsomely observe , be far less than the ingenious tale-teller , and I , the humble note-taker of his narrative , have fairly earn by the public notice which our joint lucubration have attract to his establishment .
Many a cigar have be smoke within his premise , many a glass of wine or more potent aqua vit have be quaffed , many a dinner have be eat , by curious stranger who , save for the fortunate conjunction of Mr. Tiffany and me , would never have venture through that darksome avenue which give access to the historic precinct of the Province House .
In short , if any credit be due to the courteous assurance of Mr. Thomas Waite , we have bring his forgotten mansion almost a effectually into public view a if we have throw down the vulgar range of shoe-shop and dry-good store which hide it aristocratic front from Washington street .
It may be unadvisable , however , to speak too loudly of the increased custom of the house , lest Mr. Waite should find it difficult to renew the lease on so favorable term a heretofore .
Being thus welcome a benefactor , neither Mr. Tiffany nor myself felt any scruple in do full justice to the good thing that be set before u .
If the feast be less magnificent than those same panelled wall have witness in a bygone century ; if mine host preside with somewhat less of state than might have befit a successor of the royal governor ; if the guest make a less impose show than the bewigged and powder and embroider dignitary who erst banquet at the gubernatorial table and now sleep within their armorial tomb on Copp 's Hill or round King 's Chapel , -- yet never , I may boldly say , do a more comfortable little party assemble in the province-house from Queen Anne 's day to the Revolution .
The occasion be render more interesting by the presence of a venerable personage whose own actual reminiscence go back to the epoch of Gage and Howe , and even supply him with a doubtful anecdote or two of Hutchinson .
He be one of that small , and now all but extinguish , class whose attachment to royalty , and to the colonial institution and custom that be connect with it , have never yield to the democratic heresy of after-times .
The young queen of Britain have not a more loyal subject in her realm -- perhaps not one who would kneel before her throne with such reverential love -- a this old grandsire whose head have whiten beneath the mild sway of the republic which still in his mellower moment he term a usurpation .
Yet prejudice so obstinate have not make him an ungentle or impracticable companion .
If the truth must be tell , the life of the age loyalist have be of such a scrambling and unsettled character -- he have have so little choice of friend and be so often destitute of any -- that I doubt whether he would refuse a cup of kindness with either Oliver Cromwell or John Hancock , to say nothing of any democrat now upon the stage .
In another paper of this series I may perhaps give the reader a close glimpse of his portrait .
Our host in due season uncork a bottle of Madeira of such exquisite perfume and admirable flavor that he surely must have discover it in an ancient bin down deep beneath the deep cellar where some jolly old butler store away the governor 's choice wine and forget to reveal the secret on his death-bed .
Peace to his red-nosed ghost and a libation to his memory !
This precious liquor be imbibe by Mr. Tiffany with peculiar zest , and after sip the third glass it be his pleasure to give u one of the odd legend which he have yet rake from the storehouse where he keep such matter .
With some suitable adornment from my own fancy , it run pretty much a follow .
* * * * * Not long after Colonel Shute have assume the government of Massachusetts Bay -- now nearly a hundred and twenty year ago -- a young lady of rank and fortune arrive from England to claim his protection a her guardian .
He be her distant relative , but the near who have survive the gradual extinction of her family ; so that no more eligible shelter could be find for the rich and high-born Lady Eleanore Rochcliffe than within the province-house of a Transatlantic colony .
The consort of Governor Shute , moreover , have be a a mother to her childhood , and be now anxious to receive her in the hope that a beautiful young woman would be expose to infinitely less peril from the primitive society of New England than amid the artifice and corruption of a court .
If either the governor or his lady have especially consult their own comfort , they would probably have seek to devolve the responsibility on other hand , since with some noble and splendid trait of character Lady Eleanore be remarkable for a harsh , unyielding pride , a haughty consciousness of her hereditary and personal advantage , which make her almost incapable of control .
Judging from many traditionary anecdote , this peculiar temper be hardly less than a monomania ; or if the act which it inspire be those of a sane person , it seem due from Providence that pride so sinful should be follow by a severe a retribution .
That tinge of the marvellous which be throw over so many of these half-forgotten legend have probably impart an additional wildness to the strange story of Lady Eleanore Rochcliffe .
The ship in which she come passenger have arrive at Newport , whence Lady Eleanore be convey to Boston in the governor 's coach , attend by a small escort of gentleman on horseback .
The ponderous equipage , with it four black horse , attract much notice a it rumble through Cornhill surround by the prance steed of half a dozen cavalier with sword dangle to their stirrup and pistol at their holster .
Through the large glass window of the coach , a it roll along , the people could discern the figure of Lady Eleanore , strangely combine an almost queenly stateliness with the grace and beauty of a maiden in her teen .
A singular tale have go abroad among the lady of the province that their fair rival be indebted for much of the irresistible charm of her appearance to a certain article of dress -- an embroidered mantle -- which have be work by the most skilful artist in London , and possess even magical property of adornment .
On the present occasion , however , she owe nothing to the witchery of dress , be clothe in a riding-habit of velvet which would have appear stiff and ungraceful on any other form .
The coachman rein in his four black steed , and the whole cavalcade come to a pause in front of the contorted iron balustrade that fence the province-house from the public street .
It be an awkward coincidence that the bell of the Old South be just then toll for a funeral ; so that , instead of a gladsome peal with which it be customary to announce the arrival of distinguished stranger , Lady Eleanore Rochcliffe be usher by a doleful clang , a if calamity have come embody in her beautiful person .
`` A very great disrespect ! ''
exclaim Captain Langford , an English officer who have recently bring despatch to Governor Shute .
`` The funeral should have be defer l Lady Eleanore 's spirit be affect by such a dismal welcome . ''
`` With your pardon , sir , '' reply Dr. Clarke , a physician and a famous champion of the popular party , `` whatever the herald may pretend , a dead beggar must have precedence of a living queen .
King Death confers high privilege . ''
These remarks-were interchanged while the speaker wait a passage through the crowd which have gather on each side of the gateway , leave an open avenue to the portal of the province-house .
A black slave in livery now leap from behind the coach and throw open the door , while at the same moment Governor Shute descend the flight of step from his mansion to assist Lady Eleanore in alight .
But the governor 's stately approach be anticipate in a manner that excite general astonishment .
A pale young man with his black hair all in disorder rush from the throng and prostrate himself beside the coach , thus offer his person a a footstool for Lady Eleanore Rochcliffe to tread upon .
She hold back an instant , yet with an expression a if doubt whether the young man be worthy to bear the weight of her footstep rather than dissatisfy to receive such awful reverence from a fellow-mortal .
`` Up , sir ! ''
say the governor , sternly , at the same time lift his cane over the intruder .
`` What mean the Bedlamite by this freak ? ''
`` Nay , '' answer Lady Eleanore , playfully , but with more scorn than pity in her tone ; `` Your Excellency shall not strike him .
When men seek only to be trample upon , it be a pity to deny them a favor so easily grant -- and so well deserved ! ''
Then , though as lightly a a sunbeam on a cloud , she place her foot upon the cower form and extend her hand to meet that of the governor .
There be a brief interval during which Lady Eleanore retain this attitude , and never , surely , be there an apter emblem of aristocracy and hereditary pride trample on human sympathy and the kindred of nature than these two figure present at that moment .
Yet the spectator be so smitten with her beauty , and so essential do pride seem to the existence of such a creature , that they give a simultaneous acclamation of applause .
`` Who be this insolent young fellow ? ''
inquired Captain Langford , who still remain beside Dr. Clarke .
`` If he be in his sens , his impertinence demand the bastinado ; if mad , Lady Eleanore should be secure from further inconvenience by his confinement . ''
`` His name be Jervase Helwyse , '' answer the doctor -- `` a youth of no birth or fortune , or other advantage save the mind and soul that nature give him ; and , be secretary to our colonial agent in London , it be his misfortune to meet this Lady Eleanore Rochcliffe .
He love her , and her scorn have drive him mad . ''
`` He be mad so to aspire , '' observe the English officer .
`` It may be so , '' say Dr. Clarke , frown a he speak ; `` but I tell you , sir , I could wellnigh doubt the justice of the Heaven above u if no signal humiliation overtake this lady who now tread so haughtily into yonder mansion .
She seek to place herself above the sympathy of our common nature , which envelop all human soul ; see if that nature do not assert it claim over her in some mode that shall bring her level with the low . ''
`` Never ! ''
cry Captain Langford , indignantly -- `` neither in life nor when they lay her with her ancestor . ''
Not many day afterward the governor give a ball in honor of Lady Eleanore Rochcliffe .
The principal gentry of the colony receive invitation , which be distribute to their residence far and near by messenger on horseback bearing missive seal with all the formality of official despatch .
In obedience to the summons , there be a general gathering of rank , wealth and beauty , and the wide door of the province-house have seldom give admittance to more numerous and honorable guest than on the evening of Lady Eleanore 's ball .
Without much extravagance of eulogy , the spectacle might even be term splendid , for , accord to the fashion of the time , the lady shine in rich silk and satin outspread over wide-projecting hoop , and the gentleman glitter in gold embroidery laid unsparingly upon the purple or scarlet or sky-blue velvet which be the material of their coat and waistcoat .
The latter article of dress be of great importance , since it envelop the wearer 's body nearly to the knee and be perhaps bedizen with the amount of his whole year 's income in golden flower and foliage .
The altered taste of the present day -- a taste symbolic of a deep change in the whole system of society -- would look upon almost any of those gorgeous figure a ridiculous , although that even the guest seek their reflection in the pier-glasses and rejoice to catch their own glitter amid the glittering crowd .
What a pity that one of the stately mirror have not preserve a picture of the scene which by the very traits that be so transitory might have teach u much that would be worth know and remember !

The ball at the province-house supply a topic of conversation for the colonial metropolis for some day after it occurrence , and might still longer have be the general theme , only that a subject of all-engrossing interest thrust it for a time from the public recollection .
This be the appearance of a dreadful epidemic which in that age , and long before and afterward , be wont to slay it hundred and thousand on both side of the Atlantic .
On the occasion of which we speak it be distinguish by a peculiar virulence , insomuch that it have leave it trace -- it pitmarks , to use an appropriate figure -- on the history of the country , the affair of which be throw into confusion by it ravage .

It be notice with an ungenerous bitterness of feeling that the four gentleman -- the Virginian , the British officer , the young clergyman and the governor 's secretary -- who have be her most devoted attendant on the evening of the ball be the foremost on whom the plague-stroke fell .
But the disease , pursue it onward progress , soon cease to be exclusively a prerogative of aristocracy .
Its red brand be no longer confer like a noble 's star or an order of knighthood .
It thread it way through the narrow and crooked street , and enter the low , mean , darksome dwelling and lay it hand of death upon the artisan and labor class of the town .
It compel rich and poor to feel themselves brother then , and stalk to and fro across the Three Hills with a fierceness which make it almost a new pestilence , there be that mighty conqueror -- that scourge and horror of our forefather -- the small-pox .
We can not estimate the affright which this plague inspire of yore by contemplate it a the fangless monster of the present day .
We must remember , rather , with what awe we watch the gigantic footstep of the Asiatic cholera stride from shore to shore of the Atlantic and march like Destiny upon city far remote which flight have already half depopulate .
There be no other fear so horrible and unhumanizing a that which make man dread to breathe heaven 's vital air l it be poison , or to grasp the hand of a brother or friend lest the grip of the pestilence should clutch him .

This conqueror have a symbol of his triumph : it be a blood-red flag that flutter in the tainted air over the door of every dwelling into which the small-pox have enter .

One day in the midst of these dismal time a wild figure approach the portal of the province-house , and , fold his arm , stand contemplate the scarlet banner , which a passing breeze shook fitfully , a if to fling abroad the contagion that it typify .

exclaim Shute , extend his cane to guard himself from contact .
`` There be nothing here but Death ; back , or you will meet him . ''

-- On , fool , to your own destruction ! ''

Approach , then .

princess !

A remarkable uncertainty brood over that unhappy lady 's fate .
There be a belief , however , that in a certain chamber of this mansion a female form may sometimes be duskily discern shrink into the dark corner and muffle her face within an embroidered mantle .
Supposing the legend true , can this be other than the once proud Lady Eleanore ?
* * * * * Mine host and the old loyalist and I bestow no little Warmth of applause upon this narrative , in which we have all be deeply interested ; for the reader can scarcely conceive how unspeakably the effect of such a tale be heighten when , a in the present case , we may repose perfect confidence in the veracity of him who tell it .
For my own part , know how scrupulous be Mr. Tiffany to settle the foundation of his fact , I could not have believe him one whit the more faithfully have he profess himself an eyewitness of the doings and suffering of poor Lady Eleanore .
Some sceptic , it be true , might demand documentary evidence , or even require him to produce the embroidered mantle , forget that -- Heaven be praise !
-- it be consume to ash .
But now the old loyalist , whose blood be warm by the good cheer , begin to talk , in his turn , about the tradition of the Province House , and hint that he , if it be agreeable , might add a few reminiscence to our legendary stock .

Perchance the public -- but be this a it own caprice and ours shall settle the matter -- may read the result in another tale of the Province House .
IV .
OLD ESTHER DUDLEY .
Our host have resume the chair , he as well a Mr. Tiffany and myself express much eagerness to be make acquaint with the story to which the loyalist have allude .
That venerable man first of all saw light to moisten his throat with another glass of wine , and then , turn his face toward our coal-fire , look steadfastly for a few moment into the depth of it cheerful glow .
Finally he pour forth a great fluency of speech .
The generous liquid that he have imbibe , while it warm his age-chilled blood , likewise take off the chill from his heart and mind , and give him an energy to think and feel which we could hardly have expect to find beneath the snow of fourscore winter .
His feeling , indeed , appear to me more excitable than those of a young man -- or , at least , the same degree of feeling manifest itself by more visible effect than if his judgment and will have possess the potency of meridian life .
At the pathetic passage of his narrative he readily melt into tear .
When a breath of indignation sweep across his spirit , the blood flush his withered visage even to the root of his white hair , and he shake his clinched fist at the trio of peaceful auditor , seem to fancy enemy in those who felt very kindly toward the desolate old soul .
But ever and anon , sometimes in the midst of his most earnest talk , this ancient person 's intellect would wander vaguely , lose it hold of the matter in hand and groping for it amid misty shadow .
Then would he cackle forth a feeble laugh and express a doubt whether his wit -- for by that phrase it please our ancient friend to signify his mental power -- be not get a little the bad for wear .
Under these disadvantage , the old loyalist 's story require more revision to render it fit for the public eye than those of the series which have precede it ; nor should it be conceal that the sentiment and tone of the affair may have undergone some slight -- or perchance more than slight -- metamorphosis in it transmission to the reader through the medium of a thoroughgoing democrat .
The tale itself be a mere sketch with no involution of plot nor any great interest of event , yet possess , if I have rehearse it aright , that pensive influence over the mind which the shadow of the old Province House fling upon the loiterer in it court-yard .
* * * * * The hour have come -- the hour of defeat and humiliation -- when Sir William Howe be to pass over the threshold of the province-house and embark , with no such triumphal ceremony a he once promise himself , on board the British fleet .
He bid his servant and military attendant go before him , and linger a moment in the loneliness of the mansion to quell the fierce emotion that struggle in his bosom a with a death-throb .
Preferable then would he have deem his fate have a warrior 's death leave him a claim to the narrow territory of a grave within the soil which the king have give him to defend .
With an ominous perception that a his depart footstep echoed adown the staircase the sway of Britain be pass for ever from New England , he smite his clenched hand on his brow and curse the destiny that have fling the shame of a dismembered empire upon him .
`` Would to God , '' cry he , hardly repress his tear of rage , `` that the rebel be even now at the doorstep !
A blood-stain upon the floor should then bear testimony that the last British ruler be faithful to his trust . ''
The tremulous voice of a woman reply to his exclamation .
`` Heaven 's cause and the king 's be one , '' it say .
`` Go forth , Sir William Howe , and trust in Heaven to bring back a royal governor in triumph . ''
Subduing at once the passion to which he have yield only in the faith that it be unwitnessed , Sir William Howe become conscious that an aged woman lean on a gold-headed staff be stand betwixt him and the door .
It be old Esther Dudley , who have dwell almost immemorial year in this mansion , until her presence seem a inseparable from it a the recollection of it history .
She be the daughter of an ancient and once eminent family which have fall into poverty and decay and leave it last descendant no resource save the bounty of the king , nor any shelter except within the wall of the province-house .
An office in the household with merely nominal duty have be assign to her a a pretext for the payment of a small pension , the great part of which she expend in adorn herself with an antique magnificence of attire .
The claim of Esther Dudley 's gentle blood be acknowledge by all the successive governor , and they treat her with the punctilious courtesy which it be her foible to demand , not always with success , from a neglectful world .
The only actual share which she assume in the business of the mansion be to glide through it passage and public chamber late at night to see that the servant have drop no fire from their flaring torch nor left ember crackle and blaze on the hearth .
Perhaps it be this invariable custom of walk her round in the hush of midnight that cause the superstition of the time to invest the old woman with attribute of awe and mystery , fabling that she have enter the portal of the province-house -- none know whence -- in the train of the first royal governor , and that it be her fate to dwell there till the last should have depart .
But Sir William Howe , if he ever hear this legend , have forget it .
`` Mistress Dudley , why be you loiter here ? ''
ask he , with some severity of tone .
`` It be my pleasure to be the last in this mansion of the king . ''
`` Not so , if it please Your Excellency , '' answer the time-stricken woman .
`` This roof have shelter me long ; I will not pass from it until they bear me to the tomb of my forefather .
What other shelter be there for old Esther Dudley save the province-house or the grave ? ''
`` Now , Heaven forgive me ! ''
say Sir William Howe to himself .
`` I be about to leave this wretched old creature to starve or beg .
-- Take this , good Mistress Dudley , '' he add , put a purse into her hand .
`` King George 's head on these golden guinea be sterling yet , and will continue so , I warrant you , even should the rebel crown John Hancock their king .
That purse will buy a good shelter than the province-house can now afford . ''
`` While the burden of life remain upon me I will have no other shelter than this roof , '' persist Esther Dudley , strike her stuff upon the floor with a gesture that express immovable resolve ; `` and when Your Excellency return in triumph , I will totter into the porch to welcome you . ''
`` My poor old friend ! ''
answer the British general , and all his manly and martial pride could no longer restrain a gush of bitter tear .
`` This be an evil hour for you and me .
The province which the king entrust to my charge be lose .
I go hence in misfortune -- perchance in disgrace -- to return no more .
And you , whose present be be incorporate with the past , who have see governor after governor in stately pageantry ascend these step , whose whole life have be an observance of majestic ceremony and a worship of the king , -- how will you endure the change ?
Come with u ; bid farewell to a land that have shake off it allegiance , and live still under a royal government at Halifax . ''
`` Never !
never ! ''
say the pertinacious old dame .
`` Here will I abide , and King George shall still have one true subject in his disloyal province . ''
`` Beshrew the old fool ! ''
mutter Sir William Howe , grow impatient of her obstinacy and ashamed of the emotion into which he have be betray .
`` She be the very moral of old-fashioned prejudice , and could exist nowhere but in this musty edifice .
-- Well , then , Mistress Dudley , since you will need tarry , I give the province-house in charge to you .
Take this key , and keep it safe until myself or some other royal governor shall demand it of you . ''
Smiling bitterly at himself and her , he take the heavy key of the province-house , and , deliver it into the old lady 's hand , draw his clonk around him for departure .
As the general glance back at Esther Dudley 's antique figure he deem her well fit for such a charge , a be so perfect a representative of the decayed past -- of an age go by , with it manner , opinion , faith and feeling all fall into oblivion or scorn , of what have once be a reality , but be now merely a vision of faded magnificence .
Then Sir William Howe strode forth , smite his clenched hand together in the fierce anguish of his spirit , and old Esther Dudley be leave to keep watch in the lonely province-house , dwell there with Memory ; and if Hope ever seem to flit around her , still it be Memory in disguise .
The total change of affair that ensue on the departure of the British troop do not drive the venerable lady from her stronghold .
There be not for many year afterward a governor of Massachusetts , and the magistrate who have charge of such matter saw no objection to Esther Dudley 's residence in the province-house , especially a they must otherwise have pay a hireling for take care of the premise , which with her be a labor of love ; and so they leave her the undisturbed mistress of the old historic edifice .
Many and strange be the fable which the gossip whisper about her in all the chimney-corners of the town .
Among the time-worn article of furniture that have be leave in the mansion , there be a tall antique mirror which be well worthy of a tale by itself , and perhaps may hereafter be the theme of one .
The gold of it heavily-wrought frame be tarnish , and it surface so blur that the old woman 's figure , whenever she pause before it , look indistinct and ghostlike .
But it be the general belief that Esther could cause the governor of the overthrown dynasty , with the beautiful lady who have once adorn their festival , the Indian chief who have come up to the province-house to hold council or swear allegiance , the grim provincial warrior , the severe clergyman -- in short , all the pageantry of go day , all the figure that ever sweep across the broad-plate of glass in former time , -- she could cause the whole to reappear and people the inner world of the mirror with shadow of old life .
Such legend a these , together with the singularity of her isolated existence , her age and the infirmity that each add winter flung upon her , make Mistress Dudley the object both of fear and pity , and it be partly the result of either sentiment that , amid all the angry license of the time , neither wrong nor insult ever fell upon her unprotected head .
Indeed , there be so much haughtiness in her demeanor toward intruder -- among whom she reckon all person act under the new authority -- that it be really an affair of no small nerve to look her in the face .
And , to do the people justice , stern republican a they have now become , they be well content that the old gentlewoman , in her hoop-petticoat and fade embroidery , should still haunt the palace of ruined pride and overthrown power , the symbol of a departed system , embody a history in her person .
So Esther Dudley dwell year after year in the province-house , still reverence all that others have fling aside , still faithful to her king , who , so long a the venerable dame yet hold her post , might be say to retain one true subject in New England and one spot of the empire that have be wrest from him .
And do she dwell there in utter loneliness ?
Rumor say , `` Not so . ''
Whenever her chill and wither heart desire warmth , she be wont to summon a black slave of Governor Shirley 's from the blur mirror and send him in search of guest who have long ago be familiar in those deserted chamber .
Forth go the sable messenger , with the starlight or the moonshine gleam through him , and do his errand in the burial-grounds , knock at the iron door of tomb or upon the marble slab that cover them , and whisper to those within , `` My mistress , old Esther Dudley , bid you to the province-house at midnight ; '' and punctually a the clock of the Old South tell twelve come the shadow of the Olivers , the Hutchinsons , the Dudleys -- all the grandee of a bygone generation -- glide beneath the portal into the well-known mansion , where Esther mingle with them a if she likewise be a shade .
Without vouch for the truth of such tradition , it be certain that Mistress Dudley sometimes assemble a few of the stanch though crestfallen old Tories who have linger in the rebel town during those day of wrath and tribulation .
Out of a cobwebbed bottle contain liquor that a royal governor might have smack his lip over they quaff health to the king and babble treason to the republic , feeling a if the protect shadow of the throne be still fling around them .
But , drain the last drop of their liquor , they steal timorously homeward , and answer not again if the rude mob revile them in the street .
Yet Esther Dudley 's most frequent and favored guest be the child of the town .
Toward them she be never stern .
A kindly and loving nature hinder elsewhere from it free course by a thousand rocky prejudice lavish itself upon these little one .
By bribe of gingerbread of her own making , stamp with a royal crown , she tempt their sunny sportiveness beneath the gloomy portal of the province-house , and would often beguile them to spend a whole play-day there , sit in a circle round the verge of her hoop-petticoat , greedily attentive to her story of a dead world .
And when these little boy and girl steal forth again from the dark , mysterious mansion , they go bewilder , full of old feeling that graver people have long ago forget , rub their eye at the world around them a if they have go astray into ancient time and become child of the past .
At home , when their parent ask where they have loiter such a weary while and with whom they have be at play , the child would talk of all the departed worthy of the province a far back a Governor Belcher and the haughty dame of Sir William Phipps .
It would seem a though they have be sit on the knee of these famous personage , whom the grave have hide for half a century , and have toy with the embroidery of their rich waistcoat or roguishly pull the long curl of their flowing wig .
`` But Governor Belcher have be dead this many a year , '' would the mother say to her little boy .
`` And do you really see him at the province-house ? ''
-- `` Oh yes , dear mother -- yes ! ''
the half-dreaming child would answer .
`` But when old Esther have do speak about him , he fade away out of his chair . ''
Thus , without affright her little guest , she lead them by the hand into the chamber of her own desolate heart and make childhood 's fancy discern the ghost that haunt there .
Living so continually in her own circle of idea , and never regulate her mind by a proper reference to present thing , Esther Dudley appear to have grow partially craze .
It be find that she have no right sense of the progress and true state of the Revolutionary war , but hold a constant faith that the army of Britain be victorious on every field and destine to be ultimately triumphant .
Whenever the town rejoice for a battle won by Washington or Gates or Morgan or Greene , the news , in pass through the door of the province-house a through the ivory gate of dream , become metamorphose into a strange tale of the prowess of Howe , Clinton or Cornwallis .
Sooner or later , it be her invincible belief , the colony would be prostrate at the footstool of the king .
Sometimes she seem to take for grant that such be already the case .
On one occasion she startle the townspeople by a brilliant illumination of the province-house with candle at every pane of glass and a transparency of the king 's initial and a crown of light in the great balcony-window .
The figure of the age woman in the most gorgeous of her mildewed velvet and brocade be see pass from casement to casement , until she pause before the balcony and flourish a huge key above her head .
Her wrinkled visage actually gleam with triumph , a if the soul within her be a festal lamp .
`` What mean this blaze of light ?
What do old Esther 's joy portend ? ''
whisper a spectator .
`` It be frightful to , see her glide about the chamber and rejoice there without a soul to bear her company . ''
`` It be a if she be make merry in a tomb , '' say another .
`` Pshaw !
It be no such mystery , '' observe an old man , after some brief exercise of memory .
`` Mistress Dudley be keep jubilee for the king of England 's birthday . ''
Then the people laugh aloud , and would have throw mud against the blazing transparency of the king 's crown and initial , only that they pity the poor old dame who be so dismally triumphant amid the wreck and ruin of the system to which she appertain .
Oftentimes it be her custom to climb the weary staircase that wind upward to the cupola , and thence strain her dimmed eyesight seaward and countryward , watch for a British fleet or for the march of a grand procession with the king 's banner floating over it .
The passenger in the street below would discern her anxious visage and send up a shout : `` When the golden Indian on the province-house shall shoot his arrow , and when the cock on the Old South spire shall crow , then look for a royal governor again ! ''
for this have grow a by-word through the town .
And at last , after long , long year , old Esther Dudley know -- or perchance she only dream -- that a royal governor be on the eve of return to the province-house to receive the heavy key which Sir William Howe have commit to her charge .
Now , it be the fact that intelligence bear some faint analogy to Esther 's version of it be current among the townspeople .
She set the mansion in the best order that her mean allow , and , array herself in silk and tarnish gold , stand long before the blurred mirror to admire her own magnificence .
As she gaze the gray and wither lady move her ashen lip , murmur half aloud , talk to shape that she saw within the mirror , to shadow of her own fantasy , to the household friend of memory , and bid them rejoice with her and come forth to meet the governor .
And while absorb in this communion Mistress Dudley hear the tramp of many footstep in the street , and , look out at the window , behold what she construe a the royal governor 's arrival .
`` Oh , happy day !
Oh , bless , bless hour ! ''
she exclaim .
`` Let me but bid him welcome within the portal , and my task in the province-house and on earth be do . ''
Then , with totter foot which age and tremulous joy cause to tread amiss , she hurry down the grand staircase , her silk sweeping and rustle a she go ; so that the sound be a if a train of special courtier be throng from the dim mirror .
And Esther Dudley fancy that as soon a the wide door should be fling open all the pomp and splendor of bygone time would pace majestically into the province-house and the gilded tapestry of the past would be brighten by the sunshine of the present .
She turn the key , withdraw it from the lock , unclosed the door and step across the threshold .
Advancing up the court-yard appear a person of most dignified mien , with token , a Esther interpret them , of gentle blood , high rank and long-accustomed authority even in his walk and every gesture .
He be richly dress , but wear a gouty shoe , which , however , do not lessen the stateliness of his gait .
Around and behind him be people in plain civic dress and two or three war-worn veteran -- evidently officer of rank -- array in a uniform of blue and buff .
But Esther Dudley , firm in the belief that have fasten it root about her heart , behold only the principal personage , and never doubt that this be the long-looked-for governor to whom she be to surrender up her charge .
As he approach she involuntarily sink down on her knee and tremblingly hold forth the heavy key .
`` Receive my trust !
Take it quickly , '' cry she , `` for methinks Death be strive to snatch away my triumph .
But he conies too late .
Thank Heaven for this blessed hour !
God save King George ! ''
`` That , madam , be a strange prayer to be offer up at such a moment , '' reply the unknown guest of the province-house , and , courteously remove his hat , he offer his arm to raise the aged woman .
`` Yet , in reverence for your gray hair and long-kept faith , Heaven forbid that any here should say you nay .
Over the realm which still acknowledge his sceptre , God save King George ! ''
Esther Dudley start to her foot , and , hastily clutch back the key , gaze with fearful earnestness at the stranger , and dimly and doubtfully , a if suddenly awaken from a dream , her bewildered eye half recognize his face .
Years ago she have know him among the gentry of the province , but the ban of the king have fall upon him .
How , then , come the doomed victim here ?
Proscribed , exclude from mercy , the monarch 's most dreaded and hated foe , this New England merchant have stand triumphantly against a kingdom 's strength , and his foot now tread upon humble royalty a he ascend the step of the province-house , the people 's choose governor of Massachusetts .
`` Wretch , wretch that I be ! ''
mutter the old woman , with such a heartbroken expression that the tear gush from the stranger 's eye .
`` Have I bid a traitor welcome ?
-- Come , Death !
come quickly ! ''
`` Alas , venerable lady ! ''
say Governor Hancock , lend her his support with all the reverence that a courtier would have show to a queen , `` your life have be prolong until the world have change around you .
You have treasure up all that time have render worthless -- the principle , feeling , manner , mode of be and act which another generation have fling aside -- and you be a symbol of the past .
And I and these around me -- we represent a new race of men , live no longer in the past , scarcely in the present , but project our life forward into the future .
Ceasing to model ourselves on ancestral superstition , it be our faith and principle to press onward -- onward .
-- Yet , '' continue he , turn to his attendant , `` let u reverence for the last time the stately and gorgeous prejudice of the tottering past . ''
While the republican governor speak he have continue to support the helpless form of Esther Dudley ; her weight grow heavy against his arm , but at last , with a sudden effort to free herself , the ancient woman sink down beside one of the pillar of the portal .
The key of the province-house fell from her grasp and clank against the stone .
`` I have be faithful unto death , '' murmur she .
`` God save the king ! ''
`` She hath do her office , '' say Hancock , solemnly .
`` We will follow her reverently to the tomb of her ancestor , and then , my fellow-citizens , onward -- onward .
We be no longer child of the past . ''
As the old loyalist conclude his narrative the enthusiasm which have be fitfully flash within his sunken eye and quiver across his wrinkled visage fade away , a if all the linger fire of his soul be extinguish .
Just then , too , a lamp upon the mantelpiece throw out a die gleam , which vanish as speedily a it shoot upward , compel our eye to grope for one another 's feature by the dim glow of the hearth .
With such a lingering fire , methought , with such a die gleam , have the glory of the ancient system vanish from the province-house when the spirit of old Esther Dudley take it flight .
And now , again , the clock of the Old South throw it voice of age on the breeze , knolling the hourly knell of the past , cry out far and wide through the multitudinous city , and fill our ear , a we sit in the dusky chamber , with it reverberate depth of tone .
In that same mansion -- in that very chamber -- what a volume of history have be tell off into hour by the same voice that be now tremble in the air !
Many a governor have hear those midnight accent and long to exchange his stately care for slumber .
And , a for mine host and Mr. Bela Tiffany and the old loyalist and me , we have babble about dream of the past until we almost fancy that the clock be still strike in a bygone century .
Neither of u would have wonder have a hoop-petticoated phantom of Esther Dudley totter into the chamber , walk her round in the hush of midnight a of yore , and motion u to quench the fading ember of the fire and leave the historic precinct to herself and her kindred shade .
But , a no such vision be vouchsafe , I retire unbidden , and would advise Mr. Tiffany to lay hold of another auditor , be resolve not to show my face in the Province House for a good while hence -- if ever .
THE HAUNTED MIND .
What a singular moment be the first one , when you have hardly begin to recollect yourself , after start from midnight slumber !
By unclosing your eye so suddenly you seem to have surprise the personage of your dream in full convocation round your bed , and catch one broad glance at them before they can flit into obscurity .
Or , to vary the metaphor , you find yourself for a single instant wide awake in that realm of illusion whither sleep have be the passport , and behold it ghostly inhabitant and wondrous scenery with a perception of their strangeness such a you never attain while the dream be undisturbed .
The distant sound of a church-clock be borne faintly on the wind .
You question with yourself , half seriously , whether it have steal to your wake ear from some gray tower that stand within the precinct of your dream .
While yet in suspense another clock fling it heavy clang over the slumber town with so full and distinct a sound , and such a long murmur in the neighboring air , that you be certain it must proceed from the steeple at the near corner ; You count the stroke -- one , two ; and there they cease with a booming sound like the gathering of a third stroke within the bell .
If you could choose an hour of wakefulness out of the whole night , it would be this .
Since your sober bedtime , at eleven , you have have rest enough to take off the pressure of yesterday 's fatigue , while before you , till the sun come from `` Far Cathay '' to brighten your window , there be almost the space of a summer night -- one hour to be spend in thought with the mind 's eye half shut , and two in pleasant dream , and two in that strangest of enjoyment the forgetfulness alike of joy and woe .
The moment of rise belongs to another period of time , and appear so distant that the plunge out of a warm bed into the frosty air can not yet be anticipate with dismay .
Yesterday have already vanish among the shadow of the past ; to-morrow have not yet emerge from the future .
You have find an intermediate space where the business of life do not intrude , where the passing moment lingers and become truly the present ; a spot where Father Time , when he think nobody be watch him , sit down by the wayside to take breath .
Oh that he would fall asleep and let mortal live on without grow old !
Hitherto you have lie perfectly still , because the slight motion would dissipate the fragment of your slumber .
Now , be irrevocably awake , you peep through the half-drawn window-curtain , and observe that the glass be ornament with fanciful device in frost-work , and that each pane present something like a frozen dream .
There will be time enough to trace out the analogy while wait the summons to breakfast .
Seen through the clear portion of the glass where the silvery mountain-peaks of the frost-scenery do not ascend , the most conspicuous object be the steeple , the white spire of which direct you to the wintry lustre of the firmament .
You may almost distinguish the figure on the clock that have just tell the hour .
Such a frosty sky and the snow-covered roof and the long vista of the frozen street , all white , and the distant water harden into rock , might make you shiver even under four blanket and a woollen comforter .
Yet look at that one glorious star !
Its beam be distinguishable from all the rest , and actually cast the shadow of the casement on the bed with a radiance of deep hue than moonlight , though not so accurate an outline .
You sink down and muffle your head in the clothes , shiver all the while , but less from bodily chill than the bare idea of a polar atmosphere .
It be too cold even for the thought to venture abroad .
You speculate on the luxury of wear out a whole existence in bed like an oyster in it shell , content with the sluggish ecstasy of inaction , and drowsily conscious of nothing but delicious warmth such a you now feel again .
Ah !
that idea have bring a hideous one in it train .
You think how the dead be lie in their cold shroud and narrow coffin through the drear winter of the grave , and can not persuade your fancy that they neither shrink nor shiver when the snow be drift over their little hillock and the bitter blast howl against the door of the tomb .
That gloomy thought will collect a gloomy multitude and throw it complexion over your wakeful hour .
In the depth of every heart there be a tomb and a dungeon , though the light , the music and revelry , above may cause u to forget their existence and the buried one or prisoner whom they hide .
But sometimes , and oftenest at midnight , those dark receptacle be flung wide open .
In an hour like this , when the mind have a passive sensibility , but no active strength -- when the imagination be a mirror impart vividness to all idea without the power of select or control them -- then pray that your grief may slumber and the brotherhood of remorse not break their chain .
It be too late .
A funeral train come glide by your bed in which passion and feeling assume bodily shape and thing of the mind become dim spectre to the eye .
