-- Yes , dreamer , but your lonely heart will be the colder for such fancy .
-- Sometimes , too , the Past come back , and find me here , and in her train come face which be gladsome when I know them , yet seem not gladsome now .
Would that my hiding-place be lonely , so that the Past might not find me !
-- Get ye all go , old friend , and let me listen to the murmur of the sea -- a melancholy voice , but less sad than yours .
Of what mystery be it tell ?
Of sink ship and whereabouts they lie ?
Of island afar and undiscovered whose tawny child be unconscious of other island and of continent , and deem the star of heaven their near neighbor ?
Nothing of all this .
What , then ?
Has it talk for so many age and mean nothing all the while ?
No ; for those age find utterance in the sea 's unchanging voice , and warn the listener to withdraw his interest from mortal vicissitude and let the infinite idea of eternity pervade his soul .
This be wisdom , and therefore will I spend the next half-hour in shape little boat of driftwood and launch them on voyage across the cove , with the feather of a sea-gull for a sail .
If the voice of age tell me true , this be as wise an occupation a to build ship of five hundred ton and launch them forth upon the main , bound to `` Far Cathay . ''
Yet how would the merchant sneer at me !
And , after all , can such philosophy be true ?
Methinks I could find a thousand argument against it .
Well , then , let yonder shaggy rock mid-deep in the surf -- see !
he be somewhat wrathful : he rag and roar and foam , -- let that tall rock be my antagonist , and let me exercise my oratory like him of Athens who bandy word with an angry sea and get the victory .
My maiden-speech be a triumphant one , for the gentleman in seaweed have nothing to offer in reply save an immitigable roaring .
His voice , indeed , will be hear a long while after mine be hush .
Once more I shout and the cliff reverberate the sound .
Oh what joy for a shy man to feel himself so solitary that he may lift his voice to it high pitch without hazard of a listener !
-- But hush !
Be silent , my good friend !
Whence come that stifle laughter ?
It be musical , but how should there be such music in my solitude ?
Looking upward , I catch a glimpse of three face peep from the summit of the cliff like angel between me and their native sky .
-- Ah , fair girl !
you may make yourself merry at my eloquence , but it be my turn to smile when I saw your white foot in the pool .
Let u keep each other 's secret .
The sunshine have now pass from my hermitage , except a gleam upon the sand just where it meet the sea .
A crowd of gloomy fantasy will come and haunt me if I tarry longer here in the darken twilight of these gray rock .
This be a dismal place in some mood of the mind .
Climb we , therefore , the precipice , and pause a moment on the brink gaze down into that hollow chamber by the deep where we have be what few can be -- sufficient to our own pastime .
Yes , say the word outright : self-sufficient to our own happiness .
How lonesome look the recess now , and dreary too , like all other spot where happiness have be !
There lie my shadow in the depart sunshine with it head upon the sea .
I will pelt it with pebble .
A hit !
a hit !
I clap my hand in triumph , and see my shadow clap it unreal hand and claim the triumph for itself .
What a simpleton must I have be all day , since my own shadow make a mock of my foolery !
Homeward !
homeward !
It be time to hasten home .
It be time -- it be time ; for a the sun sink over the western wave the sea grow melancholy and the surf have a sadden tone .
The distant sail appear astray and not of earth in their remoteness amid the desolate waste .
My spirit wanders forth afar , but find no resting-place and come shiver back .
It be time that I be hence .
But grudge me not the day that have be spend in seclusion which yet be not solitude , since the great sea have be my companion , and the little sea-birds my friend , and the wind have tell me his secret , and airy shape have flit around me in my hermitage .
Such companionship work an effect upon a man 's character a if he have be admit to the society of creature that be not mortal .
And when , at noontide , I tread the crowded street , the influence of this day will still be felt ; so that I shall walk among men kindly and a a brother , with affection and sympathy , but yet shall not melt into the indistinguishable mass of humankind .
I shall think my own thought and feel my own emotion and possess my individuality unviolated .
But it be good at the eve of such a day to feel and know that there be men and woman in the world .
That feeling and that knowledge be mine at this moment , for on the shore , far below me , the fishing-party have land from their skiff and be cook their scaly prey by a fire of driftwood kindle in the angle of two rude rock .
The three visionary girl be likewise there .
In the deepening twilight , while the surf be dash near their hearth , the ruddy gleam of the fire throw a strange air of comfort over the wild cove , bestrewn a it be with pebble and seaweed and expose to the `` melancholy main . ''
Moreover , a the smoke climb up the precipice , it bring with it a savory smell from a pan of fried fish and a black kettle of chowder , and remind me that my dinner be nothing but bread and water and a tuft of samphire and an apple .
Methinks the party might find room for another guest at that flat rock which serve them for a table ; and if spoon be scarce , I could pick up a clam-shell on the beach .
They see me now ; and -- the blessing of a hungry man upon him !
-- one of them send up a hospitable shout : `` Halloo , Sir Solitary !
Come down and sup with u ! ''
The lady wave their handkerchief .
Can I decline ?
No ; and be it own , after all my solitary joy , that this be the sweet moment of a day by the seashore .
EDWARD FANE 'S ROSEBUD .
There be hardly a more difficult exercise of fancy than , while gaze at a figure of melancholy age , to recreate it youth , and without entirely obliterate the identity of form and feature to restore those grace which Time have snatch away .
Some old people -- especially woman -- so age-worn and woeful be they , seem never to have be young and gay .
It be easy to conceive that such gloomy phantom be send into the world a withered and decrepit a we behold them now , with sympathy only for pain and grief , to watch at death-beds and weep at funeral .
Even the sable garment of their widowhood appear essential to their existence ; all their attribute combine to render them darksome shadow creep strangely amid the sunshine of human life .
Yet it be no unprofitable task to take one of these doleful creature and set Fancy resolutely at work to brighten the dim eye , and darken the silvery lock , and paint the ashen cheek with rose-color , and repair the shrunken and crazy form , till a dewy maiden shall be see in the old matron 's elbow-chair .
The miracle be work , then let the year roll back again , each sadder than the last , and the whole weight of age and sorrow settle down upon the youthful figure .
Wrinkles and furrow , the handwriting of Time , may thus be decipher and find to contain deep lesson of thought and feeling .
Such profit might be derive by a skilful observer from my much-respected friend the Widow Toothaker , a nurse of great repute who have breathe the atmosphere of sick-chambers and dying-breaths these forty year .
See !
she sit cower over her lonesome hearth with her gown and upper petticoat drawn upward , gather thriftily into her person the whole warmth of the fire which now at nightfall begin to dissipate the autumnal chill of her chamber .
The blaze quiver capriciously in front , alternately glimmer into the deep chasm of her wrinkled visage , and then permit a ghostly dimness to mar the outline of her venerable figure .
And Nurse Toothaker hold a teaspoon in her right hand with which to stir up the content of a tumbler in her left , whence steam a vapory fragrance abhor of temperance society .
Now she sip , now stir , now sip again .
Her sad old heart have need to be revive by the rich infusion of Geneva which be mixed half and half with hot water in the tumbler .
All day long she have be sit by a death-pillow , and quit it for her home only when the spirit of her patient leave the clay and go homeward too .
But now be her melancholy meditation cheer and her torpid blood warm and her shoulder lighten of at least twenty ponderous year by a draught from the true fountain of youth in a case-bottle .
It be strange that men should deem that fount a fable , when it liquor fill more bottle than the Congress-water .
-- Sip it again , good nurse , and see whether a second draught will not take off another score of year , and perhaps ten more , and show u in your high-backed chair the blooming damsel who plight troth with Edward Fane .
-- Get you go , Age and Widowhood !
-- Come back , unwedded Youth !
-- But , ala !
the charm will not work .
In spite of Fancy 's most potent spell , I can see only an old dame cowering over the fire , a picture of decay and desolation , while the November blast roar at her in the chimney and fitful shower rush suddenly against the window .
Yet there be a time when Rose Grafton -- such be the pretty maiden-name of Nurse Toothaker -- possess beauty that would have gladden this dim and dismal chamber a with sunshine .
It win for her the heart of Edward Fane , who have since make so great a figure in the world and be now a grand old gentleman with powdered hair and as gouty a a lord .
These early lover think to have walk hand in hand through life .
They have weep together for Edward 's little sister Mary , whom Rose tend in her sickness -- partly because she be the sweet child that ever live or die , but more for love of him .
She be but three year old .
Being such an infant , Death could not embody his terror in her little corpse ; nor do Rose fear to touch the dead child 's brow , though chill , a she curl the silken hair around it , nor to take her tiny hand and clasp a flower within it finger .
Afterward , when she look through the pane of glass in the coffin-lid and behold Mary 's face , it seem not so much like death or life a like a wax-work wrought into the perfect image of a child asleep and dreaming of it mother 's smile .
Rose think her too fair a thing to be hide in the grave , and wonder that an angel do not snatch up little Mary 's coffin and bear the slumber babe to heaven and bid her wake immortal .
But when the sod be lay on little Mary , the heart of Rose be trouble .
She shudder at the fantasy that in grasp the child 's cold finger her virgin hand have exchange a first greeting with mortality and could never lose the earthy taint .
How many a greeting since !
But a yet she be a fair young girl with the dewdrop of fresh feeling in her bosom , and , instead of `` Rose '' -- which seem too mature a name for her half-opened beauty -- her lover call her `` Rosebud . ''
The rosebud be destine never to bloom for Edward Fane .
His mother be a rich and haughty dame with all the aristocratic prejudice of colonial time .
She scorn Rose Grafton 's humble parentage and cause her son to break his faith , though , have she let him choose , he would have prize his Rosebud above the rich diamond .
The lover part , and have seldom meet again .
Both may have visit the same mansion , but not at the same time , for one be bid to the festal hall and the other to the sick-chamber ; he be the guest of Pleasure and Prosperity , and she of Anguish .
Rose , after their separation , be long seclude within the dwelling of Mr. Toothaker , whom she marry with the revengeful hope of break her false lover 's heart .
She go to her bridegroom 's arm with bitterer tear , they say , than young girl ought to shed at the threshold of the bridal-chamber .
Yet , though her husband 's head be get gray and his heart have be chill with an autumnal frost , Rose soon begin to love him , and wonder at her own conjugal affection .
He be all she have to love ; there be no child .
In a year or two poor Mr. Toothaker be visit with a wearisome infirmity which settle in his joint and make him weak than a child .
He creep forth about his business , and come home at dinner-time and eventide , not with the manly tread that gladden a wife 's heart , but slowly , feebly , jot down each dull footstep with a melancholy dub of his staff .
We must pardon his pretty wife if she sometimes blush to own him .
Her visitor , when they hear him come , look for the appearance of some old , old man , but he drag his nerveless limb into the parlor -- and there be Mr. Toothaker !
The disease increase , he never go into the sunshine save with a staff in his right hand and his left on his wife 's shoulder , bear heavily downward like a dead man 's hand .
Thus , a slender woman still look maiden-like , she support his tall , broad-chested frame along the pathway of their little garden , and pluck the rose for her gray-haired husband , and speak soothingly a to an infant .
His mind be palsy with his body ; it utmost energy be peevishness .
In a few month more she help him up the staircase with a pause at every step , and a longer one upon the landing-place , and a heavy glance behind a he cross the threshold of his chamber .
He know , poor man !
that the precinct of those four wall would thenceforth be his world -- his world , his home , his tomb , at once a dwelling-and a burial-place -- till he be bear to a darker and a narrow one .
But Rose be with him in the tomb .
He lean upon her in his daily passage from the bed to the chair by the fireside , and back again from the weary chair to the joyless bed -- his bed and hers , their marriage-bed -- till even this short journey cease and his head lay all day upon the pillow and hers all night beside it .
How long poor Mr. Toothaker be keep in misery !
Death seem to draw near the door , and often to lift the latch , and sometimes to thrust his ugly skull into the chamber , nod to Rose and point at her husband , but still delay to enter .
`` This bedridden wretch can not escape me , '' quoth Death .
`` I will go forth and run a race with the swift and fight a battle with the strong , and come back for Toothaker at my leisure . ''
Oh , when the deliverer come so near , in the dull anguish of her worn-out sympathy do she never long to cry , `` Death , come in '' ?
But no ; we have no right to ascribe such a wish to our friend Rose .
She never fail in a wife 's duty to her poor sick husband .
She murmur not though a glimpse of the sunny sky be a strange to her a him , nor answered peevishly though his complain accent rouse her from sweetest dream only to share his wretchedness .
He know her faith , yet nourish a cankered jealousy ; and when the slow disease have chill all his heart save one lukewarm spot which Death 's frozen finger be search for , his last word be , `` What would my Rose have do for her first love , if she have be so true and kind to a sick old man like me ? ''
And then his poor soul creep away and leave the body lifeless , though hardly more so than for year before , and Rose a widow , though in truth it be the wedding-night that widow her .
She felt glad , it must be own , when Mr. Toothaker be bury , because his corpse have retain such a likeness to the man half alive that she hearken for the sad murmur of his voice bid her shift his pillow .

Come put a blanket on my foot ! ''
So now the Rosebud be the widow Toothaker .
Her trouble have come early , and , tedious a they seem , have pass before all her bloom be flee .
She be still fair enough to captivate a bachelor , or with a widow 's cheerful gravity she might have win a widower , steal into his heart in the very guise of his dead wife .
But the widow Toothaker have no such project .
By her watching and continual care her heart have become knit to her first husband with a constancy which change it very nature and make her love him for his infirmity , and infirmity for his sake .
When the palsied old man be go , even her early lover could not have supply his place .
She have dwell in a sick-chamber and be the companion of a half-dead wretch till she could scarcely breathe in a free air and felt ill at ease with the healthy and the happy .
She miss the fragrance of the doctor 's stuff .
She walk the chamber with a noiseless footfall .
If visitor come in , she speak in soft and soothing accent , and be startle and shock by their loud voice .
Often in the lonesome evening she look timorously from the fireside to the bed , with almost a hope of recognize a ghastly face upon the pillow .
Then go her thought sadly to her husband 's grave .
If one impatient throb have wrong him in his lifetime , if she have secretly repine because her buoyant youth be imprison with his torpid age , if ever while slumber beside him a treacherous dream have admit another into her heart , -- yet the sick man have be prepare a revenge which the dead now claim .
On his painful pillow he have cast a spell around her ; his groan and misery have prove more captivating charm than gayety and youthful grace ; in his semblance Disease itself have win the Rosebud for a bride , nor could his death dissolve the nuptials .

At length she recognize her destiny .
We have behold her a the maid , the wife , the widow ; now we see her in a separate and insulated character : she be in all her attribute Nurse Toothaker .
And Nurse Toothaker alone , with her own shrivelled lip , could make known her experience in that capacity .
What a history might she record of the great sickness in which she have go hand in hand with the exterminate angel !
She remember when the small-pox hoist a red banner on almost every house along the street .
She have witness when the typhus fever sweep off a whole household , young and old , all but a lonely mother , who vainly shriek to follow her last loved one .
Where would be Death 's triumph if none live to weep ?
She can speak of strange malady that have break out a if spontaneously , but be find to have be import from foreign land with rich silk and other merchandise , the costly portion of the cargo .

Strange that such black mischief should lurk in a maiden 's grave !
She love to tell how strong men fight with fiery fever , utterly refuse to give up their breath , and how consumptive virgin fade out of the world , scarcely reluctant , a if their lover be woo them to a far country .
-- Tell u , thou fearful woman ; tell u the death-secrets .
Fain would I search out the meaning of word faintly gasp with intermingled sob and broken sentence half-audibly speak between earth and the judgment-seat .
An awful woman !
She be the patron-saint of young physician and the bosom-friend of old one .
In the mansion where she enter the inmate provide themselves black garment ; the coffin-maker follow her , and the bell toll a she come away from the threshold .
Death himself have meet her at so many a bedside that he put forth his bony hand to greet Nurse Toothaker .
She be an awful woman .
And oh , be it conceivable that this handmaid of human infirmity and affliction -- so darkly stain , so thoroughly imbue with all that be saddest in the doom of mortal -- can ever again be bright and gladsome even though bath in the sunshine of eternity ?
By her long communion with woe have she not forfeit her inheritance of immortal joy ?
Does any germ of bliss survive within her ?
Hark !
an eager knocking st Nurse Toothaker 's door .
She start from her drowsy reverie , set aside the empty tumbler and teaspoon , and light a lamp at the dim ember of the fire .

Edward Fane !

I be ready .

THE THREEFOLD DESTINY .
A FARY LEGEND .
I have sometimes produce a singular and not unpleasing effect , so far a my own mind be concern , by imagine a train of incident in which the spirit and mechanism of the fary legend should be combine with the character and manner of familiar life .
In the little tale which follow a subdued tinge of the wild and wonderful be throw over a sketch of New England personage and scenery , yet , it be hop , without entirely obliterate the sober hue of nature .
Rather than a story of event claim to be real , it may be consider a an allegory such a the writer of the last century would have express in the shape of an Eastern tale , but to which I have endeavor to give a more lifelike warmth than could be infuse into those fanciful production .
In the twilight of a summer eve a tall dark figure over which long and remote travel have throw an outlandish aspect be enter a village not in `` fary londe , '' but within our own familiar boundary .
The staff on which this traveller lean have be his companion from the spot where it grow in the jungle of Hindostan ; the hat that overshadow his sombre brow , have shield him from the sun of Spain ; but his cheek have be blacken by the red-hot wind of an Arabian desert and have felt the frozen breath of an Arctic region .
Long sojourn amid wild and dangerous men , he still wear beneath his vest the ataghan which he have once strike into the throat of a Turkish robber .
In every foreign clime he have lose something of his New England characteristic , and perhaps from every people he have unconsciously borrow a new peculiarity ; so that when the world-wanderer again trod the street of his native village it be no wonder that he pass unrecognized , though excite the gaze and curiosity of all .
Yet , a his arm casually touch that of a young woman who be wend her way to an evening lecture , she start and almost utter a cry .
`` Ralph Cranfield ! ''
be the name that she half articulate .
`` Can that be my old playmate Faith Egerton ? ''
think the traveller , look round at her figure , but without pause .
Ralph Cranfield from his youth upward have felt himself mark out for a high destiny .
He have imbibe the idea -- we say not whether it be reveal to him by witchcraft or in a dream of prophecy , or that his brooding fancy have palm it own dictate upon him a the oracle of a sybil , but he have imbibe the idea , and hold it firm among his article of faith -- that three marvellous event of his life be to be confirm to him by three sign .
The first of these three fatality , and perhaps the one on which his youthful imagination have dwell most fondly , be the discovery of the maid who alone of all the maid on earth could make him happy by her love .
He be to roam around the world till he should meet a beautiful woman wear on her bosom a jewel in the shape of a heart -- whether of pearl or ruby or emerald or carbuncle or a changeful opal , or perhaps a priceless diamond , Ralph Cranfield little care , so long a it be a heart of one peculiar shape .
On encounter this lovely stranger he be bind to address her thus : `` Maiden , I have bring you a heavy heart .
May I rest it weight on you ? ''
And if she be his fated bride -- if their kindred soul be destine to form a union here below which all eternity should only bind more closely -- she would reply , with her finger on the heart-shaped jewel , `` This token which I have wear so long be the assurance that you may . ''
And , secondly , Ralph Cranfield have a firm belief that there be a mighty treasure hidden somewhere in the earth of which the burial-place would be reveal to none but him .
When his foot should press upon the mysterious spot , there would be a hand before him point downward -- whether carve of marble or hewn in gigantic dimension on the side of a rocky precipice , or perchance a hand of flame in empty air , he could not tell , but at least he would discern a hand , the forefinger point downward , and beneath it the Latin word '' Effode '' -- `` Dig ! ''
And , dig thereabouts , the gold in coin or ingot , the precious stone , or of whatever else the treasure might consist , would be certain to reward his toil .
The third and last of the miraculous event in the life of this high-destined man be to be the attainment of extensive influence and sway over his fellow-creatures .
Whether he be to be a king and founder of a hereditary throne , or the victorious leader of a people contend for their freedom , or the apostle of a purified and regenerated faith , be leave for futurity to show .
As messenger of the sign by which Ralph Cranfield might recognize the summons , three venerable men be to claim audience of him .
The chief among them -- a dignified and majestic person array , it may be suppose , in the flowing garment of an ancient sage -- would be the bearer of a wand or prophet 's rod .
With this wand or rod or staff the venerable sage would trace a certain figure in the air , and then proceed to make know his Heaven-instructed message , which , if obey , must lead to glorious result .
With this proud fate before him , in the flush of his imaginative youth Ralph Cranfield have set forth to seek the maid , the treasure , and the venerable sage with his gift of extended empire .
And have he find them ?
Alas !
it be not with the aspect of a triumphant man who have achieve a nobler destiny than all his fellow , but rather with the gloom of one struggle against peculiar and continual adversity , that he now pass homeward to his mother 's cottage .
He have come back , but only for a time , to lay aside the pilgrim 's staff , trust that his weary manhood would regain somewhat of the elasticity of youth in the spot where his threefold fate have be foreshow him .
There have be few change in the village , for it be not one of those thriving place where a year 's prosperity make more than the havoc of a century 's decay , but , like a gray hair in a young man 's head , an antiquated little town full of old maid and age elm and moss-grown dwelling .
Few seem to be the change here .
The drooping elm , indeed , have a more majestic spread , the weather-blackened house be adorn with a denser thatch of verdant moss , and doubtless there be a few more gravestone in the burial-ground inscribed with name that have once be familiar in the village street ; yet , sum up all the mischief that ten year have work , it seem scarcely more than if Ralph Cranfield have go forth that very morning and dream a day-dream till the twilight , and then turn back again .
But his heart grow cold because the village do not remember him a he remember the village .
`` Here be the change , '' sigh he , strike his hand upon his breast .
`` Who be this man of thought and care , weary with world-wandering and heavy with disappointed hope ?
The youth return not who go forth so joyously . ''
And now Ralph Cranfield be at his mother 's gate , in front of the small house where the old lady , with slender but sufficient mean , have keep herself comfortable during her son 's long absence .
Admitting himself within the enclosure , he lean against a great old tree , trifle with his own impatience a people often do in those interval when year be sum into a moment .
He take a minute survey of the dwelling -- it window brighten with the sky-gleam , it doorway with the half of a millstone for a step , and the faintly-traced path wave thence to the gate .
He make friend again with his childhood 's friend -- the old tree against which he lean -- and , glance his eye down it trunk , behold something that excite a melancholy smile .
It be a half-obliterated inscription -- the Latin word '' Effode '' -- which he remember to have carve in the bark of the tree with a whole day 's toil when he have first begin to muse about his exalted destiny .
It might be account a rather singular coincidence that the bark just above the inscription have put forth an excrescence shape not unlike a hand , with the forefinger point obliquely at the word of fate .
Such , at least , be it appearance in the dusky light .
`` Now , a credulous man , '' say Ralph Cranfield , carelessly , to himself , `` might suppose that the treasure which I have seek round the world lie bury , after all , at the very door of my mother 's dwelling .
That would be a jest indeed . ''
More he think not about the matter , for now the door be open and an elderly woman appear on the threshold , peer into the dusk to discover who it might be that have intrude on her premise and be stand in the shadow of her tree .
It be Ralph Cranfield 's mother .
Pass we over their greeting , and leave the one to her joy and the other to his rest -- if quiet rest he find .
But when morning break , he arise with a troubled brow , for his sleep and his wakefulness have alike be full of dream .
All the fervor be rekindle with which he have burn of yore to unravel the threefold mystery of his fate .
The crowd of his early vision seem to have await him beneath his mother 's roof and throng riotously around to welcome his return .
In the well-remembered chamber , on the pillow where his infancy have slumber , he have pass a wilder night than ever in an Arab tent or when he have repose his head in the ghastly shade of a haunt forest .
A shadowy maid have steal to his bedside and lay her finger on the scintillating heart ; a hand of flame have glow amid the darkness , point downward to a mystery within the earth ; a hoary sage have wave his prophetic wand and beckon the dreamer onward to a chair of state .
The same phantom , though fainter in the daylight , still flit about , the cottage and mingle among the crowd of familiar face that be draw thither by the news of Ralph Cranfield 's return to bid him welcome for his mother 's sake .
There they find him , a tall , dark , stately man of foreign aspect , courteous in demeanor and mild of speech , yet with an abstract eye which seem often to snatch a glance at the invisible .
Meantime , the widow Cranfield go bustle about the house full of joy that she again have somebody to love and be careful of , and for whom she might vex and tease herself with the petty trouble of daily life .
It be nearly noon when she look forth from the door and descry three personage of note come along the street through the hot sunshine and the mass of elm-tree shade .
At length they reach her gate and undo the latch .
`` See , Ralph ! ''
exclaim she , with maternal pride ; `` here be Squire Hawkwood and the two other selectman come on purpose to see you .
Now , do tell them a good long story about what you have see in foreign part . ''
The foremost of the three visitor , Squire Hawkwood , be a very pompous but excellent old gentleman , the head and prime-mover in all the affair of the village , and universally acknowledge to be one of the sage men on earth .
He wear , accord to a fashion even then become antiquate , a three-cornered hat , and carry a silver-headed cane the use of which seem to be rather for flourish in the air than for assist the progress of his leg .
His two companion be elderly and respectable yeoman who , retain an ante-Revolutionary reverence for rank and hereditary wealth , keep a little in the squire 's rear .
As they approach along the pathway Ralph Cranfield sit in an oaken elbow-chair half unconsciously gaze at the three visitor and envelop their homely figure in the misty romance that pervade his mental world .
`` Here , '' think he , smile at the conceit -- `` here come three elderly personage , and the first of the three be a venerable sage with a staff .
What if this embassy should bring me the message of my fate ? ''
While Squire Hawkwood and his colleague enter , Ralph rise from his seat and advance a few step to receive them , and his stately figure and dark countenance a he bend courteously toward his guest have a natural dignity contrast well with the bustle importance of the squire .
The old gentleman , accord to invariable custom , give an elaborate preliminary flourish with his cane in the air , then remove his three-cornered hat in order to wipe his brow , and finally proceed to make know his errand .
`` My colleague and myself , '' begin the squire , `` be burden with momentous duty , be jointly selectman of this village .
Our mind for the space of three day past have be laboriously bent on the selection of a suitable person to fill a most important office and take upon himself a charge and rule which , wisely consider , may be rank no low than those of king and potentate .
And whereas you , our native townsman , be of good natural intellect and well cultivate by foreign travel , and that certain vagary and fantasy of your youth be doubtless long ago correct , -- take all these matter , I say , into due consideration , we be of opinion that Providence hath send you hither at this juncture for our very purpose . ''
During this harangue Cranfield gaze fixedly at the speaker , a if he behold something mysterious and unearthly in his pompous little figure , and a if the squire have wear the flow robe of an ancient sage instead of a square-skirted coat , flap waistcoat , velvet breech and silk stocking .
Nor be his wonder without sufficient cause , for the flourish of the squire 's staff , marvellous to relate , have describe precisely the signal in the air which be to ratify the message of the prophetic sage whom Cranfield have seek around the world .
`` And what , '' inquire Ralph Cranfield , with a tremor in his voice -- `` what may this office be which be to equal me with king and potentate ? ''
`` No less than instructor of our village school , '' answer Squire Hawkwood , `` the office be now vacant by the death of the venerable Master Whitaker after a fifty year ' incumbency . ''
`` I will consider of your proposal , '' reply Ralph Cranfield , hurriedly , `` and will make known my decision within three day . ''
After a few more word the village dignitary and his companion take their leave .
But to Cranfield 's fancy their image be still present , and become more and more invested with the dim awfulness of figure which have first appear to him in a dream , and afterward have show themselves in his waking moment , assume homely aspects among familiar thing .
His mind dwelt upon the feature of the squire till they grow confuse with those of the visionary sage and one appear but the shadow of the other .
The same visage , he now thought , have look forth upon him from the Pyramid of Cheops ; the same form have beckon to him among the colonnade of the Alhambra ; the same figure have mistily reveal itself through the ascend steam of the Great Geyser .
At every effort of his memory he recognize some trait of the dreamy messenger of destiny in this pompous , bustling , self-important , little-great man of the village .
Amid such musing Ralph Cranfield sit all day in the cottage , scarcely hear and vaguely answer his mother 's thousand question about his travel and adventure .
At sunset he rouse himself to take a stroll , and , pass the age elm tree , his eye be again catch by the semblance of a hand point downward at the half-obliterated inscription .
As Cranfield walk down the street of the village the level sunbeam throw his shadow far before him , and he fancy that , a his shadow walk among distant object , so have there be a presentiment stalk in advance of him throughout his life .
And when he draw near each object over which his tall shadow have precede him , still it prove to be one of the familiar recollection of his infancy and youth .
Every crook in the pathway be remember .
Even the more transitory characteristic of the scene be the same a in by-gone day .
A company of cow be graze on the grassy roadside , and refresh him with their fragrant breath .
`` It be sweeter , '' think he , `` than the perfume which be waft to our ship from the Spice Islands . ''
The round little figure of a child roll from a doorway and lay laugh almost beneath Cranfield 's foot .
The dark and stately man stoop down , and , lift the infant , restore him to his mother 's arm .
`` The child , '' say he to himself , and sigh and smile -- `` the child be to be my charge . ''
And while a flow of natural feeling gush like a well-spring in his heart he come to a dwelling which he could nowise forbear to enter .
A sweet voice which seem to come from a deep and tender soul be warble a plaintive little air within .
He bend his head and pass through the lowly door .
As his foot sound upon the threshold a young woman advance from the dusky interior of the house , at first hastily , and then with a more uncertain step , till they meet face to face .
There be a singular contrast in their two figure -- he dark and picturesque , one who have battle with the world , whom all sun have shine upon and whom all wind have blow on a varied course ; she neat , comely and quiet -- quiet even in her agitation -- a if all her emotion have be subdue to the peaceful tenor of her life .
Yet their face , all unlike a they be , have an expression that seem not so alien -- a glow of kindred feeling flash upward anew from half-extinguished ember .
`` You be welcome home , '' say Faith Egerton .
But Cranfield do not immediately answer , for his eye have , be catch by an ornament in the shape of a heart which Faith wear a a brooch upon her bosom .
The material be the ordinary white quartz , and he recollect have himself shape it out of one of those Indian arrowhead which be so often find in the ancient haunt of the red men .
It be precisely on the pattern of that worn by the visionary maid .
When Cranfield depart on his shadowy search , he have bestow this brooch , in a gold setting , a a part gift to Faith Egerton .
`` So , Faith , you have keep the heart ? ''
say he , at length .
`` Yes , '' say she , blush deeply ; then , more gayly , `` And what else have you bring me from beyond the sea ? ''
`` Faith , '' reply Ralph Cranfield , utter the fat word by an uncontrollable impulse , `` I have bring you nothing but a heavy heart .
May I rest it weight on you ? ''
`` This token which I have wear so long , '' say Faith , lay her tremulous finger on the heart , `` be the assurance that you may . ''
`` Faith , Faith ! ''
cried Cranfield , clasp her in his arm ; `` you have interpret my wild and weary dream ! ''
Yes , the wild dreamer be awake at last .
To find the mysterious treasure he be to till the earth around his mother 's dwelling and reap it product ; instead of warlike command or regal or religious sway , he be to rule over the village child ; and now the visionary maid have fade from his fancy , and in her place he saw the playmate of his childhood .
Would all who cherish such wild wish but look around them , they would oftenest find their sphere of duty , of prosperity and happiness , within those precinct and in that station where Providence itself have cast their lot .

High above the city , on a tall column , stand the statue of the Happy Prince .
He be gild all over with thin leaf of fine gold , for eye he have two bright sapphire , and a large red ruby glow on his sword-hilt .
He be very much admire indeed .
`` He be as beautiful a a weathercock , '' remark one of the Town Councillors who wish to gain a reputation for have artistic taste ; `` only not quite so useful , '' he add , fear l people should think him unpractical , which he really be not .
`` Why ca n't you be like the Happy Prince ? ''
ask a sensible mother of her little boy who be cry for the moon .
`` The Happy Prince never dream of cry for anything . ''
`` I be glad there be some one in the world who be quite happy , '' mutter a disappointed man a he gaze at the wonderful statue .
`` He look just like an angel , '' say the Charity Children a they come out of the cathedral in their bright scarlet cloak and their clean white pinafore .

say the Mathematical Master , `` you have never see one . ''
`` Ah !
but we have , in our dream , '' answer the child ; and the Mathematical Master frown and look very severe , for he do not approve of child dream .
One night there fly over the city a little Swallow .
His friend have go away to Egypt six week before , but he have stay behind , for he be in love with the most beautiful Reed .
He have meet her early in the spring a he be fly down the river after a big yellow moth , and have be so attracted by her slender waist that he have stop to talk to her .
`` Shall I love you ? ''
say the Swallow , who like to come to the point at once , and the Reed make him a low bow .
So he fly round and round her , touch the water with his wing , and make silver ripple .
This be his courtship , and it last all through the summer .
`` It be a ridiculous attachment , '' twitter the other Swallows ; `` she have no money , and far too many relation '' ; and indeed the river be quite full of Reeds .
Then , when the autumn come they all fly away .
After they have go he felt lonely , and begin to tire of his lady - love .
`` She have no conversation , '' he say , `` and I be afraid that she be a coquette , for she be always flirt with the wind . ''
And certainly , whenever the wind blew , the Reed make the most graceful curtsey .

`` Will you come away with me ? ''
he say finally to her ; but the Reed shake her head , she be so attached to her home .
`` You have be trifle with me , '' he cry .
`` I be off to the Pyramids .
Good-bye ! ''
and he fly away .
All day long he fly , and at night-time he arrive at the city .
`` Where shall I put up ? ''
he say ; `` I hope the town have make preparation . ''
Then he saw the statue on the tall column .
`` I will put up there , '' he cry ; `` it be a fine position , with plenty of fresh air . ''
So he alight just between the foot of the Happy Prince .
`` I have a golden bedroom , '' he say softly to himself a he look round , and he prepare to go to sleep ; but just a he be put his head under his wing a large drop of water fell on him .
`` What a curious thing ! ''
he cry ; `` there be not a single cloud in the sky , the star be quite clear and bright , and yet it be rain .
The climate in the north of Europe be really dreadful .
The Reed use to like the rain , but that be merely her selfishness . ''
Then another drop fell .
`` What be the use of a statue if it can not keep the rain off ? ''
he say ; `` I must look for a good chimney-pot , '' and he determine to fly away .
But before he have open his wing , a third drop fell , and he look up , and saw -- Ah !
what do he see ?

he say .
`` I be the Happy Prince . ''
