`` When I be alive and have a human heart , '' answer the statue , `` I do not know what tear be , for I live in the Palace of Sans - Souci , where sorrow be not allow to enter .
In the daytime I play with my companion in the garden , and in the evening I lead the dance in the Great Hall .
Round the garden run a very lofty wall , but I never care to ask what lay beyond it , everything about me be so beautiful .
My courtier call me the Happy Prince , and happy indeed I be , if pleasure be happiness .
So I live , and so I die .
And now that I be dead they have set me up here so high that I can see all the ugliness and all the misery of my city , and though my heart be make of lead yet I can not choose but weep . ''
`` What !
be he not solid gold ? ''
say the Swallow to himself .
He be too polite to make any personal remark out loud .
`` Far away , '' continue the statue in a low musical voice , `` far away in a little street there be a poor house .
One of the window be open , and through it I can see a woman seat at a table .
Her face be thin and worn , and she have coarse , red hand , all prick by the needle , for she be a seamstress .
She be embroider passion - flower on a satin gown for the lovely of the Queen 's maids-of - honour to wear at the next Court-ball .
In a bed in the corner of the room her little boy be lie ill .
He have a fever , and be ask for orange .
His mother have nothing to give him but river water , so he be cry .
Swallow , Swallow , little Swallow , will you not bring her the ruby out of my sword-hilt ?
My foot be fasten to this pedestal and I can not move . ''
`` I be wait for in Egypt , '' say the Swallow .
`` My friend be fly up and down the Nile , and talk to the large lotus - flower .
Soon they will go to sleep in the tomb of the great King .
The King be there himself in his painted coffin .
He be wrap in yellow linen , and embalm with spice .
Round his neck be a chain of pale green jade , and his hand be like withered leaf . ''
`` Swallow , Swallow , little Swallow , '' say the Prince , `` will you not stay with me for one night , and be my messenger ?
The boy be so thirsty , and the mother so sad . ''
`` I do n't think I like boy , '' answer the Swallow .
`` Last summer , when I be stay on the river , there be two rude boy , the miller 's son , who be always throw stone at me .
They never hit me , of course ; we swallow fly far too well for that , and besides , I come of a family famous for it agility ; but still , it be a mark of disrespect . ''
But the Happy Prince look so sad that the little Swallow be sorry .
`` It be very cold here , '' he say ; `` but I will stay with you for one night , and be your messenger . ''
`` Thank you , little Swallow , '' say the Prince .
So the Swallow pick out the great ruby from the Prince 's sword , and fly away with it in his beak over the roof of the town .
He pass by the cathedral tower , where the white marble angel be sculpture .
He pass by the palace and hear the sound of dancing .
A beautiful girl come out on the balcony with her lover .
`` How wonderful the star be , '' he say to her , `` and how wonderful be the power of love ! ''
`` I hope my dress will be ready in time for the State-ball , '' she answer ; `` I have order passion-flowers to be embroider on it ; but the seamstress be so lazy . ''
He pass over the river , and saw the lantern hang to the mast of the ship .
He pass over the Ghetto , and saw the old Jews bargaining with each other , and weigh out money in copper scale .
At last he come to the poor house and look in .
The boy be toss feverishly on his bed , and the mother have fall asleep , she be so tired .
In he hop , and lay the great ruby on the table beside the woman 's thimble .
Then he fly gently round the bed , fan the boy 's forehead with his wing .
`` How cool I feel , '' say the boy , `` I must be get good '' ; and he sink into a delicious slumber .
Then the Swallow fly back to the Happy Prince , and tell him what he have do .
`` It be curious , '' he remark , `` but I feel quite warm now , although it be so cold . ''
`` That be because you have do a good action , '' say the Prince .
And the little Swallow begin to think , and then he fell asleep .
Thinking always make him sleepy .
When day break he fly down to the river and have a bath .
`` What a remarkable phenomenon , '' say the Professor of Ornithology a he be pass over the bridge .
`` A swallow in winter ! ''
And he write a long letter about it to the local newspaper .
Every one quote it , it be full of so many word that they could not understand .
`` To-night I go to Egypt , '' say the Swallow , and he be in high spirit at the prospect .
He visit all the public monument , and sit a long time on top of the church steeple .
Wherever he go the Sparrows chirrup , and say to each other , `` What a distinguished stranger ! ''
so he enjoy himself very much .
When the moon rise he fly back to the Happy Prince .
`` Have you any commission for Egypt ? ''
he cry ; `` I be just start . ''
`` Swallow , Swallow , little Swallow , '' say the Prince , `` will you not stay with me one night long ? ''
`` I be wait for in Egypt , '' answer the Swallow .
`` To-morrow my friend will fly up to the Second Cataract .
The river-horse couch there among the bulrush , and on a great granite throne sit the God Memnon .
All night long he watch the star , and when the morning star shine he utter one cry of joy , and then he be silent .
At noon the yellow lion come down to the water 's edge to drink .
They have eye like green beryl , and their roar be loud than the roar of the cataract .
`` Swallow , Swallow , little Swallow , '' say the Prince , `` far away across the city I see a young man in a garret .
He be lean over a desk cover with paper , and in a tumbler by his side there be a bunch of withered violet .
His hair be brown and crisp , and his lip be red a a pomegranate , and he have large and dreamy eye .
He be try to finish a play for the Director of the Theatre , but he be too cold to write any more .
There be no fire in the grate , and hunger have make him faint . ''
`` I will wait with you one night longer , '' say the Swallow , who really have a good heart .
`` Shall I take him another ruby ? ''
`` Alas !
I have no ruby now , '' say the Prince ; `` my eye be all that I have leave .
They be make of rare sapphire , which be bring out of India a thousand year ago .
Pluck out one of them and take it to him .
He will sell it to the jeweller , and buy food and firewood , and finish his play . ''
`` Dear Prince , '' say the Swallow , `` I can not do that '' ; and he begin to weep .
`` Swallow , Swallow , little Swallow , '' say the Prince , `` do a I command you . ''
So the Swallow pluck out the Prince 's eye , and fly away to the student 's garret .
It be easy enough to get in , a there be a hole in the roof .
Through this he dart , and come into the room .
The young man have his head bury in his hand , so he do not hear the flutter of the bird 's wing , and when he look up he find the beautiful sapphire lie on the withered violet .
`` I be begin to be appreciate , '' he cry ; `` this be from some great admirer .
Now I can finish my play , '' and he look quite happy .
The next day the Swallow fly down to the harbour .
He sit on the mast of a large vessel and watch the sailor haul big chest out of the hold with rope .
`` Heave a-hoy ! ''
they shout a each chest come up .
`` I be go to Egypt '' !
cry the Swallow , but nobody mind , and when the moon rise he fly back to the Happy Prince .
`` I be come to bid you good-bye , '' he cry .
`` Swallow , Swallow , little Swallow , '' say the Prince , `` will you not stay with me one night long ? ''
`` It be winter , '' answer the Swallow , `` and the chill snow will soon be here .
In Egypt the sun be warm on the green palm-trees , and the crocodile lie in the mud and look lazily about them .
My companion be build a nest in the Temple of Baalbec , and the pink and white dove be watch them , and coo to each other .
Dear Prince , I must leave you , but I will never forget you , and next spring I will bring you back two beautiful jewel in place of those you have give away .
The ruby shall be red than a red rise , and the sapphire shall be as blue a the great sea . ''
`` In the square below , '' say the Happy Prince , `` there stand a little match-girl .
She have let her match fall in the gutter , and they be all spoil .
Her father will beat her if she do not bring home some money , and she be cry .
She have no shoe or stocking , and her little head be bare .
Pluck out my other eye , and give it to her , and her father will not beat her . ''
`` I will stay with you one night longer , '' say the Swallow , `` but I can not pluck out your eye .
You would be quite blind then . ''
`` Swallow , Swallow , little Swallow , '' say the Prince , `` do a I command you . ''
So he pluck out the Prince 's other eye , and dart down with it .
He swoop past the match-girl , and slip the jewel into the palm of her hand .
`` What a lovely bit of glass , '' cry the little girl ; and she run home , laugh .
Then the Swallow come back to the Prince .
`` You be blind now , '' he say , `` so I will stay with you always . ''
`` No , little Swallow , '' say the poor Prince , `` you must go away to Egypt . ''
`` I will stay with you always , '' say the Swallow , and he sleep at the Prince 's foot .
All the next day he sit on the Prince 's shoulder , and tell him story of what he have see in strange land .
He tell him of the red ibis , who stand in long row on the bank of the Nile , and catch gold-fish in their beak ; of the Sphinx , who be as old a the world itself , and live in the desert , and know everything ; of the merchant , who walk slowly by the side of their camel , and carry amber bead in their hand ; of the King of the Mountains of the Moon , who be as black a ebony , and worship a large crystal ; of the great green snake that sleep in a palm-tree , and have twenty priest to fee it with honey-cakes ; and of the pygmy who sail over a big lake on large flat leaf , and be always at war with the butterfly .
`` Dear little Swallow , '' say the Prince , `` you tell me of marvellous thing , but more marvellous than anything be the suffering of men and of woman .
There be no Mystery so great a Misery .
Fly over my city , little Swallow , and tell me what you see there . ''
So the Swallow fly over the great city , and saw the rich making merry in their beautiful house , while the beggar be sit at the gate .
He fly into dark lane , and saw the white face of starve child look out listlessly at the black street .
Under the archway of a bridge two little boy be lie in one another 's arm to try and keep themselves warm .
`` How hungry we be ! ''
they say .
`` You must not lie here , '' shout the Watchman , and they wander out into the rain .
Then he fly back and tell the Prince what he have see .
`` I be cover with fine gold , '' say the Prince , `` you must take it off , leaf by leaf , and give it to my poor ; the living always think that gold can make them happy . ''
Leaf after leaf of the fine gold the Swallow pick off , till the Happy Prince look quite dull and grey .
Leaf after leaf of the fine gold he bring to the poor , and the child 's face grow rosier , and they laugh and play game in the street .
`` We have bread now ! ''
they cry .
Then the snow come , and after the snow come the frost .
The street look a if they be make of silver , they be so bright and glistening ; long icicle like crystal dagger hang down from the eaves of the house , everybody go about in fur , and the little boy wear scarlet cap and skate on the ice .
The poor little Swallow grow colder and colder , but he would not leave the Prince , he love him too well .
He pick up crumb outside the baker 's door when the baker be not look and try to keep himself warm by flap his wing .
But at last he know that he be go to die .
He have just strength to fly up to the Prince 's shoulder once more .
`` Good-bye , dear Prince ! ''
he murmur , `` will you let me kiss your hand ? ''
`` I be glad that you be go to Egypt at last , little Swallow , '' say the Prince , `` you have stay too long here ; but you must kiss me on the lip , for I love you . ''
`` It be not to Egypt that I be go , '' say the Swallow .
`` I be go to the House of Death .
Death be the brother of Sleep , be he not ? ''
And he kiss the Happy Prince on the lip , and fell down dead at his foot .
At that moment a curious crack sound inside the statue , a if something have break .
The fact be that the leaden heart have snap right in two .
It certainly be a dreadfully hard frost .
Early the next morning the Mayor be walk in the square below in company with the Town Councillors .
As they pass the column he look up at the statue : `` Dear me !
how shabby the Happy Prince look ! ''
he say .
`` How shabby indeed ! ''
cry the Town Councillors , who always agree with the Mayor ; and they go up to look at it .
`` The ruby have fall out of his sword , his eye be go , and he be golden no longer , '' say the Mayor in fact , `` he be litttle beter than a beggar ! ''
`` Little good than a beggar , '' say the Town Councillors .
`` And here be actually a dead bird at his foot ! ''
continue the Mayor .
`` We must really issue a proclamation that bird be not to be allow to die here . ''
And the Town Clerk make a note of the suggestion .
So they pull down the statue of the Happy Prince .
`` As he be no longer beautiful he be no longer useful , '' say the Art Professor at the University .
Then they melt the statue in a furnace , and the Mayor hold a meeting of the Corporation to decide what be to be do with the metal .
`` We must have another statue , of course , '' he say , `` and it shall be a statue of myself . ''
`` Of myself , '' say each of the Town Councillors , and they quarrel .
When I last heard of them they be quarrel still .
`` What a strange thing ! ''
say the overseer of the workman at the foundry .
`` This broken lead heart will not melt in the furnace .
We must throw it away . ''
So they throw it on a dust-heap where the dead Swallow be also lie .
`` Bring me the two most precious thing in the city , '' say God to one of His Angels ; and the Angel bring Him the leaden heart and the dead bird .
`` You have rightly choose , '' say God , `` for in my garden of Paradise this little bird shall sing for evermore , and in my city of gold the Happy Prince shall praise me . ''
THE NIGHTINGALE AND THE ROSE `` She say that she would dance with me if I bring her red rose , '' cry the young Student ; `` but in all my garden there be no red rise . ''
From her nest in the holm-oak tree the Nightingale hear him , and she look out through the leaf , and wonder .
`` No red rise in all my garden ! ''
he cry , and his beautiful eye fill with tear .
`` Ah , on what little thing do happiness depend !
I have read all that the wise men have write , and all the secret of philosophy be mine , yet for want of a red rise be my life make wretched . ''
`` Here at last be a true lover , '' say the Nightingale .
`` Night after night have I sing of him , though I know him not : night after night have I tell his story to the star , and now I see him .
His hair be dark a the hyacinth-blossom , and his lip be red a the rise of his desire ; but passion have make his face like pale ivory , and sorrow have set her seal upon his brow . ''
`` The Prince give a ball to-morrow night , '' murmur the young Student , `` and my love will be of the company .
If I bring her a red rise she will dance with me till dawn .
If I bring her a red rise , I shall hold her in my arm , and she will lean her head upon my shoulder , and her hand will be clasp in mine .
But there be no red rise in my garden , so I shall sit lonely , and she will pass me by .
She will have no heed of me , and my heart will break . ''
`` Here indeed be the true lover , '' say the Nightingale .
`` What I sing of , he suffer -- what be joy to me , to him be pain .
Surely Love be a wonderful thing .
It be more precious than emerald , and dear than fine opal .
Pearls and pomegranate can not buy it , nor be it set forth in the marketplace .
It may not be purchase of the merchant , nor can it be weigh out in the balance for gold . ''
`` The musician will sit in their gallery , '' say the young Student , `` and play upon their stringed instrument , and my love will dance to the sound of the harp and the violin .
She will dance so lightly that her foot will not touch the floor , and the courtier in their gay dress will throng round her .
But with me she will not dance , for I have no red rise to give her '' ; and he fling himself down on the grass , and bury his face in his hand , and wept .
`` Why be he weep ? ''
ask a little Green Lizard , a he run past him with his tail in the air .
`` Why , indeed ? ''
say a Butterfly , who be flutter about after a sunbeam .
`` Why , indeed ? ''
whisper a Daisy to his neighbour , in a soft , low voice .
`` He be weep for a red rise , '' say the Nightingale .
`` For a red rise ? ''
they cry ; `` how very ridiculous ! ''
and the little Lizard , who be something of a cynic , laugh outright .
But the Nightingale understand the secret of the Student 's sorrow , and she sit silent in the oak-tree , and think about the mystery of Love .
Suddenly she spread her brown wing for flight , and soar into the air .
She pass through the grove like a shadow , and like a shadow she sail across the garden .
In the centre of the grass-plot be stand a beautiful Rose-tree , and when she saw it she fly over to it , and light upon a spray .
`` Give me a red rise , '' she cry , `` and I will sing you my sweet song . ''
But the Tree shake it head .
`` My rose be white , '' it answer ; `` as white a the foam of the sea , and white than the snow upon the mountain .
But go to my brother who grow round the old sun-dial , and perhaps he will give you what you want . ''
So the Nightingale fly over to the Rose-tree that be grow round the old sun-dial .
`` Give me a red rise , '' she cry , `` and I will sing you my sweet song . ''
But the Tree shake it head .
`` My rose be yellow , '' it answer ; `` as yellow a the hair of the mermaiden who sit upon an amber throne , and yellow than the daffodil that bloom in the meadow before the mower come with his scythe .
But go to my brother who grow beneath the Student 's window , and perhaps he will give you what you want . ''
So the Nightingale fly over to the Rose-tree that be grow beneath the Student 's window .
`` Give me a red rise , '' she cry , `` and I will sing you my sweet song . ''
But the Tree shake it head .
`` My rose be red , '' it answer , `` as red a the foot of the dove , and red than the great fan of coral that wave and wave in the ocean-cavern .
But the winter have chill my vein , and the frost have nip my bud , and the storm have break my branch , and I shall have no rose at all this year . ''
`` One red rise be all I want , '' cry the Nightingale , `` only one red rise !
Is there no way by which I can get it ? ''
`` There be away , '' answer the Tree ; `` but it be so terrible that I dare not tell it to you . ''
`` Tell it to me , '' say the Nightingale , `` I be not afraid . ''
`` If you want a red rise , '' say the Tree , `` you must build it out of music by moonlight , and stain it with your own heart 's - blood .
You must sing to me with your breast against a thorn .
All night long you must sing to me , and the thorn must pierce your heart , and your life-blood must flow into my vein , and become mine . ''
`` Death be a great price to pay for a red rise , '' cry the Nightingale , `` and Life be very dear to all .
It be pleasant to sit in the green wood , and to watch the Sun in his chariot of gold , and the Moon in her chariot of pearl .
Sweet be the scent of the hawthorn , and sweet be the bluebell that hide in the valley , and the heather that blow on the hill .
Yet Love be good than Life , and what be the heart of a bird compare to the heart of a man ? ''
So she spread her brown wing for flight , and soar into the air .
She sweep over the garden like a shadow , and like a shadow she sail through the grove .
The young Student be still lie on the grass , where she have leave him , and the tear be not yet dry in his beautiful eye .
`` Be happy , '' cry the Nightingale , `` be happy ; you shall have your red rise .
I will build it out of music by moonlight , and stain it with my own heart 's - blood .
All that I ask of you in return be that you will be a true lover , for Love be wise than Philosophy , though she be wise , and mighty than Power , though he be mighty .
Flame - colour be his wing , and colour like flame be his body .
His lip be sweet a honey , and his breath be like frankincense . ''
The Student look up from the grass , and listen , but he could not understand what the Nightingale be say to him , for he only know the thing that be write down in book .
But the Oak-tree understood , and felt sad , for he be very fond of the little Nightingale who have build her nest in his branch .
`` Sing me one last song , '' he whisper ; `` I shall feel very lonely when you be go . ''
So the Nightingale sang to the Oak-tree , and her voice be like water bubble from a silver jar .
When she have finish her song the Student get up , and pull a note-book and a lead-pencil out of his pocket .
`` She have form , '' he say to himself , a he walk away through the grove -- `` that can not be deny to her ; but have she get feel ?
I be afraid not .
In fact , she be like most artist ; she be all style , without any sincerity .
She would not sacrifice herself for others .
She think merely of music , and everybody know that the art be selfish .
Still , it must be admit that she have some beautiful note in her voice .
What a pity it be that they do not mean anything , or do any practical good . ''
And he go into his room , and lay down on his little pallet-bed , and begin to think of his love ; and , after a time , he fell asleep .
And when the Moon shone in the heaven the Nightingale fly to the Rose-tree , and set her breast against the thorn .
All night long she sing with her breast against the thorn , and the cold crystal Moon lean down and listen .
All night long she sing , and the thorn go deep and deeply into her breast , and her life-blood ebbed away from her .
She sing first of the birth of love in the heart of a boy and a girl .
And on the top-most spray of the Rose-tree there blossom a marvellous rise , petal follow petal , a song follow song .
Pale be it , at first , a the mist that hang over the river -- pale a the foot of the morning , and silver a the wing of the dawn .
As the shadow of a rise in a mirror of silver , a the shadow of a rise in a water-pool , so be the rise that blossom on the topmost spray of the Tree .
But the Tree cry to the Nightingale to press closer against the thorn .
`` Press closer , little Nightingale , '' cry the Tree , `` or the Day will come before the rise be finish . ''
So the Nightingale press closer against the thorn , and louder and louder grow her song , for she sing of the birth of passion in the soul of a man and a maid .
And a delicate flush of pink come into the leaf of the rise , like the flush in the face of the bridegroom when he kiss the lip of the bride .
But the thorn have not yet reach her heart , so the rise 's heart remain white , for only a Nightingale 's heart 's - blood can crimson the heart of a rise .
And the Tree cry to the Nightingale to press closer against the thorn .
`` Press closer , little Nightingale , '' cry the Tree , `` or the Day will come before the rise be finish . ''
So the Nightingale press closer against the thorn , and the thorn touch her heart , and a fierce pang of pain shot through her .
Bitter , bitter be the pain , and wilder and wilder grow her song , for she sing of the Love that be perfect by Death , of the Love that die not in the tomb .
And the marvellous rise become crimson , like the rise of the eastern sky .
Crimson be the girdle of petal , and crimson a a ruby be the heart .
But the Nightingale 's voice grow fainter , and her little wing begin to beat , and a film come over her eye .
Fainter and fainter grow her song , and she felt something choke her in her throat .
Then she give one last burst of music .
The white Moon heard it , and she forget the dawn , and linger on in the sky .
The red rise heard it , and it tremble all over with ecstasy , and open it petal to the cold morning air .
Echo bore it to her purple cavern in the hill , and wake the sleeping shepherd from their dream .
It float through the reed of the river , and they carry it message to the sea .
`` Look , look ! ''
cry the Tree , `` the rise be finish now '' ; but the Nightingale make no answer , for she be lie dead in the long grass , with the thorn in her heart .
And at noon the Student open his window and look out .
`` Why , what a wonderful piece of luck ! ''
he cry ; `` here be a red rise !
I have never see any rise like it in all my life .
It be so beautiful that I be sure it have a long Latin name '' ; and he lean down and pluck it .
Then he put on his hat , and run up to the Professor 's house with the rise in his hand .
The daughter of the Professor be sit in the doorway wind blue silk on a reel , and her little dog be lie at her foot .
`` You say that you would dance with me if I bring you a red rise , '' cry the Student .
`` Here be the reddest rise in all the world .
You will wear it to-night next your heart , and a we dance together it will tell you how I love you . ''
But the girl frown .
`` I be afraid it will not go with my dress , '' she answer ; `` and , besides , the Chamberlain 's nephew have send me some real jewel , and everybody know that jewel cost far more than flower . ''
`` Well , upon my word , you be very ungrateful , '' say the Student angrily ; and he throw the rise into the street , where it fell into the gutter , and a cart-wheel go over it .
`` Ungrateful ! ''
say the girl .
`` I tell you what , you be very rude ; and , after all , who be you ?
Only a Student .
Why , I do n't believe you have even get silver buckle to your shoe a the Chamberlain 's nephew have '' ; and she get up from her chair and go into the house .
`` What I a silly thing Love be , '' say the Student a he walk away .
`` It be not half as useful a Logic , for it do not prove anything , and it be always tell one of thing that be not go to happen , and make one believe thing that be not true .
In fact , it be quite unpractical , and , a in this age to be practical be everything , I shall go back to Philosophy and study Metaphysics . ''
So he return to his room and pull out a great dusty book , and begin to read .
THE SELFISH GIANT Every afternoon , a they be come from school , the child use to go and play in the Giant 's garden .
It be a large lovely garden , with soft green grass .
Here and there over the grass stand beautiful flower like star , and there be twelve peach-trees that in the spring-time broke out into delicate blossom of pink and pearl , and in the autumn bore rich fruit .
The bird sit on the tree and sang so sweetly that the child use to stop their game in order to listen to them .
`` How happy we be here ! ''
they cry to each other .
One day the Giant come back .
He have be to visit his friend the Cornish ogre , and have stay with him for seven year .
After the seven year be over he have say all that he have to say , for his conversation be limit , and he determine to return to his own castle .
When he arrive he saw the child play in the garden .
`` What be you do here ? ''
he cry in a very gruff voice , and the child run away .
`` My own garden be my own garden , '' say the Giant ; `` any one can understand that , and I will allow nobody to play in it but myself . ''
So he build a high wall all round it , and put up a notice-board .
TRESPASSERS WILL BE PROSECUTED He be a very selfish Giant .
The poor child have now nowhere to play .
They try to play on the road , but the road be very dusty and full of hard stone , and they do not like it .
They use to wander round the high wall when their lesson be over , and talk about the beautiful garden inside .
`` How happy we be there , '' they say to each other .
Then the Spring come , and all over the country there be little blossom and little bird .
Only in the garden of the Selfish Giant it be still winter .
The bird do not care to sing in it a there be no child , and the tree forget to blossom .
Once a beautiful flower put it head out from the grass , but when it saw the notice-board it be so sorry for the child that it slip back into the ground again , and go off to sleep .
The only people who be pleased be the Snow and the Frost .
`` Spring have forget this garden , '' they cry , `` so we will live here all the year round . ''
The Snow cover up the grass with her great white cloak , and the Frost paint all the tree silver .
Then they invite the North Wind to stay with them , and he come .
He be wrap in fur , and he roar all day about the garden , and blow the chimney-pots down .
`` This be a delightful spot , '' he say , `` we must ask the Hail on a visit . ''
So the Hail come .
Every day for three hour he rattle on the roof of the castle till he break most of the slate , and then he run round and round the garden as fast a he could go .
He be dress in grey , and his breath be like ice .
`` I can not understand why the Spring be so late in coming , '' say the Selfish Giant , a he sit at the window and look out at his cold white garden ; `` I hope there will be a change in the weather . ''
But the Spring never come , nor the Summer .
The Autumn give golden fruit to every garden , but to the Giant 's garden she give none .
`` He be too selfish , '' she say .
So it be always Winter there , and the North Wind , and the Hail , and the Frost , and the Snow dance about through the tree .
One morning the Giant be lie awake in bed when he hear some lovely music .
It sound so sweet to his ear that he think it must be the King 's musician pass by .
It be really only a little linnet sing outside his window , but it be so long since he have hear a bird sing in his garden that it seem to him to be the most beautiful music in the world .
Then the Hail stop dance over his head , and the North Wind cease roaring , and a delicious perfume come to him through the open casement .
`` I believe the Spring have come at last , '' say the Giant ; and he jump out of bed and look out .
What do he see ?
He saw a most wonderful sight .
Through a little hole in the wall the child have creep in , and they be sit in the branch of the tree .
In every tree that he could see there be a little child .
And the tree be so glad to have the child back again that they have cover themselves with blossom , and be wave their arm gently above the child 's head .
The bird be fly about and twitter with delight , and the flower be look up through the green grass and laughing .
It be a lovely scene , only in one corner it be still winter .
It be the farthest corner of the garden , and in it be stand a little boy .
He be so small that he could not reach up to the branch of the tree , and he be wander all round it , cry bitterly .
The poor tree be still quite cover with frost and snow , and the North Wind be blow and roar above it .
`` Climb up !
little boy , '' say the Tree , and it bend it branch down as low a it could ; but the boy be too tiny .
And the Giant 's heart melt a he look out .
`` How selfish I have be ! ''
he say ; `` now I know why the Spring would not come here .
I will put that poor little boy on the top of the tree , and then I will knock down the wall , and my garden shall be the child 's playground for ever and ever . ''
He be really very sorry for what he have do .
So he creep downstairs and open the front door quite softly , and go out into the garden .
But when the child saw him they be so frighten that they all run away , and the garden become winter again .
Only the little boy do not run , for his eye be so full of tear that he do not see the Giant coming .
And the Giant steal up behind him and take him gently in his hand , and put him up into the tree .
And the tree broke at once into blossom , and the bird come and sing on it , and the little boy stretch out his two arm and fling them round the Giant 's neck , and kiss him .
And the other child , when they saw that the Giant be not wicked any longer , come run back , and with them come the Spring .
`` It be your garden now , little child , '' say the Giant , and he take a great axe and knock down the wall .
And when the people be go to market at twelve o'clock they find the Giant playing with the child in the most beautiful garden they have ever see .
All day long they play , and in the evening they come to the Giant to bid him good-bye .
`` But where be your little companion ? ''
he say : `` the boy I put into the tree . ''
The Giant love him the best because he have kiss him .
`` We do n't know , '' answer the child ; `` he have go away . ''
`` You must tell him to be sure and come here to-morrow , '' say the Giant .
But the child say that they do not know where he live , and have never see him before ; and the Giant felt very sad .
Every afternoon , when school be over , the child come and play with the Giant .
But the little boy whom the Giant love be never see again .
The Giant be very kind to all the child , yet he long for his first little friend , and often speak of him .
`` How I would like to see him ! ''
he use to say .
Years go over , and the Giant grow very old and feeble .
He could not play about any more , so he sit in a huge armchair , and watch the child at their game , and admire his garden .
`` I have many beautiful flower , '' he say ; `` but the child be the most beautiful flower of all . ''
One winter morning he look out of his window a he be dress .
He do not hate the Winter now , for he know that it be merely the Spring asleep , and that the flower be rest .
Suddenly he rub his eye in wonder , and look and look .
It certainly be a marvellous sight .
In the farthest corner of the garden be a tree quite cover with lovely white blossom .
Its branch be all golden , and silver fruit hang down from them , and underneath it stand the little boy he have love .
Downstairs run the Giant in great joy , and out into the garden .
He hasten across the grass , and come near to the child .
And when he come quite close his face grow red with anger , and he say , `` Who hath dare to wind thee ? ''
For on the palm of the child 's hand be the print of two nail , and the print of two nail be on the little foot .
`` Who hath dare to wind thee ? ''
cry the Giant ; `` tell me , that I may take my big sword and slay him . ''
`` Nay ! ''
answer the child ; `` but these be the wound of Love . ''
`` Who art thou ? ''
say the Giant , and a strange awe fell on him , and he kneel before the little child .
And the child smile on the Giant , and say to him , `` You let me play once in your garden , to-day you shall come with me to my garden , which be Paradise . ''
And when the child run in that afternoon , they find the Giant lie dead under the tree , all cover with white blossom .
THE DEVOTED FRIEND One morning the old Water-rat put his head out of his hole .
He have bright beady eye and stiff grey whisker and his tail be like a long bit of black india-rubber .
The little duck be swim about in the pond , look just like a lot of yellow canary , and their mother , who be pure white with real red leg , be try to teach them how to stand on their head in the water .
`` You will never be in the best society unless you can stand on your head , '' she keep say to them ; and every now and then she show them how it be do .
But the little duck pay no attention to her .
They be so young that they do not know what an advantage it be to be in society at all .
`` What disobedient child ! ''
cry the old Water-rat ; `` they really deserve to be drown . ''
`` Nothing of the kind , '' answer the Duck , `` every one must make a beginning , and parent can not be too patient . ''
`` Ah !
I know nothing about the feeling of parent , '' say the Water - rat ; `` I be not a family man .
In fact , I have never be marry , and I never intend to be .
Love be all very well in it way , but friendship be much high .
Indeed , I know of nothing in the world that be either nobler or rarer than a devoted friendship . ''
`` And what , pray , be your idea of the duty of a devote friend ? ''
ask a Green Linnet , who be sit in a willow-tree hard by , and have overhear the conversation .
`` Yes , that be just what I want to know , '' say the Duck ; and she swim away to the end of the pond , and stand upon her head , in order to give her child a good example .
`` What a silly question ! ''
cry the Water-rat .
`` I should expect my devoted friend to be devote to me , of course . ''
`` And what would you do in return ? ''
say the little bird , swing upon a silver spray , and flap his tiny wing .
`` I do n't understand you , '' answer the Water-rat .
`` Let me tell you a story on the subject , '' say the Linnet .
`` Is the story about me ? ''
ask the Water-rat .
`` If so , I will listen to it , for I be extremely fond of fiction . ''
`` It be applicable to you , '' answer the Linnet ; and he fly down , and alight upon the bank , he tell the story of The Devoted Friend .
`` Once upon a time , '' say the Linnet , `` there be an honest little fellow name Hans . ''
`` Was he very distinguish ? ''
ask the Water-rat .
`` No , '' answer the Linnet , `` I do n't think he be distinguish at all , except for his kind heart , and his funny round good-humoured face .
He live in a tiny cottage all by himself , and every day he work in his garden .
In all the country-side there be no garden so lovely a his .
Sweet-william grow there , and Gilly-flowers , and Shepherds ' - purse , and Fair-maids of France .
There be damask Roses , and yellow Roses , lilac Crocuses , and gold , purple Violets and white .
Columbine and Ladysmock , Marjoram and Wild Basil , the Cowslip and the Flower-de-luce , the Daffodil and the Clove-Pink bloom or blossom in their proper order a the month go by , one flower take another flower 's place , so that there be always beautiful thing to look at , and pleasant odour to smell .
`` Little Hans have a great many friend , but the most devoted friend of all be big Hugh the Miller .
Indeed , so devote be the rich Miller to little Hans , that be would never go by his garden without lean over the wall and pluck a large nosegay , or a handful of sweet herb , or fill his pocket with plum and cherry if it be the fruit season . ''
` Real friend should have everything in common , ' the Miller use to say , and little Hans nod and smile , and felt very proud of have a friend with such noble idea .
`` Sometimes , indeed , the neighbour think it strange that the rich Miller never give little Hans anything in return , though he have a hundred sack of flour store away in his mill , and six milch cow , and a large flock of woolly sheep ; but Hans never trouble his head about these thing , and nothing give him great pleasure than to listen to all the wonderful thing the Miller use to say about the unselfishness of true friendship .
`` So little Hans work away in his garden .
During the spring , the summer , and the autumn he be very happy , but when the winter come , and he have no fruit or flower to bring to the market , he suffer a good deal from cold and hunger , and often have to go to bed without any supper but a few dried pear or some hard nut .
In the winter , also , he be extremely lonely , a the Miller never come to see him then . ''
` There be no good in my go to see little Hans as long a the snow last , ' the Miller use to say to his wife , ` for when people be in trouble they should be leave alone , and not be bother by visitor .
That at least be my idea about friendship , and I be sure I be right .
So I shall wait till the spring come , and then I shall pay him a visit , and he will be able to give me a large basket of primrose and that will make him so happy . ' ''
` You be certainly very thoughtful about others , ' answer the Wife , a she sit in her comfortable armchair by the big pinewood fire ; ` very thoughtful indeed .
It be quite a treat to hear you talk about friendship .
I be sure the clergyman himself could not say such beautiful thing a you do , though he do live in a three-storied house , and wear a gold ring on his little finger . ' ''
` But could we not ask little Hans up here ? '
say the Miller 's young son .
` If poor Hans be in trouble I will give him half my porridge , and show him my white rabbit . ' ''
` What a silly boy you be ' !
cry the Miller ; ' I really do n't know what be the use of send you to school .
You seem not to learn anything .
Why , if little Hans come up here , and saw our warm fire , and our good supper , and our great cask of red wine , he might get envious , and envy be a most terrible thing , and would spoil anybody 's nature .
I certainly will not allow Hans ' nature to be spoil .
I be his best friend , and I will always watch over him , and see that he be not lead into any temptation .
Besides , if Hans come here , he might ask me to let him have some flour on credit , and that I could not do .
Flour be one thing , and friendship be another , and they should not be confuse .
Why , the word be spell differently , and mean quite different thing .
Everybody can see that . ' ''
` How well you talk ' !
say the Miller 's Wife , pour herself out a large glass of warm ale ; ` really I feel quite drowsy .
It be just like be in church . ' ''
` Lots of people act well , ' answer the Miller ; ` but very few people talk well , which show that talk be much the more difficult thing of the two , and much the finer thing also ' ; and he look sternly across the table at his little son , who felt so ashamed of himself that he hang his head down , and grow quite scarlet , and begin to cry into his tea .
However , he be so young that you must excuse him . ''
`` Is that the end of the story ? ''
ask the Water-rat .
`` Certainly not , '' answer the Linnet , `` that be the beginning . ''
`` Then you be quite behind the age , '' say the Water-rat .
`` Every good story-teller nowadays start with the end , and then go on to the beginning , and conclude with the middle .
That be the new method .
I hear all about it the other day from a critic who be walk round the pond with a young man .
He speak of the matter at great length , and I be sure he must have be right , for he have blue spectacle and a bald head , and whenever the young man make any remark , he always answer ` Pooh ! '
But pray go on with your story .
I like the Miller immensely .
I have all kind of beautiful sentiment myself , so there be a great sympathy between u . ''
`` Well , '' say the Linnet , hop now on one leg and now on the other , `` as soon a the winter be over , and the primrose begin to open their pale yellow star , the Miller say to his wife that he would go down and see little Hans . ''
` Why , what a good heart you have ' !
cry his Wife ; ` you be always think of others .
And mind you take the big basket with you for the flower . '
`` So the Miller tie the sail of the windmill together with a strong iron chain , and go down the hill with the basket on his arm . ''
` Good morning , little Hans , ' say the Miller . ''
` Good morning , ' say Hans , lean on his spade , and smile from ear to ear . ''
` And how have you be all the winter ? '
say the Miller . ''
` Well , really , ' cried Hans , ` it be very good of you to ask , very good indeed .
I be afraid I have rather a hard time of it , but now the spring have come , and I be quite happy , and all my flower be do well . ' ''
` We often talk of you during the winter , Hans , ' say the Miller , ` and wonder how you be get on . ' ''
` That be kind of you , ' say Hans ; ' I be half afraid you have forget me . ' ''
` Hans , I be surprise at you , ' say the Miller ; ` friendship never forgets .
That be the wonderful thing about it , but I be afraid you do n't understand the poetry of life .
How lovely your primrose be look , by-the-bye '' ! ''
` They be certainly very lovely , ' say Hans , ` and it be a most lucky thing for me that I have so many .
I be go to bring them into the market and sell them to the Burgomaster 's daughter , and buy back my wheelbarrow with the money . ' ''
` Buy back your wheelbarrow ?
You do n't mean to say you have sell it ?
What a very stupid thing to do ' ! ''
` Well , the fact be , ' say Hans , ` that I be oblige to .
You see the winter be a very bad time for me , and I really have no money at all to buy bread with .
So I first sell the silver button off my Sunday coat , and then I sell my silver chain , and then I sell my big pipe , and at last I sell my wheelbarrow .
But I be go to buy them all back again now . ' ''
` Hans , ' say the Miller , ' I will give you my wheelbarrow .
It be not in very good repair ; indeed , one side be go , and there be something wrong with the wheel-spokes ; but in spite of that I will give it to you .
I know it be very generous of me , and a great many people would think me extremely foolish for part with it , but I be not like the rest of the world .
I think that generosity be the essence of friendship , and , besides , I have get a new wheelbarrow for myself .
Yes , you may set your mind at ease , I will give you my wheelbarrow . ' ''
` Well , really , that be generous of you , ' say little Hans , and his funny round face glow all over with pleasure . '
I can easily put it in repair , a I have a plank of wood in the house . ' '' '
A plank of wood ' !
say the Miller ; ` why , that be just what I want for the roof of my barn .
There be a very large hole in it , and the corn will all get damp if I do n't stop it up .
How lucky you mention it !
It be quite remarkable how one good action always breed another .
I have give you my wheelbarrow , and now you be go to give me your plank .
Of course , the wheelbarrow be worth far more than the plank , but true , friendship never notice thing like that .
Pray get it at once , and I will set to work at my barn this very day . ' ''
` Certainly , ' cry little Hans , and he run into the shed and drag the plank out . ''
` It be not a very big plank , ' say the Miller , look at it , ` and I be afraid that after I have mend my barn-roof there wo n't be any left for you to mend the wheelbarrow with ; but , of course , that be not my fault .
And now , a I have give you my wheelbarrow , I be sure you would like to give me some flower in return .
Here be the basket , and mind you fill it quite full . ' ''
` Quite full ? '
say little Hans , rather sorrowfully , for it be really a very big basket , and he know that if he fill it he would have no flower leave for the market and he be very anxious to get his silver button back . ''
` Well , really , ' answer the Miller , ` a I have give you my wheelbarrow , I do n't think that it be much to ask you for a few flower .
I may be wrong , but I should have think that friendship , true friendship , be quite free from selfishness of any kind . ' ''
` My dear friend , my best friend , ' cry little Hans , ` you be welcome to all the flower in my garden .
I would much sooner have your good opinion than my silver button , any day ' ; and he run and pluck all his pretty primrose , and fill the Miller 's basket . ''
` Good-bye , little Hans , ' say the Miller , a he go up the hill with the plank on his shoulder , and the big basket in his hand . ''
` Good-bye , ' say little Hans , and he begin to dig away quite merrily , he be so pleased about the wheelbarrow .
`` The next day he be nail up some honeysuckle against the porch , when he hear the Miller 's voice call to him from the road .
So he jump off the ladder , and run down the garden , and look over the wall .
`` There be the Miller with a large sack of flour on his back . ''
` Dear little Hans , ' say the Miller , ` would you mind carry this sack of flour for me to market ? ' ''
` Oh , I be so sorry , ' say Hans , ` but I be really very busy to-day .
I have get all my creeper to nail up , and all my flower to water , and all my grass to roll . ' ''
` Well , really , ' say the Miller , ' I think that , consider that I be go to give you my wheelbarrow , it be rather unfriendly of you to refuse . ' ''
` Oh , do n't say that , ' cry little Hans , ' I would n't be unfriendly for the whole world ' ; and he run in for his cap , and trudge off with the big sack on his shoulder .
`` It be a very hot day , and the road be terribly dusty , and before Hans have reach the sixth milestone he be so tired that he have to sit down and rest .
However , he go on bravely , and a last he reach the market .
After he have wait there some time , he sell the sack of flour for a very good price , and then he return home at once , for he be afraid that if he stop too late he might meet some robber on the way . ''
` It have certainly be a hard day , ' say little Hans to himself a he be go to bed , ` but I be glad I do not refuse the Miller , for he be my best friend , and , besides , he be go to give me his wheelbarrow . '
`` Early the next morning the Miller come down to get the money for his sack of flour , but little Hans be so tired that he be still in bed . ''
` Upon my word , ' say the Miller , ` you be very lazy .
Really , consider that I be go to give you my wheelbarrow , I think you might work harder .
Idleness be a great sin , and I certainly do n't like any of my friend to be idle or sluggish .
You must not mind my speak quite plainly to you .
Of course I should not dream of do so if I be not your friend .
But what be the good of friendship if one can not say exactly what one mean ?
Anybody can say charming thing and try to please and to flatter , but a true friend always say unpleasant thing , and do not mind give pain .
Indeed , if he be a really true friend he prefer it , for he know that then he be do good . ' '' '
I be very sorry , ' say little Hans , rub his eye and pull off his night-cap , ` but I be so tired that I think I would lie in bed for a little time , and listen to the bird singing .
Do you know that I always work well after hear the bird sing ? ' ''
` Well , I be glad of that , ' say the Miller , clap little Hans on the back , ` for I want you to come up to the mill as soon a you be dress , and mend my barn-roof for me . '
`` Poor little Hans be very anxious to go and work in his garden , for his flower have not be water for two day , but he do not like to refuse the Miller , a he be such a good friend to him . ''
` Do you think it would be unfriendly of me if I say I be busy ? '
he inquire in a shy and timid voice . ''
` Well , really , ' answer the Miller , ' I do not think it be much to ask of you , consider that I be go to give you my wheelbarrow ; but of course if you refuse I will go and do it myself . ' ''
` Oh !
on no account , ' cry little Hans and he jump out of bed , and dress himself , and go up to the barn .
`` He work there all day long , till sunset , and at sunset the Miller come to see how he be get on . ''
` Have you mend the hole in the roof yet , little Hans ? '
cry the Miller in a cheery voice . ''
` It be quite mend , ' answer little Hans , come down the ladder . ''
` Ah ' !
say the Miller , ` there be no work so delightful a the work one do for others . ' ''
` It be certainly a great privilege to hear you talk , ' answer little Hans , sit down , and wipe his forehead , ' a very great privilege .
But I be afraid I shall never have such beautiful idea a you have . ' ''
` Oh !
they will come to you , ' say the Miller , ` but you must take more pain .
At present you have only the practice of friendship ; some day you will have the theory also . ' ''
` Do you really think I shall ? '
ask little Hans . '' '
I have no doubt of it , ' answer the Miller , ` but now that you have mend the roof , you have good go home and rest , for I want you to drive my sheep to the mountain to-morrow . '
`` Poor little Hans be afraid to say anything to this , and early the next morning the Miller bring his sheep round to the cottage , and Hans start off with them to the mountain .
It take him the whole day to get there and back ; and when he return he be so tired that he go off to sleep in his chair , and do not wake up till it be broad daylight . ''
` What a delightful time I shall have in my garden , ' he say , and he go to work at once .
`` But somehow he be never able to look after his flower at all , for his friend the Miller be always come round and send him off on long errand , or get him to help at the mill .
Little Hans be very much distress at time , a he be afraid his flower would think he have forget them , but he console himself by the reflection that the Miller be his best friend .
` Besides , ' he use to say , ` he be go to give me his wheelbarrow , and that be an act of pure generosity . '
`` So little Hans work away for the Miller , and the Miller say all kind of beautiful thing about friendship , which Hans take down in a note-book , and use to read over at night , for he be a very good scholar .
`` Now it happen that one even little Hans be sit by his fireside when a loud rap come at the door .
It be a very wild night , and the wind be blow and roar round the house so terribly that at first he think it be merely the storm .
But a second rap come , and then a third , louder than any of the others . ''
` It be some poor traveller , ' say little Hans to himself , and he run to the door .
`` There stand the Miller with a lantern in one hand and a big stick in the other . ''
` Dear little Hans , ' cry the Miller , ' I be in great trouble .
My little boy have fall off a ladder and hurt himself , and I be go for the Doctor .
But he live so far away , and it be such a bad night , that it have just occur to me that it would be much good if you go instead of me .
You know I be go to give you my wheelbarrow , and so , it be only fair that you should do something for me in return . ' ''
` Certainly , ' cry little Hans , ' I take it quite a a compliment your come to me , and I will start off at once .
But you must lend me your lantern , a the night be so dark that I be afraid I might fall into the ditch . ' '' '
I be very sorry , ' answer the Miller , ` but it be my new lantern , and it would be a great loss to me if anything happen to it . ' ''
` Well , never mind , I will do without it , ' cry little Hans , and he take down his great fur coat , and his warm scarlet cap , and tie a muffler round his throat , and start off .
`` What a dreadful storm it be !
The night be so black that little Hans could hardly see , and the wind be so strong that he could scarcely stand .
However , he be very courageous , and after he have be walk about three hour , he arrive at the Doctor 's house , and knock at the door . ''
` Who be there ? '
cry the Doctor , put his head out of his bedroom window . ''
` Little Hans , Doctor . ' ''
` What do you want , little Hans ? ' ''
` The Miller 's son have fall from a ladder , and have hurt himself , and the Miller want you to come at once . ' ''
` All right ! '
say the Doctor ; and he order his horse , and his big boot , and his lantern , and come downstairs , and ride off in the direction of the Miller 's house , little Hans trudge behind him .
`` But the storm grow bad and bad , and the rain fell in torrent , and little Hans could not see where he be go , or keep up with the horse .
At last he lose his way , and wander off on the moor , which be a very dangerous place , a it be full of deep hole , and there poor little Hans be drown .
His body be find the next day by some goatherd , float in a great pool of water , and be bring back by them to the cottage .
`` Everybody go to little Hans ' funeral , a he be so popular , and the Miller be the chief mourner . ''
` As I be his best friend , ' say the Miller , ` it be only fair that I should have the best place ' ; so he walk at the head of the procession in a long black cloak , and every now and then he wipe his eye with a big pocket-handkerchief . ''
` Little Hans be certainly a great loss to every one , ' say the Blacksmith , when the funeral be over , and they be all seat comfortably in the inn , drink spice wine and eat sweet cake . '' '
A great loss to me at any rate , ' answer the Miller ; ` why , I have as good a give him my wheelbarrow , and now I really do n't know what to do with it .
It be very much in my way at home , and it be in such bad repair that I could not get anything for it if I sell it .
I will certainly take care not to give away anything again .
One always suffer for be generous . ' ''
`` Well ? ''
say the Water-rat , after a long pause .
`` Well , that be the end , '' say the Linnet .
`` But what become of the Miller ? ''
ask the Water-rat .
`` Oh !
I really do n't know , '' reply the Linnet ; `` and I be sure that I do n't care . ''
`` It be quite evident then that you have no sympathy in your nature , '' say the Water-rat .
`` I be afraid you do n't quite see the moral of the story , '' remark the Linnet .
`` The what ? ''
scream the Water-rat .
`` The moral . ''
`` Do you mean to say that the story have a moral ? ''
`` Certainly , '' say the Linnet .
`` Well , really , '' say the Water-rat , in a very angry manner , `` I think you should have tell me that before you begin .
If you have do so , I certainly would not have listen to you ; in fact , I should have say ` Pooh , ' like the critic .
However , I can say it now '' ; so he shout out `` Pooh '' at the top of his voice , give a whisk with his tail , and go back into his hole .
`` And how do you like the Water-rat ? ''
ask the Duck , who come paddle up some minute afterwards .
`` He have a great many good point , but for my own part I have a mother 's feeling , and I can never look at a confirmed bachelor without the tear come into my eye . ''
`` I be rather afraid that I have annoy him , '' answer the Linnet .
`` The fact be , that I tell him a story with a moral . ''
`` Ah !
that be always a very dangerous thing to do , '' say the Duck .
And I quite agree with her .
THE REMARKABLE ROCKET The King 's son be go to be marry , so there be general rejoicing .
He have wait a whole year for his bride , and at last she have arrive .
She be a Russian Princess , and have drive all the way from Finland in a sledge drawn by six reindeer .
The sledge be shape like a great golden swan , and between the swan 's wing lay the little Princess herself .
Her long ermine-cloak reach right down to her foot , on her head be a tiny cap of silver tissue , and she be as pale a the Snow Palace in which she have always live .
So pale be she that a she drive through the street all the people wonder .
`` She be like a white rise ! ''
they cry , and they throw down flower on her from the balcony .
At the gate of the Castle the Prince be wait to receive her .
He have dreamy violet eye , and his hair be like fine gold .
When he saw her he sink upon one knee , and kiss her hand .
`` Your picture be beautiful , '' he murmur , `` but you be more beautiful than your picture '' ; and the little Princess blush .
`` She be like a white rise before , '' say a young Page to his neighbour , `` but she be like a red rise now '' ; and the whole Court be delight .
For the next three day everybody go about say , `` White rise , Red rise , Red rise , White rise '' ; and the King give order that the Page 's salary be to be double .
As he receive no salary at all this be not of much use to him , but it be consider a great honour , and be duly publish in the Court Gazette .
When the three day be over the marriage be celebrate .
It be a magnificent ceremony , and the bride and bridegroom walk hand in hand under a canopy of purple velvet embroider with little pearl .
Then there be a State Banquet , which last for five hour .
The Prince and Princess sit at the top of the Great Hall and drink out of a cup of clear crystal .
Only true lover could drink out of this cup , for if false lip touch it , it grow grey and dull and cloudy .
`` It 's quite clear that they love each other , '' say the little Page , `` as clear a crystal ! ''
and the King double his salary a second time .
`` What an honour ! ''
cry all the courtier .
After the banquet there be to be a Ball .
The bride and bridegroom be to dance the Rose-dance together , and the King have promise to play the flute .
He play very badly , but no one have ever dare to tell him so , because he be the King .
Indeed , he know only two air , and be never quite certain which one he be play ; but it make no matter , for , whatever he do , everybody cry out , `` Charming !
charm ! ''
The last item on the programme be a grand display of firework , to be let off exactly at midnight .
The little Princess have never see a firework in her life , so the King have give order that the Royal Pyrotechnist should be in attendance on the day of her marriage .
`` What be firework like ? ''
she have ask the Prince , one morning , a she be walk on the terrace .
`` They be like the Aurora Borealis , '' say the King , who always answer question that be address to other people , `` only much more natural .

''
And then , in the minister 's own hand , be add : `` Likewise for sprain , rub it in ; and for the cholic , a great spooneful in the hour . ''
To be sure , I laugh over this ; but it be rather tremulous laughter ; and I be glad to get my bundle on my staff 's end and set out over the ford and up the hill upon the farther side ; till , just a I come on the green drove-road run wide through the heather , I take my last look of Kirk Essendean , the tree about the manse , and the big rowan in the kirkyard where my father and my mother lay .
CHAPTER II I COME TO MY JOURNEY 'S END On the forenoon of the second day , come to the top of a hill , I saw all the country fall away before me down to the sea ; and in the midst of this descent , on a long ridge , the city of Edinburgh smoke like a kiln .
There be a flag upon the castle , and ship move or lie anchor in the firth ; both of which , for a far away a they be , I could distinguish clearly ; and both brought my country heart into my mouth .
Presently after , I come by a house where a shepherd live , and get a rough direction for the neighbourhood of Cramond ; and so , from one to another , work my way to the westward of the capital by Colinton , till I come out upon the Glasgow road .
And there , to my great pleasure and wonder , I behold a regiment marching to the fife , every foot in time ; an old red-faced general on a grey horse at the one end , and at the other the company of Grenadiers , with their Pope 's - hat .
The pride of life seem to mount into my brain at the sight of the red coat and the hearing of that merry music .
A little farther on , and I be tell I be in Cramond parish , and begin to substitute in my inquiry the name of the house of Shaws .
It be a word that seem to surprise those of whom I seek my way .
At first I think the plainness of my appearance , in my country habit , and that all dusty from the road , consort ill with the greatness of the place to which I be bind .
But after two , or maybe three , have give me the same look and the same answer , I begin to take it in my head there be something strange about the Shaws itself .
The good to set this fear at rest , I change the form of my inquiry ; and spy an honest fellow come along a lane on the shaft of his cart , I ask him if he have ever hear tell of a house they call the house of Shaws .
He stop his cart and look at me , like the others .
`` Ay '' say he .
`` What for ? ''
`` It 's a great house ? ''
I ask .
`` Doubtless , '' say he .
`` The house be a big , muckle house . ''
`` Ay , '' say I , `` but the folk that be in it ? ''
`` Folk ? ''
cry he .
`` Are ye daft ?
There 's nae folk there -- to call folk . ''
`` What ? ''
say I ; `` not Mr. Ebenezer ? ''
`` Ou , ay '' say the man ; `` there 's the laird , to be sure , if it 's him you 're want .
What 'll like be your business , mannie ? ''
`` I be lead to think that I would get a situation , '' I say , look as modest a I could .
`` What ? ''
cry the carter , in so sharp a note that his very horse start ; and then , `` Well , mannie , '' he add , `` it 's nane of my affair ; but ye seem a decent-spoken lad ; and if ye 'll take a word from me , ye 'll keep clear of the Shaws . ''
The next person I come across be a dapper little man in a beautiful white wig , whom I saw to be a barber on his round ; and know well that barber be great gossip , I ask him plainly what sort of a man be Mr. Balfour of the Shaws .
`` Hoot , hoot , hoot , '' say the barber , `` nae kind of a man , nae kind of a man at all ; '' and begin to ask me very shrewdly what my business be ; but I be more than a match for him at that , and he go on to his next customer no wise than he come .
I can not well describe the blow this dealt to my illusion .
The more indistinct the accusation be , the less I like them , for they leave the wider field to fancy .
What kind of a great house be this , that all the parish should start and stare to be ask the way to it ?
